THE 11ED PAGE.
A Melba Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ 'VT'OU will have to be at Co vent Garden
| half-past six if you want to get in—it
a Melba night! ”
Patti makes her appearance at long intervals a
the vast Albert Hall, and delights an adoring aud
Once with that wonderful voiceof hers. Wonderfi
is the word, for watching the tiny figure on th
little platform, down at the centre of that grea
crater of humanity, one’s first sensation is of won
der. How does that little, youthful, sprightl
figure, with the airs and graces of a gir
of seventeen support her age with so marvellou
an art, and produce her splendid surviva
of a voice with such exquisite perfection ? Bu
not all Patti’s art, and the keen pleasure tha
velvet voice thrills you with, can keep from th
background of your consciousness something lik
a panic. There is such excessive care in her sing
ing that one feels that she is at her highest pitch
one effort more and she will break down. As she
trips on to the platform, a little old-young figure
robed in diamonds, and smiles her quaint, pleasoc
smile at the human whirlpool of which she is the
centre, you think, “ This time she will break ;she
cannot reach that note ! ” But no; she sing
again and again ; the years pass on, and Patti i
as perfect as ever. But it is art, not voice.
At half-past 6 I was at the Covent Garden
Theatre, ft is one of the few big theatres ii
London ; the rest are drawing-rooms. In the lan
leading to the amphitheatre (2s. (>d.) —l had no
taken one of the eight-guinea boxes that night—
was the usual queue of waiting people, two anc
two, marshalled the length of the pavement b’
two policemen. I moved to the end of the “tail.’
In a few minutes, however, I had become a join
in the serpent, much nearer the head, and every
minute new arrivals lengthened the queue. Ther
was no crushing. English people are patient, anc
do not push.
At lust, there was a surge forward as the human
serpent began to writhe up the winding stairs. I
took the reptile 30 minutes of spasmodic progres
sion to get to the box-office. When my turn
came, I was told “ Standing-room only.” It was
a Melba night.
I found a rail to hang over, ,'t tho extiwme top
corner of the big building. Below me were tiers
and tiers of boxes, slowly tilling with bediamondec
women and spectacularly uninteresting men—one
man to every three wotnen. By leaning danger-
ously over, I caught a glimpse of the distant stage.
It was like looking for the bottom of a well.
Behind me was the crowd, wedged into the pas-
sages. Half of them were quite unable to see
the stage at all. But they did not go away.
Several sat calmly on the stairs, out of sight of
everything, and listened. They had come to hear
Melba. The opera was “Faust.” Plan 9011 was
Mephistopheles—without the impressive stage ap-
pearance of Lempriere Pringle, and with a ges-
ticulation somewhat too Gallic to convince, yet
with a marvellous voice. Saleza was Faust, his
tenor voice in the concerted music ringing true
and clarion-clear.
. But the Marguerite of Melba? touch a homely
little body; such a pleasing, piquant face ! A
figure verging a trifle towards the matronly, yet
so only to the supercritical. A powerful singer’s
neck—short and ungraceful. But the face was so
pleasing, the expression altogether so charming !
And the voice! One imagines a great organ, a
soprano Clara Butt, vast sonorous notes that
flood the big auditorium and compel the hearer’s
instant submission. But it is not like that.
Melba’s voice is small and light; yet every pulsa-
tion of it soars through the great building. Her
whispers float to the ceiling, wing to the utter-
corners of the crowd. And it is of such an inti-
mate, soft, caressing quality ! Sweet as honey,
charged with femininity, it is the embodied soul of
womanhood. And it comes from her little figure
with almost a contemptuous ease. In the jewel-
song she was happiness articulate. There was no
effort, no care for effect: she sang because she
must. It was more than Art.
Of the splendid stage-effects, the crowd that
streamed in for the choruses, the perfection of the
vocalists and orchestra, I may not speak ; they
served but as a bodyguard for this homely little
woman with the greatest voice in the world,
moving quietly through the tragedy, flinging out
her soul in song.
London.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
A New Religion,
THOSE people who made the mistake of re-
garding Elijah 11. Dowie, the man with
the Purple Wings, as too absurd a fraud
as ever to be taken seriously, and now hear
wonderingly of Zion city, should hasten to give a
cordial preliminary welcome to the latest new
religion. This hails from the Argentine .Republic
(which evidently envies Australia its Dowie). Its
platform is set down in the following “open
letter,” circulated in MS. (the new religion evi-
dently doesn’t run- to a printing press yet):
Open Letter to General William Booth, Salv. Army,
101 Queen Victoria-st., 101, London, E.O.
Concordia, E.R., Argentine, June 24, 1902.
Brother in Christ!
I hereby, inform you that to-day I have resigned my
commission as ensign in the Salvation Army, after nine
years of work in your forces.
I call on Brigadier Maidment and all my comrades to
witness that I go out clean.
The Lord Jesus calls me forth to follow Him as an
outcast and a tramp, without'acent. in my pocket, with no
other home than the King’s highway and the Father’s
heart. My rest for each night shall be the poor man’s
hut, or the open field, as they were my Master’s.
But I go forth in the name of the Lord, and in His
strength.
Christ the Lord has sent me,
Through the midnight lands
Mine the mighty ordination
Of the pierced hands.
Moreover, the Lord calls me forth to raise up unto Him
an army of 144,000 tramp apostles, like unto myself, will-
ing to follow the Lamb whithersoever He goeth, that
would as soon think of burdening- their warfare with
home, wife and children as you would of getting drunk,
Men of God, taking their stand with the Lamb on Mount
Zion, and in whose mouth no guile is found.
Our “ Rules and Regulations ” you will find in the 9th
chapter of Matthew ; our Sacraments—the cold and heat,
the hunger and thirst, the weariness of the homeless
train]), and the lowliness of Him who had not where to
lav His head ; our uniform—the tramp’s “coat of many
colours.”
And now listen, O General of the Salvation Army :
I “ft “P mv hand to Him that liveth for ever, and swear
that I shall fight it out to a finish, on these lines.
And when you see me and my sons and brethren, the
apostles whom the Lord will raise up unto Himself, by
His Spirit, turn into “cosil.v-furnished quarters” and
snug- barracks,” you may then write on our work what
you ought to write on your headquarters and vour hat-
bands : namety, (t Ichabod.”
ci.* « a ll-° r ,',- vou pother in Christ, your Commissioners,
j .> tield and bile, and all other Christians : Come out
and follow me, even as I follow the Master, on the wallaby
track, according to the royal law of the apost.e, forth to
the conquest of the world for Christ.
. fche five continents I proclaim : The time is
tulhded, the Kingdom -s come, and the Lord is at hand.
Vours in Christ, the Apostle of the Lord,
Paul Beffen.
The Tramp Apostles ” ! That sounds good for
a start. But Paul seems to have forgotten the
collection. Christianity without a collection went
out of fashion more than 1800 years ago. Paul
must look to that point.
The Mugs and the M. ammonites.
A CAREER of engaging' impecuniosity,”
says 1 mefo, “ usually begins with a
brief and disastrous experience on the
btoek Exchange or the Turf.” It is a
fact that the victims of the bookmaker and the
company-promoter are very often of the “ en-
gaging ” type—young men of “blood” who have
no brains for any profession, and so reckon they’d
best pick up a fortune backing winners or buying
Stocks for a rise ; the struggling clerk, or doctor,
or lawyer, or clergyman (sometimes), whose ambi-
tions are greater than his talents, and who sees in
speculation a safe high-road to fortune* Its
largely the best fellows in the world ” who are
ruined on the Turf or on ’Change, the men
who haven t an atom of cunning, and only
that clangorous modicum of brains which gives
a man that degree of self-esteem, which is pardoned
as manly, and a hazy idea that the capacity to
make 100 runs at cricket, or sing a godd song, or
score 38 in a break on a billiard table must tell in*
the game of money-making. These are the
. ouigs the class on whom the men of
Mammon fatten. The other great class of
victims is made up of those who go on
the highway to rob and are robbed. It’s
some consolation to a world, which sees, in
that vitally important part of life, money-making,
the great natural law of the survival of the fittest
seemingly so rudely flouted, that the sharks do
not spare one another, that the “ bookie’s” ulti-
mate fate is generally to be broken by other
bookies, and that Bear often succumbs to Bull
and Bull to Bear.
It is not possible for “mugs” to learn from the
experience of others. No man can ever be taught,
except m the school of misfortune, that that lack
of qualities which makes him an unsuccessful
doctor, or lawyer, or tradesman will, with almost
mathematical certainty, make him an unsuccessful
speculator : that, even by dabbling in speculation,
what possibility there is of gain has to be seriously
discounted by the certainty of loss through the
diversion of energy from his real business in life.
(There is nothing so enervating as gambling. It
saps at the roots of a man’s vital force more surely
than does drug-taking.) But the man with the
bent that way can’t be kept from gambling by the
sight of the skulls on the path : he must go his
way to learn his lesson from personal experience.
If it were possible to warn off the foolish, “ The
Critic Black Book.” published in England by Mr
Henry Hess, would surely have that effect. Mr
Hess edits the newspaper called the Critic, and he
is possibly not exactly a financial Sir Galahad
Perhaps, however, that gives him a better chance
of usefulness as a financial critic. Mr. Hess
“ Black Book ” is an exposure of “ ramps ” on the
Stock Exchange of London, and for 1901-2 he has
certain appendices, to bring it up to date, as far as
possible, with the transactions of the year. A
valuable section of the book gives lists of company
promoters, with their promotions and their fate.
Mr. Whitaker Wright, for instance, between 1894
and 1900 promoted 40 companies, of which the
fate of almost all was to be reconstructed or liqui-
dated.
But the most characteristic part of the Black
Book is its “histories of companies.” To take a
sample—an Australian mining company whose
name it is not necessary to quote here. This
company was founded nearly half a century ago,
and had a blameless if impecunious career for
many years, until, in 1889, it became an instru-
ment in the hands of the editor of a leading
.London financial paper and was “ reconstructed,”
the editor and his friends holding most of the
shares. The editor began to boom the co. in his
paper, in articles, in “answers to correspondents,”
and so on. Almost every issue there was
advice, to more or less genuine inquirers, to buy
the shares, which Mr. Editor industriously un-
loaded. He and his friends had 155,085 shares
originally. By 1889 they had got their holding,
down to 2720. Prices for the shares had fluctuated
from Is. to 2s. 3d. Later, when the editor had stood
from under and boomed the show no more, stock
dropped down to 3d., and subsequently the co.
was “reconstructed.”
And that sort of thing is simply part of the
business of mining speculation. It is a poor sort
of promoter who doesn’t own or influence one or
two “leading financial papers ” to boom anything
that he has to unload. And these “leading
financial papers” are the guides to which the
“ mugs ” turn when they set out to make fortunes
by speculation !

Mr. H. B. Higgins, M.P. for North Melbourne,
has published as a pamphlet (through the Advo-
cate newspaper, Melbourne) his recent address,
“The Duties of Patriotism.” It is not too much
to say that the publication should be in the hands
of every political student in Australia, for we
know no more able and succinct statement of the
case against unreasoning Jingoism. Discussing
first the dictum, “My country right or wrong,”
Mr. Higgins, following closely on the lines of
argument of Mr. Herbert Spencer, shows its in-
herent immorality. He refers to the recklessness
with which wars are made:—
It has struck me as curious to see what precautions we
take in the law courts in trying prisoners before we punish
them—what strict evidence we require of their guilt—-
and yet how slender is the evidence which one nation
demands before it seeks to punish with death, of with
subjugation, the people of another nation. There is
ictually more care taken before punishing- a resident of
Bilking-square for larceny, than in making up our minds,
from a perusal of the newspapers, that our Government
ought to make war. Someone, we do not know who,
prints something in a newspaper— false news, perhaps, like
the news of the massacre at Pekin, or like the news of the
secret will of Peter the Great. This is probably extracted,
with embellishments, from some other unknown source ;
and we find two nations presently engaged in a deadly strug-
gle. When Bismarck wanted to work up the Germans to war
with the French, and the French to war with the Ger-
mans, he deliberately got falsehoods, false telegrams, etc.,
inserted in the newspapers, and the tide of feeling on
both sides soon became irrepressible. This difficulty,
arising from a public credulous, and a press courting sen-
sation, popularity, circulation, money, is one of the
greatest difficulties of modern politics—one of the greatest
hindrances to peace and to social development.
Then follow various historical examples of wars,
now generally acknowledged as wicked and
needless, in which England has engaged—the war
against the Dutcli (Charles IT.), the war
against the French (William II.), against
the French (George II.), against the American
colonies (George III.), against the young
French Republic (George III.), the Opium
war against China (Victoria), the Crimean war
against Russia (Victoria), the Zulu war and the
Afghan war in the same reign. Mr. Higgins
points out that all those wars had their opponents
in England when waged ; and that from Chatham
down, down, down to Chamberlain, almost every
English public man of note has opposed some war
or other. It is only the modern hoodlum press
which insists that it is the duty of patriotism to
support every war which it howls for.
Edmund Gosse on Dumas (a different opinion,
it will be noted, to that of a recent Red Page
contributor): —
The French discover a certain puerility in our English
attitude to the elder Dumas. ... To glance at the
French criticism of to-day is to be obliged to recognise
that Dumas does not exist any longer fpr serious readers
in France, except as the author of such interesting plays
as “ Henri 111. et sa Oour” and “Antony.” . . . The
popularity of Dumas lias entirely ceased among edu-
cated people in France, and he takes his place
with Eugene Sue and Paul de Kock as a kitchen-
classic, as one of the purveyors of mechanical
entertainment for uneducated minds, without moral,
intellectual, or philosophical aim whatsoever. .. .
There can he little doubt that native criticism has gone
too far in reaction against the immense popularity of
Dumas. But when one roads the crude and childish
laudation of him in English newspapers and. magazines,
one is tempted to endorse the worst that the French can
say. It is a negation of all the principles of literary taste
to prefer this exuberant iinprovisatore, this author of
interminable visions without dignity and without reality,
to the noble figures which surround him on the stage of
his own time, and tower more and inpre steadily above him.
. . . It is astonishing that this rampageous elderly school-
boy can he taken seriously by grown-up people. . . .
This, perhaps, is not a very gracious way of talking about
an amiable colored gentleman on the hundredth anniver-
sary of his birth. But it is the fault of his English
admirers, who thrust him down our throats as if iu him
alone resided all the law and all the prophets. It is a
chapter of Mr. Lang’s “Essays in Little” which has
soured my temper. The exaggeration and the exclusive-
ness of the critic are past belief. In the course of this
essay I learn that the writings of Dumas exist “ for the
sorrow of prigs.” It is vexing to be called a prig, simply
because one does not join in a ohorus of tempestuous
eulogy round a sportive old quadroon, who chattered
gaily away in 1200 volumes of irresponsible romance. . . .
Where we see Dumas best of all—if one who does not
pretend to have read a quarter of his immense pro-
duction may venture to say so—is in the trilogy which
tells the ever-fresh, the ever-amusing adventures of those
Three Musketeers, who were Four. This, surely, is
Dumas’ greatest achievement in romance, the one perfect
flower of his dream of the legendary elegance and ardour
of French aristocracy. . . . But in all other matters the
talent of Dumas is quite of a secondary order. Mr. Lang
admires his pathos, and attributes to him “ great tears
like those of Pantagruel.” They are like those of Panta-
gruel, hut what a confession of rodomontade it is to
boast of the size of tears, as though they were marrow-
fats or marbles! All is on a Rabelaisian scale in the
career of Dumas ; he is Hercules and Goliath and Sar-
danapalus. But the finest art is not produced bv strong
men in country fairs, who wipe away with the' back of a
hairy hand,as M. Parigot admiringly puts it, the sweat that
beads the enormous forehead. Let it be sufficient praise
for Dumas that if he only wrote books for boys, still these
are the best boys’ books that ever were written. He may
still be praised, without reserve, by critics of from 13 to
17 summers. But of the influence" of this amiable gian t
on literature what is to be said ? Merely this : that it has
been detestable and fatal. The merits of Dumas—his
national sentiment, his gaiety, his exuberance—have
been neither imitated or perceived, but his example has
flooded our literature with facile and ridiculous “his-
torical ” romances where all is pinchbeck, all false and
puerile and silly, all devoted to a mere schoolboy cele-
bration of muscular strength and money. That is what
we have to thank Dumas for—the infiuitely contemptible
thing known as the minor English novel of adventure.

Edward Sugden, Master of “Queens,” ap-
pointed to the temporary position of Lecturer
in English at Melbourne University, rendered
vacant by the death of the late Alexander Suther-
land, has a wide and'-Jiberal knowledge of letters
classical and modem. He is a book-lover, a
musician, a humorist, and a good fellow withal’
Mr. M'Cubbin is showing in Melbourne a
portrait of Mr. Sam M'Caughey, M.L.C. The
painter had introduced an ornamental background
with sheep and cattle, but the squatter said that
he hated cattle, and that M‘Cubbiii had painted
in the wrong kind of sheep. He liked prize stock
best. So M’Caughey now stands forth in all the
splendor of Court dress, and the other sheep are
absent.
In a speech delivered at a Melb. Liedertafel smoke
night, Marshall-Hall contended that only persons
who had shown promise of accomplishing original
work, and not persons with good voices and no
musical intuition, should be sent to Europe to
hear the best that could be heard in music. Rut
is it essential that a local,musician should be made
in Germany? Is it not possible that an Austra-
lian, who never leaves Australia, can become a
great musician and write the true symphony of
the bush ? We have a little Australian art, a
little Australian letters, but no Australian music.
Can not our future Australian musician be a
natural outgrowth of the soil ?
JS
“ The men of science crowed over discovery as
though it were the same thing as understanding.
The quality of towns did not matter; the great
tiling was that you could get from one to another
10 times as quickly as your ancestors. You might
have nothing better in your head than the latest
price of corn, but being able to communicate this
fact to a man thousands of miles away in a few
minutes, you were a finer example of civilisation
than Plato.”— G. S. Street.
Some Books .
OF a big batch of novels, etc., by the last
two mails, hardly a one is worth reading,
except as a matter of “filling in time ” on
a railway journey.
John Long & Co. send :
“Woman, the Sphinx,” by Fergus Hume, a
highly-sensational record of the doings of a female
Jekyll and Hyde, who teaches Sunday-school in
one half of her life, and is a Paris courtesan in the
other
“The Investigators,” by J. S. Fletcher—per-
haps the best of the batch; a smart, well-told
yarn of the scientific type, which has some vogue
now.
“ Csesar’s Wife ” —a story of love-intrigue, with
the scenery of the Oberammagau Passion Play.
Not badly told, and of some woman-interest.
“The Diamond of Evil,” by Fred. Wishaw —an
adventure-yarn of fair interest.
“ The Court of Destiny,” by C. Gf. Chatterton
—a love-story, wholesome in tone, but not par-
ticularly strong.
“ Something in the City,” by Florence Warden
—a very “ bluggy ” tale of murder and burglary.
All these reach us through George Robertson
and . Co. We have also received—
“ Sewage Works Analyses,” by Gilbert S.
Fowler (P. S. King and Co., London). The author
is superintendent of the Manchester Corporation
Sewage Works, and his work should be of great
value to drainage engineers. It deals especially
with the septic tank system.
“Saint Antony of Padua” (Duckworth and Co.),
a translation of Albert Lepitre’s work; not a
critical life, but a eulogy of the saint.
*
TO CORRESPONDENTS.
Ignoramus (who confesses to an intellect “ not beyond
the average,” and wishes some books recommended,
“fiction or light study preferred”): Try, in fiction,
“Vanity Fair,” “ Lorna Doone,” “Kings in Exile,”
“Hereward the Wake,” “The Scarlet Letter,” “ Les
Miserables,” “Pickwick Papers,” “Evan Harrington,”
“ His Natural Life ” ; in poetry, “ The Ancient Mariner ”
(Coleridge), “ Endymion ” (Keats); for study (more or
less light), “ Sesame and Lilies ” and Bosweli’s Johnson.
This doesn’t pretend to be anything but a hurried pre-
scription.
G.B. writes:— Apropos the mistakes of pressmen as to
their authorship of paragraphs, some years ago, when a
regular contributor to The Bulletin, I continually laid
claim to pars, also claimed by another well-known press
writer— “ Boondi.” Possibly we both erred at different
times. Such confusion arises through men of somewhat
similar tastes selecting the same subjects for treatment,
and treating them in a very similar fashion. Should such
a par. appear somewhat different to the one sent in by
such a one, he often attributes the difference to editorial
touching up. There is also a tendency amongst all men,
whether merely readers or also writers, to regard in time
the thoughts they have merely, read as original with
themselves. Hence the unconscious plagiarist.
“Kiwi” writes : In reply to “QuilpN.” (B. 2/8/’f)2) —
who, by the way, knows something of his subject—allow
me to argue with deference that there is provision for
registration of copyright of literary works in Maoriland.
Granted that there is no local statutory provision, we
still have two undisputable facts—the Copyright Act 1886
(Imperial) is in force here, and we have registration of
copyright in works of art (including dramatic works)
under our two statutes of 1877-79. From these faots, I
argue that the application of the Copyright Act is not
nullified by the accidental omission of a local statutory
provision for registration, and that the Patents Registry
office inherits the jurisdiction of the corresponding
Imperial office. This argument can be supported strongly
by analogy. In a contested will-case in this State many
years ago,, one of the parties claimed that certain words in
the will conferred only an estate tail instead of a fee simple,
asallegedby theotherside. It wasadmittedthatthewords
used Were such as would confer an estate tail in England ;
but as the law of England required a disentailing deed to
be enrolled in the Court of Common Pleas, if the tenant-
m-tail-i repossession wished to convert the‘tail estate into
a fee ; and as there was no Court of Common Pleas in
Maoriland, there could be no enrolment, and hence no
estate tail. It was decided, however, that the Maoriland
Supreme Court had inherited the jurisdiction of the
English Court of Chancery, and that enrolment of the
disentailing deed could be made in the Supreme Court
accordingly. Thus there could be an estate tail in
Maoriland by virtue of the application here of the Fines
and Recoveries Act, 1833, although there had been no
statutory provision for enrolment, which is practically a
convertible term for registration. Analogy, therefore, is
strongly in favor of my contention, and, while I did miss
the point in “JJuilp N.’s” first contribution as to the
absence of provision for registration, I think he will see
that it is, at least, very arguable whether registration
cannot be effected by. means of the Imperial Statute.

A Doctor’s Present.
A Thrilling 1 Story of Suffering.
A Silver Lining to the Darkest Cloud.
For a long time they tried to cheer ma
by uttering kind and encouraging words,
but they did not do me any practical good
with their medicines, so then I submitted
myself for treatment at several hospitals.
Ihe physic prescribed at those institutions
took no effect, so I sank down to the lowest
depths of despair, because I thought my
fate was sealed. Later on I thought X
would make a final effort, and I sent for one
of the doctors who had been attending me
before, but he said that he could not do
any more for me than what he had
previously done, excepting to advise me to
take a course of Clements Tonic. He said
it was an excellent medicine, and soon after-
wards he good-naturedly made me a present
of two bottles.”
“ How did you feel then, Mrs. Josephs ?”
“ I felt as if my whole life depended
upon Clements Tonic, and in doing so I am
sure I did not exaggerate the seriousness
of the situation, as every other treatment
had failed. When those two bottles of
Clements Tonic were finished there was
more life in me than there had been for
twelve months. I could then take a little
food and enjoy it. Before taking Clements
Tonic everything I tasted was as if I had
put a penny in my mouth, which left a
coppery flavor behind ; but that unpleasant-
ness was soon remedied, and, besides, the
coating over my tongue was cleared away.
Constipation had troubled me, but Clements
Tonic soon remedied that, and all the time
I was getting more light-hearted than ever,
because my pains were decreasing. V These
iim rovements gave me a renewal of hope,
and, as was prudent, I clung tenaciously to
Clements Tonic. The next thing to cheer
my drooping spirits was to observe the
swellings in my head and limbs were being
reduced, and the pains were much less
severe than they had been. It was a very
unusual thing for me to wake up in the
night, but if I did I soon went to sleep
again, and rested peacefully till morning;
and another great benefit that I derived
from the use of Clements Tonic was that
my nerves were toned to their normal con-
dition. Those of my friends who had daily
expected to hear of my death could haidly
realise the fact that I was being carried
further away from the grave every day by
Clements Tonic, and although four years
have passed since then, yet we often have a
chat about the uarrow escape 1 had, and
Clements Tonic is the household remedy of
us all. .1 was trying to tell you how 1
progressed. Well, in a week or two more I
was able to eat solid food without the least
compunction, as my digestive organs were
as healthy as they could be, for there was
no flatulence or chest pains to inconvenience
me, and there was not an ache in any part
of my system. When my strength and
health were completely restored I devoutly
thanked God for my happy release, and as
there is no doubt that Clements Tonic saved
my life you are at liberty to publi.% my
story in any way that the public may taar
of it.”

and also contains my full permission to publish in

hundred and two, before me,
EDWARD W. BUTLER, J.P.
The Case of Mrs. A. JOSEPHS.
(BY a SYDNEY REPORTER.)
There is no suburb that has changed its
status as completely during the past twenty
years as that of Woolloomooloo. Facing
the bay bearing that name, with its
picturesque walks along the foreshores, and
with Hyde Park, the Botanical Gardens,
and the extensive Domain recreation
grounds in close proximity, Woolloomooloo
is one of the most attractive localities
around Sydney, and in it, at No. 18 Wood’s
Lane, lives Mrs. Annie Josephs, whose in-
terview with our reporter is reproduced
below.
“ I have lived at this address,” said Mrs.
Josephs, “for the past eight years, and
when I had been here about half that time
I thought I was going to die. It was after
a confinement that I suffered from blood
poisoning, besides which I lost a great
quantity of blood, and became so weak
that I could not put a foot to the ground
for nearly twelve months. For almost a
year I was in bed, being nothing but a
helpless and hopeless invalid, for I must
tell you that all the treatment I had
received did not lead me to believe
that I would ever get better. There
seemed nothing for it but to lie and
wait .patiently for death to relieve me of
my sufferings, as I kept on wasting and
wasting away, and I was almost paralysed
with agony.”
“Where were the pains principally
located ? ” enquired the pressman.
“ They were in every part of my body,
and my head swelled till it was about twice
its natural size, besides aching so intensely
that I scarcely knew what was going on
around me. Sometimes I became quite
stupefied with pain, and as I never could
sloep at night it is not to be wondered at
that I got so worn out that I used to lie
like one in a trance. All my limbs were
swollen and terrible pains afflicted them as
well, and when the severity of my aches
became so intolerable that a change in my
position was necessary, I had to have a
hoop bound round with wadding put under
my legs, because I was too weak to keep
them bent for a second without having
something underneath to support them.
There was no doubt that the longer I lived
the worse I got, for my nervous system was
frightfully strained, as 1 could not keep my-
self from shaking, and my mind was always
in a state of fear and anxiety. By some
means that 1 cannot explain, because I was
not enlightened on the subject myself, my
vision became very dim, and I nearly lost
the sight of my right eye. As an operation
was indispensable, I went under it, and
although my sight was saved, yet the strain
on my feeble constitution was almost too
great for me to bear. I got more nervous
than eve.r, and could not be left alone in the
house. The shaking of a window or the
creaking of a door sent a thrill of terror
right through me, and the next sensation
that I usually experienced was giddiness,
as though things were moving about in the
room. And you would hardly credit how I
felt if I ate any food, although I should say
‘drank’ instead of ‘ate, 5 because I was
only allowed the lightest of diet, and it
was invariably liquid. All the same, my
stomach could not digest it, and the way I
used to swell out with wind was something
awful, my chest also being troublesome
with a feeling as if a weight was inside me.
My skin was as yellow as a guinea, owing
to my liver being diseased, the doctors said,
and my face was so thin and disfigured that
many of my friends did not know me.
Having previously been very stout, they
could not realise it was me when they saw
nothing but a shadow before them, and the
ominous shakes of their heads made me feel
very downhearted. It was evident they
thought I was dying, and some of them
have since told me they felt sure I could not
live.”
“ You mentioned something about doctors
just now. What did they say about your
prospects ? ”

“OH OUR SELECTION.”
BY
“Steele Ruddr
Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Monsoonal.
“Monsoon oh !” the South was crying,
“ Lord God Monsoon of the North,
We are suffering, fainting, dying,
Charge thy tempests, roll them forth !
Lord God Monsoon straightway crowded
Fleece on fleece, and fold on fold,
Half earth’s firmanent enshrouded,
Blew God’s breath, and southward rolled.
Onward, Onward, to the Southland,
Ocean-freighted cloud-cars stream,
To the death-land, to the drouth-land
Where white bones, drought’s fruitage, gleam.
Diamantina neard his thunder ;
Barcoo saw his wet front shine
Warrego awoke in wonder ;
Swelled thy founts, old Cond amine.
Bree, Bokhara, Culgoa, Narran,
Took the Balonne’s flood again ;
Half-a-thousand miles of Bar won
Broke in splendor on the plain.
Western falls were drenched with blessing ;
Gwydir streams came glimmering down;
Castlereagh awoke caressing
Each drought-stricken desert town.
New births clothed earth’s haggard bosom,
Bared on territorial zones ;
Blade and leaf and bud and blossom
Hurried o’er the wide-strewn bones.
“Oh, Monsoon ! ” the people shouted ;
“ Brave Monsoon ! roll southward still ” ;
While the roaring cloud-rack spouted
Living floods on plain and hill.
But, beneath the healing smother,
Quickening half a continent,
Sadly sighed the Storm King’s brother,
Deep in caverned darkness pent.
He, the Underlord, invested
With a surer, greater boon
Than bore down that brave storm-crested,
Bounteous-breasted, bold Monsoon.
Neath stupendous zones, awaiting
Human effort, human touch ;
All inspiring, all creating,
Succouring all to upward rush.
’Twixt the anvil and the hammer,
Earth of iron and sky of brass,
Shall we ever cease to clamor
That which seldom comes to p?
Strike the firmament beneath us
With the true divining-rod ?
Face the task the Fates bequeath us,
Loose our own imprisoned God ?
F. M.
An Explorer.
He lies outstretched, as does a swimmer,
Who, after days of sweat and simmer,
Has found a flood;
Was he, at last, befooled ?
Did he think a river cooled
His fever’d blocd ?
Not that to him it mattered much,
He was not drown’d ;
That here, instead, his fingers clutch
The ground.
Beneath his brows are darksome caverns,
' Which serve the flies for wayside taverns,
With portals wide ;
Crowds stream through where once shone doors,
Ants and worms on all the floors,
Carouse inside;
He stirs not when the stealthy snake
Uprears its head,
Nor does the scuttling lizard wake
The dead.
His name, in hieroglyphics written
By blood and sand, the Sun has smitten
And made a blot;
Though his bones lie coffinless,
Bleaching in the wilderness,
He troubles not;
The starry sky, the desert rough,
The spaces dim
Are tomb and temple grand enough
For him.
The night winds chant for him their dirges,
Their organ-music swells and surges
Through nave and aisle,
Where the Days in surplice sit,
And the Nights, with tapers lit,
Before him file;
The arches of the firmament
His church-roof span—
He lies, his own white monument—
A man !
There is no Land of Never-Never,
In which is lost his soul’s endeavor,
It lives again
In the man whose pulses dance
Proudly to the proud “ Advance ! ”
As o’er Death’s plain
They bear the flag to Victory,
Whose fields and bars
Link with the Cross eternally
The Stars!
ENGLAND.
HENRY WOOLLEY.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralin and Dlaoriland .
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
€l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of 1 \d. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.

postage — lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of Tub Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.

expressly reserved by the publishers.

acknowledge in the “ Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceed-

clippings. All communications will be regarded as
strictly confidential.

address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 13, 1902.
The Ingratitude of England.
Mr. Edmund Barton, speaking in Glasgow recently, said
it was an “appalling fact” "to him that Australia had
only two-thirds of the population of London. He added
that the island continent had plenty of room for the
surplus population, and that it offered abounding oppor-
tunity, “ not to paupers,” but to those with a little money
and plenty of independence.
Mr. Edmund Barton and his fellow citizens can get rid
of the “appalling fact” as soon as they choose. Aus-
tralia has prospered not alone by the industry and
enterprise of its inhabitants, but also by reason of the
protection it enjoys within the British Empire, and by
reason of the loans for public works which it is able to
obtain because of its political status. Yet these inhabi-
tants, instead of encouraging emigration, have extin-
guished such facilities as existed in former years, the
Labor party in the colonies pursuing a policy of Protec-
tion, fearing that the influx of newcomers might tend to
reduce their own Wages. Hence the ‘ appalling fact.’
“The cost of maintaining the Empire, Australia in-
cluded, comes mainly out of the pockets of the people
Mr. Edmund Barton describes as ‘ paupers ’ —that is, the
people who have not sufficient funds to emigrate; the
people who are not wanted in Australia. Mr. Barton
may reply that it is not the ordinary artisan and laborer
that he refers to as a pauper. Then what does he mean ?
No one has any desire to turn off into the colonies a stream
of workhouse inmates ; but the interests of the Empire
demand that the stream of able-bodied laborers and
artisans, such as now goes in steady volume to the United
States, should be directed to the oversea States of the
Empire. The policy of Australia tends to keep these men
at home.
“ Colonial statesmen would do well, instead of talking
in the old country about appalling facts, and only trying
to get preferential treatment in our markets for'colonial
products—at the cost of the ‘ paupers ’—to concert with
Imperialistic Colonial Secretaries and others some means
of State-aided and State-directed emigration. In this
way the labor market at home would be relieved, the
colonies would be built up, and the Empire would be
stronger. The Labor party in Australia bars the way.”—
English Paper.
The above yarn, which is having a wide cir-
culation in Australia, represents a bad form
of a popular English delusion. Also it
shows up the beastly ingratitude of the
English people in its worst shape.
* * * »
Australia has toiled hard, and is toiling
hard, to preserve Australia for the ungrate-
ful Briton. The vast majority of the Aus-
tralians are either Britons or the descendants
of Britons, and the great mass of these
desire to keep Australia for their own British
selves, for their children, and for the new
Britons who may. join them in the future.
The Aliens Restriction Act, as now adminis-
tered, makes the qualification for entrance to
Australia the ability to write a few sentences
in the English language, and under that law
all manner of Britain’s enemies are shut out,
but the Englishman himself—unless he is
incurably diseased, or an idiot, or a pauper
of the most hopeless variety—comes in
freely. . He is admitted more freely into
Australia than he is now into the United
States, and is asked fewer questions at
Sydney than at New York. Australia lies
at the back door of Asia, and adjacent to the
Hindu, the Chinaman and the Polynesian ;
and its biggest fight with the English
authorities has been for the right to shut
out these individuals, to keep Aus-
tralia white, to preserve it for the
Briton who has come here and become an
Australian, and for the Briton who, it is
hoped, will come and be an Australian by-
and-bye. If cheap black, yellow and piebald
labor is allowed to spread over Australia,
then there will be no more room for British
immigration here than there is in India or
Jamaica, and the policy of the Labor party
has been steadily directed towards preserv-
ing Australia as a fit and suitable field for
British immigrants. In doing so it has
been reviled by unpatriotic British papers
and un-British British authorities. It was
compelled to resort to the subterfuge of an
educational test for immigrants, because the
British Govt, refused to permit the direct
exclusion of the swarm of blacks and yellows
who were elbowing out the white Briton who
is here, and destroying all prospect for the
white Briton who is coming here. And it
was only after long argument with the un-
English Chamberlain—Chamberlain of the
n 'SS er an d the orchid—that it was able to
do anything at all to prevent the English
immigrant being wholly shunted off the face
of Australia.
* •
Australia, in fact, has done, and is doing,
a vast amount more for the British immi-
grant than Britain does itself. It offers him
land on easy terms, and in some cases gratis ;
England only offers him a landlord, and a
rack-rent, and a bailiff, and an eviction. It
offers him freedom—as much freedom as the
British Government will allow it to offer—
from the competition of cheap aliens;
in England he must scramble for work in
competition with swarms of Poles, Bohe-
mians, and Italians, and all the other debris
of Europe. It offers him a share of political
power, such as is undreamt of in England.
The British working man may come out
here and become Premier, as did Parkes,
the Warwickshire agricultural laborer, and
Seddon, the Lancashire artisan ; the British
Premiership is a sacred preserve for the
“ gentlemen of England.” To every healthy
and industrious Englishman, whom England
crowds out so that his farm may be a grouse
moor for some American millionaire, or so
that his billet may go to some cheap and
filthy Polish Jew, Australia offers free access
and such opportunities as its own people
en j°y. The fact that these opportunities
don’t amount to much is another story.
* * * *
As a matter of fact, despite all that Aus-
tralia has done for the British immigrant,
there is practically no British immigrant at
all. He makes for Canada occasionally, for
the United States mostly, for Australia,
since the alluvial gold was practically ex-
hausted, hardly ever. In the main he avoids
British colonies nowadays as he would a pes-
tilence. And the cause of his preference for
the U.S. is simply the enormous Protection
of the U.S. tariff. Australia buys goods
from England to the annual value of about
£6 10s. for every inhabitant of the Common-
wealth ; the U.S. buys from England
annually less than 10s. worth for every
inhabitant. Australia imports altogether
nearly £ll worth of goods annually per in-
habitant—most of it from various parts of
the British Empire ; the United States im-
ports a shade over £2 worth per inhabitant
annually. If Australia adopted the U.S.
tariff, and practically prohibited imports,
then it would necessarily manufacture for
itself an immense variety of goods which
it now imports (the fiscal wrangle as to
whether it would manufacture them profit-
ably or otherwise is not under discussion at
present). Then there would be huge fac-
tories dotted over this country, and the
British immigrant could find work in
them. The only reason why the multi-
tudinous British immigrant can’t find work
here at making our boots and engines
and agricultural implements, and cottons,
woollens, silks, &c., &c., is that we are so
foolish as to buy a ridiculously large propor-
tion of them from England, and consequently
the work is there. The work can’t be in two
places at once. Australia, in proportion to
its population, buys more from England
than does any other country on earth ; it
is an unspeakable fool for doing so, and
when it expands its Protective system to be
something like that of the United States
it will cease from that folly. The rest of
the story can be condensed into three simple
propositions. Because the bulk of oUr goods
are manufactured in England they are not
manufactured here. Because they are not
manufactured here there is hardly any work
to offer the British immigrant. Because
there is hardly any work to offer him he
doesn’t come—or if he does come he is very
sorry for it.
* * * *
Australia certainly offers the British
immigrant as much as it can, while still giving
this foolish and ridiculous support to the
British manufacturer and trader, and it offers
him a lot more than it can afford. It gives
him cheap land and 30 or 40 or 50 years in
which to pay for the land, and very often he
doesn’t pay for it at all; cheap or gratis edu-
cation ; seed-wheat in bad seasons ; railway
carriage for his produce at much less than
the carriage costs ; old-age pensions (in the
two principal States) ; jobs on public works
when he is hard pressed ; cheap advances
per medium of State Land Banks ;
hardly any local taxation ; aid to irri-
gate ; assisted or free passages (in one State,
anyhow) ; and a vote, so that if he
doesn’t think he is treated properly
he can bring influence to bear on the local
member and have his grievances expounded.
But, with all aids and concessions, nine-
tenths of Australia is a country which hardly
offers a bare living to the agriculturist, and,
as the British agriculturist is becoming more
and more of a failure even in his own well-
watered country, he naturally doesn’t
come to our desert lands and make a
success of them in any great numbers.
And the only way we can make room for any
large influx of any other classes is by declar-
ing a relentless trade-war against England,
doing our utmost to damage English indus-
tries, destroying one-tenth of the British
export trade, and transplanting, by main
force, a huge mass of British industries to
Australia. Then we could offer a field for
British immigration, inasmuch as the
artisan who used to make our goods in
Manchester or Sheffield would come
here and make them in Melbourne or
Sydney instead. This is how the United
States has created a great opening
for British immigration. It is its
two thousand million pounds worth per
annum of manufactures—not its compara-
tively paltry seven hundred million pounds
worth of agricultural produce—which enables
the United States to absorb the surplus
labor of England.
* • *
No British possession has ventured to
declare war on British industry with the
relentless ferocity that has characterised
the United States, and to systematically
damage British manufactures and British
export trade as they have done. There-
fore, none of them has anything even
faintly resembling the industries that the
United States possess, for it is impossible
for us to manufacture our own goods and
buy our goods from Britain at the same time.
As a natural result, no British possession
offers the employment that the United
States do, and the British emigrant avoids
“the colonies,” and goes where he can find
work. So the best of Britain’s manhood
leaves it to build up a great, overshadowing
and more or less hostile State, and British
colonisation is coming to be something very
like a failure, and the British empire begins
to sound hollow underfoot. It is sacrificed
to the octopus of the British manufacturer
and the British merchant, and the super-
stition of the British theorist. And Britain
isn’t in the least degree grateful for the
sacrifice. Papers like the one quoted at the
head of this article have a vaguely abusive
idea that Australia should do as much as
heretofore for the support of the British
manufacturer by buying, in proportion to
its population, more of his goods than any
other community on earth ; that it should
neither supply itself with manufactured
goods, and so destroy Britain’s market here,
nor export manufactured goods and destroy
Britain’s market elsewhere ; and at
the same time it should manufacture
largely so that its industries will supply
employment for the British emigrant—for
whom Britain won’t find employment
itself lest the great Freetrado policy should
be damaged. And when this double-
barrelled arrangement won’t go off, the
British press has a grievance against the
Australian Labor party, and blathers at
large. The only British possession which
attracts British immigrants in anything like
large numbers is Canada. And Canada does
so because, even with the concessions it
grants to British trade, it has the heaviest
and most bitterly anti-British tariff in the
Empire. It buys from England to the ex-
tent of 335. per inhabitant per annum, as
compared with Australia’s £6 10s. Because
it buys less it manufactures more, and
because it manufactures more it is able to
offer the emigrant British artisan a job and
the emigrant British farmer a large and
profitable local market. Except while a gold
or diamond or loan boom isin progress there
isno permanent flow of immigration nowadays
to any save manufacturing States. Therefore,
the British immigrant must still go to
strengthen a foreign and hostile State, or the
British oversea possessions must find work
for him—and they can only do that by
throwing the British manufacturer over-
board. And if the British manufacturer is
thrown overboard, the only thing left for
him to do is to start supplying his own
country, and he can only do that, apparently,
by shutting out a great part of the
£500,000,000 worth of imports over which
England is so inflated, and bursting up the
ConDEsr myth.
The Secession Silliness.
It was inevitable that there should arise a “ seces-
sion party ” in Federated Australia. Progress
always arouses opposition. When steam machinery
replaced hand looms, the ignorant saw the Devil
in the innovation, and opposed it as heartily as
they did the locomotive which took the place of
the stage coach. Every step forward, in science,
in industrial enterprise, in politics, has to be
made in the face of the bitter opposition of the
foolish, and, when reform does become a realised
fact, it has to still to face the unpleasant
duty of clearing opponents of cow-like intellect
off the track. This is especially the case
in regard to political reform movements.
Every extension of the franchise, every better-
ment of the system of popular government, has
been fought resolutely when suggested, and
when accomplished has still had to face, for a
while, the despairing attacks of Tories anxious ta
go back to “ the good old days.” The Federation
of the American States, for example, was only
perfected after years of struggle in the forum and
in the field. Beginning as a loose confederation
of almost absolutely autonomous settlements, the
American colonies found it necessary to their
industrial and national prosperity to develop into
a scientific Federation of States, supreme in local
matters, but subject to the legislation of the
Union in all affairs of national importance. But
every step forward —though rendered urgently
necessary by the need for preserving and develop-
ing the new nation—was made in the teeth of
bitter opposition. The argument from the Federal
side at the time when the most serious change was
made (1788), which distinctly stamped the United
States as a firm Federation, instead of a loose
Confederation, has been preserved in that classic
of constitutional literature, The Federalist.
* * * *
It will be noted from that work that the argu-
ments of the Secessionists in America then, or
of those who believed at least in a looser form of
union, were worthy of some serious notice, and
had to be argued by Hamilton, Jay and Madison
on serious lines. The dis-unionists were in the
wrong, but they erred in a dignified and pseudo-
patriotic way. Their claim, however mistaken,
was evidently inspired by a genuine belief that
the Federation would contract the liberties and
prejudice the prosperity of the States. It is a
pitiful comparison to turn from them to the Aus-
tralian secessionists of to-day, like turning from
an honest, respectable, good-intentioned if dull-
witted ox to a small, disreputable, and irre-
sponsible he-goat. There could be no better proof
of the satisfaction which the Federal union has
given to reasonable Australians than is provided
by the character of the opposition which it has
aroused. There is not one argument deserving of
serious consideration alleged against the union,
not one person worthy of serious notice among
its enemies.
* * * •
Mr. Philp of Queensland may be taken, per-
haps, as the leader of the secessionists ; he, at any
rate, is the most noisy and tli9 most prominent of
the crew. The best reason for destroying the
union that he can allege is its refusal to
allow a continuation of the Kanaka slave-
trade in Queensland. This might fairly
be advanced as, in a sense, a valid,
though essentially a paltry, provincial and
un-Australian excuse for hostility to the Federa-
tion in Queensland, but for the fact that that
State is itself just as resolute in opposition to the
slave traffic as is the Federation. The anti-Kanaka
laws were passed largely at the instigation of, and
with the unwavering support of, a majority of the
Queensland representatives in both Houses of the
Federal Parliament. With that fact in view the
charge against the Federation of being insensible
to the State views of Queensland falls to the
ground. If Mr. Philp secured the secession which
he professes to hope for, and at the same time
allowed the Queensland people a free and un-
fettered franchise, he would have just as serious a
ground for complaint against the State Parlia-
ment on the black labor question, and would have
to start a new secession movement with Thursday
Island as the Promised Land.
• • » *
The opposition in N.S.W. to the Federal Union
is fairly represented—its ignorance, its appalling
contempt for facts, and its narrow-mindedness—by
a letter from “Junius” which Sydney D.T. pub-
lished last week with evident relish :
Under Federation it is indispensable that the sum of
£9,000,000 be raised through the customs, to become
£1.8,000,000 by the time it reaches the consumer. And
whether that enormous sum be raised by Mr. Barton or
by Mr. Reid, or by Mr. Anybody else, the result must
practically be the same to the great mass of the com-
munity, and the purchasing power of the poor man’s
sovereign in New South Wales must thereby shrink to
13s. 4d. With this fact before them, can anyone suppose
that the poor and the comparatively poor are so much in
love with Federation, and with Mr. Barton, and with Mr.
Reid, and with the Victorian manufacturers, that they
will consent to pauperise themselves and their wives and
their little ones in order that manufacturers and pro-
minent politicians may be enriched ? I think not. For-
tunately there is a remedy. Bet the 107,000 who voted for
the bill and the 82,000 who voted against it combine their
numbers boldly and unitedly, and then let them call
upon their representatives in Parliament to pass a reso-
lution to withdraw from Federation. And then who shall
hinder us from resuming the political freedom which we
so foolishly threw away ? Holland and Belgium separated,
and why should not we ?
In conclusion, let me remind the people of New South
Wales that “Providence helps those who help them-
selves,” and that “ they who would be free, themselves
must strike the blow.”
It is not a fact that under Federation “ it is indis-
pensable that the sum of £9,000,000 be raised
through the Customs.” There is no necessity to
raise a penny. The Federation could, if it pleased,
obtain all its revenue by direct taxation, and
raise nothing through the Customs. But it is a
fact that the present Federal Government, out of
consideration for the States, and more particularly
for the State of N.S.W. (whose Treasurer, repre-
senting a Government which commanded a huge
majority at last State election, has publicly
pleaded for a higher Federal tariff), has very
properly sought to raise a sufficient customs
revenue to keep the States, at any rate, fairly
solvent. Passing over a few of the more idiotic
remarks —there is not a remedy by passing a
resolution in the N.S.W. Parliament to withdraw
from the Federation. Such a resolution would
have as much effect as a vote of censure on the
Balfour Cabinet by the Balmain Town Council.
“Junius” is even out in his proverbs. Those
“ who help themselves” often go to prison, as the
man who stole the sausages discovered, and those
“who would be free and themselves strike the
blow” are apt under some circumstances to get
hurt.
* * * *
An almost equal degree of folly and lack of
candor as that of “Junius” is shown by Mr.
Stone, M.P., who has arisen as the leader of the
Secessionists in Groperland. He has given notice
in the Westralian Parliament:—
It is desirable that the Government should take such
steps as will bring about a separation of West Australia
from the Federal union, in order that this State may have
the same control over its finances and other important
matters it heretofore enjoyed, and that the present time
would be more opportune for the withdrawal than a more
distant date, when the State may have to share a heavy
Federal debt.
Mr. Stone ignores the facts that since Federation
Westralia has had control of more revenue
than it ever had before ; and that since it
occupies about the second worst place (with the
prospect of soon being absolutely worst) in thq


loan indebtedness per head of the Australian
States, an anxiety on its part about being bur-
dened with an unfair share of a Federahsed debt
is needless. The State which owes least may
suffer a slight temporary loss if the debts are
federalised ; the State which owes most, or next
to most, certainly cannot.
m * • *
These are fair samples of the imbecile Secession-
ist argument. They practically prove that in its
working Federation is in all respects bene-
ficial. If the party of disunion cannot
advance any facts, or even any plausible lies, to
help their case, it must be a very bad case indeed.
And that is just the position.. The Secessionists
are made up of a few disappointed notoriety-hunt-
ing politicians, followed by a few of those
persons of weak intellect who would follow any-
thing that is passing with a brass band or a noisy
bellman, even to a flea’s funeral. They are backed
up by certain petty-minded and parochial papers
whose proprietaries are being asked by an unfeel-
ing Federal Government to pay postage on their
parcels. There couldn’t be better proof of the
strength of the union than the character of its
enemies.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Indus trial Arbitration in Working 1 .
N.S.W. Trades Unions are having brought home
to them the fact that Industrial Arbitration car-
ries its responsibilities as well as its privileges.
The Industrial Arbitration Court, thanks to Mr.
Justice Cohen and the other members, has shaped
itsdecisions, not alone with perfect justice, but with
a notable sympathy for the admirable principle of
trades unionism. Still, it has had occasion to
administer some severe rebukes to labor organisa-
tions. The litigation in regard to the Australian
Workers’ Union and some of its very foolish rules
has not yet been completed, and cannot be made
the subject of comment; but the proceedings now
pending in Equity to make the union responsible
for alleged unlawful acts of its members
and officers, and the Arbitration Court action
in regard to registration, however they may
result, will certainly teach the necessity of a care-
ful observance of the law which makes for indus-
trial peace. The best advice which a friend can
give to the A.W.U. —at preseut encompassed
round with many difficulties and beset with many
enemies—is to hire a front-rank lawyer at once.
The Union has done such good work for the bush
toilers that it would be a pity to see it, for lack of
sound legal advice, subjected to severe loss because
of the mistakes of well-meaning officers who
“didn’t know.” The Shipwrights’ Union—one
of the oldest and most Conservative industrial
bodies in the State —which didn’t bother to
register under the Act, because it was quite
able to take care of itself, thank you, has
also had a mild shock by being hauled before
the court and ordered to keep the peace in regard
to a little strike. On the other hand, the Bread-
Carters’ Union has won a decision which is not
only just but sympathetic to labor aspirations.
And Mr. Justice Cohen has gone out of his way
to state that any prejudice he had ever enter-
tained against trades-unions has now been effaced
by the knowledge of their working that he
has gained. Presumably, Mr. Cohen’s ideas were
previously gathered from the daily papers ; now
they are founded on facts. On the whole,, the
labor unions have, so far, had much the better of
the argument, and of the decisions, in the Arbitra-
tion Court; but they must decide, for the future, to
have officers who make it their business to under-
stand the law as it affects industrial disputes, and
must keep in mind the fact that they are forbidden
(as well as the other fellow) to break the peace.

The Debts that are Falling Due.
It is commonly understood that close on £8,000,000
worth of new Australian loans are pending, and
will be put on the London market the moment
there is a fair prospect of getting them through.
This is in addition to, and quite apart from, the
fact that N.S.W., Victoria, and S..A. are con-
stantly selling stock locally—selling it every day
and as much of it as they can dispose of. Queens-
land wants £2,000,000 ; Victoria, at least
.£1,000,000; Westralia, about as much as it can
get; S.A. wants something; N.S.W. wants the
earth, but would like about £4,000,000 to go on
with. The local subscriptions are not large
now this despite the fact that the latest
banking returns show that there is more
actual cash in the Commonwealth than there
has been for a considerable time. The latest
Victorian loan issued in London was mostly left
on the hands of the underwriters, and all the
recent flotations have been done at a very heavy
cost. Also the English papers are speaking more
disrespectfully of Australian credit, and the Aus-
tralian way of using loans as revenue, and Aus-
tralian profligacy and helplessness, than they have
done for a long while. All these things make it
ten times more important that the Federal Gov-
ernment should at once assume the responsibility
of the old State debts so far as to take over each
State loan as it falls due, and renew it by issuing
Federal consols for the amount. It isn’t necessary
to devise any great scheme of conversion or con-
solidation as yet; all that is wanted is that it
should take over each loan as it matures, renew it,
and charge the interest to the State concerned. It
is growing more and more obvious that a crisis in
the credit of these States may come at any
time —it may come before next Federal session
begins. If the crisis merely prevented the float-
ing of new loans it would be the best thing
that could happen. But old loans keep falling
due, and they must be renewed, crisis or no
crisis, and if they have to be renewed in a bad
time, on no better security than the much depre-
ciated State credit, the price paid for the money
may be something enormous. Queensland, in
1891, had an old loan to be renewed, and it
sold its £lOO securities for £BB each to raise the
money. In 1893 it had a still worse experience,
and was glad to take less _ than £B6. The
Federal Government has nothing to do with the
new State profligacies, and need offer them no
encouragement. But the old ones can’t be un-
done ; and by taking over these ancient liabilities
as the respective pay-days arrive, with the, as
yet, unsmirclied Federal credit to back the oper-
ation, hundreds of thousands, or even millions,
might be saved. The present Federal session
should not be allowed to end without this matter
being attended to. These States were badly
“ squeezed ” over most of the loans when they
were first floated. There is no reason why they
should be still more badly squeezed over the re-
newals, if the Federal credit can help them.

Be the Quiraing.
“Shell-back ” writes about a vanished ship
By all means let the scope of the Quiraing inquiry be
as wide as possible, with the object of discovering if
culpable neglect exists in any direction. Amongst the
profession, however, a prevalent notion exists that these
so-called inquiries into marine disasters are little more
than whitewashing performances.
Take some examples of the last few years. In the Alert
case the Board of Inquiry found (I believe) that a vessel
of the Alert’s type should not have been granted a certifi-
cate allowing her to trade outside the Heads, being osten-
sibly built for Bay work. But has that finding prevented
other vessels of a similar build engaging in the outside
business ?
In the Glenelg case it was proved at the inquiry that
the Vic. Marine Board surveyor permitted the vessel to
go to sea knowing that her shaft was defective (he himself
had condemned it, and a new one under construction
was to be fitted on her return). I do not say that this
was the actual cause of the disaster, yet who knows but
what the defective machinery may have seriously con-
tributed to it ? Yet the finding of the court was to the
effect that no one connected with the running of the
vessel was to blame.
The case of the Federal is still more wrapped up in
mystery, there not being even a single survivor to throw
any light on the cause of her foundering. Possibly she
may have been overloaded. Such things do occasionally
happen at the N.S.W. southern coal jetties, where super-
vision is notoriously lax. I have seen, on more than one
occasion, colliers arrive in Hobson’s Bay, after a four-
days’ passage from the coal ports, with the centre of the
disc still awash, even after allowing for consumption of
fuel, water and stores. An inch or two in the amount of
freeboard may seem but a trivial matter to the shore-
going mind, but it is an item of serious importance to a
deep-loaded collier in dirty weather. There is always
lcwt. of coal in an over-loaded vessel which was the cwt.
with which safety ended and danger began.
The Quiraing was a vessel of a build much in vogue
some quarter century past—long, narrow and deep in
comparison to her tonnage—a type that has since been
discarded for the beamy light-draught, big carriers of
to-day. Vessels of the Quiraing type are always ugly in
a seaway when coal-laden, and many have gone under,
leaving none to tell the tale. Technically, when deep-
loaded and heeled-over by the action of wind or sea,
“ their metacentric height is too small to ensure a position
of average stability.” The Quiraing’s immunity from
accident up to the iast voyage might be due to the fact
that her career in Australian waters has been mostly con-
fined to the smooth seas of the Queensland coast.
In common with many others, I hold the opinion that
the system under which our Marine Board surveying
officials are appointed is radically wrong. They owe their
appointment to, and are responsible to, a body consisting
largely of shipowners or men controlling large shipping
interests, whose natural tendencies are to make the best of
the second-rate vessels it is a surveyor’s duty to check. I do
not say that they are under the direct influence of
individual members of the Board ; still, the tendency to
subordinate (in doubtful cases) their sense of duty to the
opinions and wishes of interested members (briefly, to
stretch a point) must always be present. These officials
should be appointed directly by Government, the
positions thrown open to public competition ; they should
be given an absolutely free hand, and, like judges, their
appointments should be for life orduring good behaviour.

The Inevitable Gibe.
Mr. Irvine declares that the State is committed to a
certain public works policy, which must be carried out.
What he really means is that the country must continue
to be artificially fed with borrowed money to enable its
people to struggle on against the obstacles which “ labor
legislation ” is constantly placing in the way of industrial
development. The difficulty of the position is, therefore,
quite apparent.—Sydney D.T., 4/9/’O2.
That is the first warning whisper of the torrent
of reproach which will one day fall upon (and per-
haps overwhelm) the “labor parties” in the
various Australian State Parliaments. The finan-
cial position in each State is becoming desperate.
The period of reckless borrowing is rapidly
merging into the period when further borrowing
must be from reckless lenders—and they can’t,
as a rule, be found; and when this great new
country will have to paint its gaols, metal its roads,
and buy its Marie Corelli for its bucolic school
of arts, absolutely out of its own resources. This
period will be one of weeping and gnashing of
teeth, and tightening of waist-belts, and general
uncomfortableness ; and the fat Tory person and
his newspaper are already preparing to throw the
whole blame of it on the Labor party. Truth to
tell, the Labor party in every State, except, per-
haps, Queensland, has put itself in the position to
have the responsibility thrown upon its shoulders.
The chief characteristic of a Labor party seems to
have always been a willingness to force on
reforms, such as old age pensions and a minimum
wage, and an equal willingness to _ let the
issue of paying for those reforms slide. At
present, they are being paid for out of
borrowed money. When no more money can be
borrowed the indications are that the desperate
straits of the various States will be attributed
solely to such labor legislation, and that it will be
abolished with a triumphant snort from the Fat
section of the community. _ Then the Labor
parties will recognise the mistake they. made. in
wholly neglecting the financial problem, in forcing
up the rate of spending in some quarters without
insisting on corresponding savings or a corres-
ponding increase in taxation, and in meekly
endorsing the 1.0. U. policy. It is not yet too late
to make a sudden change and put Financial
Reform in the forefront of every Federal and State
Labor platform. But it is the Eleventh Hour.
They must hustle to get to the vineyard in time.
Tho Imported Criminal. r ’
“ Stiff ” writes on the imported criminal
The correspondent who commented in a recent issue
upon the prevalence of foreign names among those con-
nected with particularly shocking crimes will find the
same thing in U.S. Pick up any American paper and you
will read that Schwartz is still at large, or that Hein-
bofer has confessed. I am no Jingo, but it is plain to me
that the U.K. is miles above any continental country,
both as regards the crime-rate and the seriousness of the
crimes committed, and I am satisfied that the foreign
Australian, excellent citizen as he is in other respects,
does more to provide interesting items for the newspapers
than all our multitude of politicians.
Against this aspersion on Schwartz there
is the experience of N.S.W., which finds
that the most criminal section of its
population is that belonging to the United
States; then, on a gradually-improving scale of
badness, Scandinavians, French, Russians, Danes,
Italians, Irish, Hindus, Scotch, Maorilanders,
Germans (better than all the aforementioned),
English (exactly level with Germans), Tasmanians,
Victorians, South Australians, N.S. Walers, and
Chinamen. The N.S. Walers naturally show up
well because they include most of the babies.
But the imported Schwartz when brought down
to hard figures, is in N.S.W. as law-abiding as the
Englishman, and more so than the Scot or the
Irishman, and only gets into gaol at the rate of
17 per 10,000, against the Yankee’s 71 per 10,000.
And why these things are even Policeman
Murphy, pondering deeply on his “bate,” can-
not explain. The U.K. has, compared to popu-
tion, the smallest prison population in Europe;
but then what gets a man into prison in one
country doesn’t always have the same effect in
another, so it isn’t always possible to judge by
that. Under the same code of laws in N.S.W.
Fritz Heinbofer shows up better than Alex-
ander M‘Tavish. Of course, a crime committed
by Heinbofer stands out conspicuously —so does
one by Heintzenroder or Sausagestein or
Schmellitsbeer. The name is sufficient to guar-
antee that.

Wanted: Boom for the Sweater.
There is a tendency among mainland employers
to regard Tasmania just now as a sort of haven of
rest for the sweater. In that delectable land there
is as yet no Wages Board, no Parliamentary Labor
Party, no devastating socialistic legislation ; and
it is the one State of the Commonwealth where an
employer is granted the sacred privilege of con-
ducting his business in his own way. And under
Federation the Tasmanian product gets free access
to all Australia. In Victoria, it is true, factories
keep multiplying, despite the Minimum Wage
Law, and there are more factory employees now
than there ever were before —far more than there
ever were in the days when there was no Mini-
mum Wage. But one man who ran a very small
brush factory employing 20 hands lately shifted
from Melbourne to Tasmania, and though 2000 or
3000 new workers found employment last
year in Victorian factories, that one brush-
shop, and these 20 employees, have been
the subject of such a devastating yell con-
cerning the “ ruin of Victorian industries ” that
the 2000 or 3000 new Vic. employees and the
dozens of new factories passed almost unnoticed.
If all Victoria had been one huge brush to brush the
head of Time and to shine the boots of Space there
could hardly have been more disturbance. But
just when it was understood that all Victoria was
going bodily to Tasmania (where factory inspection
is left to the aldermen, who mostly own the fac-
tories and consequently don’t inspect), Nicholls,
M.L.A., began to interfere. By reason of
Nicholls there was a debate in the Hobart Par-
liament, in which things like this were said :
The sanitary condition of the various factories was
filthy. It was no use hiding the facts. People could
smell us across the Straits-(laughter)—and while Hobart
was held up as one of the fairest. cities on the face of the
earth, from a sanitary point of view it was most filthy.
On the strength of these assertions, the House
granted Nicholls leave to bring in a Bill making
factory inspection a State function in lasmania,
instead of leaving it to be left undone by
the City Council, and Premier Lewis was
deeply shocked by the revelation and pro-
mised support to the Bill. So, after all, it
seems possible that, in a few years, there won t be
one thoroughly dirty State left to which the
sweater can retreat to smell as badly as he wants
to, and not one place in the Commonwealth where
Factory Acts will cease from troubling. Every
indignant manufacturer on this side has been
threatening to shift to Tasmania and be dirty and
sordid and at peace. And behold Nicholls
threatens to clean up the Promised Land also,
and to start inspecting it, just as the downtrodden
States of the mainland are cleaned and inspected.

The Testing of Socialism.
The Australasian’s political “leaders” are as
inspiring as ever. A slab of a recent one as a
sample :
Socialism is now on its trial in Australia. It is now
being tested.” The fact is that at best socialism enables
a small section of society to take something from other
sections in prosperous times. When the community is
hard hit by adverse circumstances socialism has nothing
to say or give. Tested by the times which are its oppor-
tunity, it is blind, powerless, bankrupt—a contemptible
fraud upon human intelligence.
Comment on that sort of thing is almost impos-
sible. Who can gild the lily or add one inch to
the stature of everlasting Truth ? Isn’t it super-
fluous to point out that socialism is a failure,
especially where it hasn’t been tried ? If it wasn’t
for Private Enterprise rushing to the assistance
of a “hard hit” community where would the
State be? The State bank-notes issued by
Dibbs’s Government during the N.S.W. bank
crisis of 1893 were doubtless only kept afloat
through the guarantee of the A.J.S. Bank; and
the deposits in. Melbourne Saving’s Bank were
probably saved by the guarantee of the Standard
Bank and the Premier Permanent Building
Society, and Bent and Matthew Davies, and
Jimmy Munro. During the worst phases of the
drought it is well-known that the socialistic railways
would have ceased running altogether if the private
sheep-growers hadn’t supplied tallow for wheel-
grease, and mutton at cheap rates for the use of
the starving railway commissioners. What would
have happened if Private Enterprise hadn t come
along to voluntarily undertake mail contracts,
gratis, in Queensland this year ? It is surely un-
necessary to enlarge upon the established fact
that the socialistic attempts to run a Government
Post Office would long since have resulted in
utter bankruptcy, if the big metropolitan
newspaper owners hadn’t taken the whole con-
cern under their patronage, and subscribed
to keep the show going. And isn’t every
lighthouse on our coasts, and every jetty m
our harbors, a standing monument to the whole-
souled philanthropy of Private Enterprise, and
the incapacity of socialistic methods of doing
things? Of course. That being so, why in
thunder should the Australasian consider it
necessary to periodically resuscitate the bogie ?

A Crown Defender —Once more.
A satire on the Law’s tender regard for accused
persons and its alleged belief that “ every man is
innocent until he is proved guilty ” was afforded
by an application made to Vic. Judge Holroyd
lately that the Crown should pay the “trial
expenses of a youth who was acquitted of shooting
Chinaman One Sin. The lad’s father had been
put to large expense in lawyer’s fees, bringing
witnesses from the backblocks, etc., and Judge
Holroyd reckoned it a fair thing the Crown
should pay, but he had no power to make an
order ; indeed he wasn’t quite certain about his
right to make a recommendation. The Law in
Victoria hypocritically prides itself on providing
legal assistance (15 guineas’ worth) for persons
capitally charged, well knowing that it couldn’t
run the case itself for less than ten times that
sum. For the prosecution of a presumably
innocent man the Law provides an expensive
barrister, a solicitor, a well-equipped Crown Law
dept., several detectives, and as many police as may
be required ; also it splashes revenue around in
bringing witnesses from the ends of the earth, if
necessary; but, while assuring the accused that it
considers him perfectly moral, honest, and quite
unobjectionable until proved otherwise, it stead-
fastly refrains from helping him to prove that, he
is not otherwise —even to the extent of declining
to put at his disposal the servants of the so-called
Department of Justice, whom he himself helps, to
maintain. In any case requiring much searching
of evidence or the bringing from a distance of
many witnesses, unless the accused or his relations
are wealthy, the defence has to be practically
abandoned. And every Judge must needs know
that he is expected to sentence persons who are
found guilty simply because they hadn’t the
means to bring together the witnesses who would
have pi’oved them innocent, and because the
Crown refused to find the means.
