Tasmania’s land assessment system is a weird and
wonderful and dreadful affair. First, a local
authority (as often as not the bush policeman)
fixes the rental value of the property, and takes
care not to over-estimate the farm of his brother
Mike or his uncle Bill. His estimate may be
appealed against before the local magistracy,
which carefully reduces the valuation as regards
its brother Mike and its uncle Bill. Another
official guesses what the capital value should be
from the previous guesses at the rental value, and
on this is levied a small sum in the £, the result
being that brother Mike and uncle Bill pay next
to nothing. Treasurer Bird, in the course of
amend men t,~--a§ks for power to resume properties
at 10 per cent, above the valuation, but he doesn’t
propose to do away with the ridiculous method of
valuing, and there is hardly enough energy
in Tasmania to resume anything. And the
resumption idea only works well when, as
in Maoriland, the owner has to make his
own valuation, and when he knows that the
Government is in dead earnest over the resump-
tion business. An active resumption policy,
worked in conjunction with the Tasmanian
assessment system, would only mean that the
valuator would have a chance, to grossly under-
value the property of his enemy Mike, so that the
State might lake it from him at much less than it
is worth unless the owner appealed against the
absurdly low figure put upon his property. And
that isn’t exactly what is aimed at.

A Hobart pressman (any politician who desires
to know his name may apply unsuccessfully at
this office) writes about the House-tax proceed-
ings :
On what is called a “ catch vote,” the Lewis-Bird Minis-
try met with a reverse of 14 to 8, the other night. Lewis
immediately adjourned the House to think things over,
the suddenness and unexpectedness of the vote having
winded him, so to speak. The Opposition appeared to be
quite as surprised as Lewis— so surprised, in fact, that it
didn’t know what to say or what to do, consequently the
House rose without any apology being offered to Lewis
for the unfortunate accident. When the House met next
night strenuous efforts were made by the Opposition
people to convince Lewis that the blow had been acci-
dental, that they had no desire to knock the Government
out, and that, if they had the faintest idea that Lewis
would take the vote seriously, they would have made
arrangements to hit him softer in a harder place, or
words to that effect. Still, they urged that the Govern-
ment had, in a way, invited the b'ow by its awful House-
tax proposals, and gave Lewis to understand that, though
no harm was meant on the present occasion, some modi-
fication of the House-tax programme would be necessary
if similar unfortunate Occurrences were to be avoided in
future. So, for the present, things are slinking along as
usual.

Queensland Premier Philp has a pathetio faith
in owing lots of money as the true secret of
national greatness. The absentee is simply a man
to whom a country owes lots of money, and a pro-
posal to levy a special absentee-tax on this person
was put forward the other day in Queensland
Assembly. Then Philp grew excited as follows :
History would show that the whole of the industries in
Queensland had been opened up by people living outside.
Take the sugar industry and sheep farming for instance,
which had all been opened up by New South Wales and
Victorian people who had spent their money here. It
was a mistake to do anything which might have the effect
of sending away capital already invested here, or pre-
venting other capital from coming in. The great cry now
was for more work for the people ; but an absentee-tax
would cause people who had money invested here, and
who did not care to live here, to sell out and take their
money away. They did not want this. They wanted
money spent in the colony.
Philp’ s alarm lest legislation should lift the
mortgage, and scare away the debt that is already
in Queensland, and prevent more debt .arriving,
and stop the outflow of interest, and compel the
absentees to sell out-to local men so that Queens-
land may own some of its own industries, would
be comic if it hadn’t so many of the elements of
sordid tragedy.
Pious Politicians.
It has been said that people cannot be made
sober, or even virtuous, by Act of Parliament.
The reason, perhaps, is that up-to-date Members
of Parliament themselves have not been con-
spicuous for virtue or sobriety. We know what
happened to those who touched the Ark with
profane hands. But the Christian Endeavour
Society is going to alter this state of affairs.
Speaking at a meeting of that body held in
Sydney Y.M.C. A. Hall, the other evening, the
Rev. James became greatly uplifted by the spirit
and said: “If we could make every one of the
politicians a Christian Endeavourer there would
be a great upraising and purifying of public life
(cheers). The more fear of God they got into
their representatives the better it would be for the
people. Politicians should fear the will of God
first, and the will of the people afterwards ; and
if the will of the people was not on a line with
that of God, then, even at the risk of losing their
seats, that will should be disregarded.” _ Noble
sentiments. But how are these chosen politicians
to make sure as to which is really the will of God
in any given ease—the closing of Public Libraries
on Sundays, for instance? The clergy will ex-
pound :
Vox populi vox Dei ? No,
It is a heathen lie:
Vox clerici vox Dei —so
The churches all reply.
It needs no learning polygot
To prove this statement true—
The will of Gon is always whab
The clergy wish to do.
God is conservative. For why ?
Because, with humour grim,
Progressive statesmen they might try
Their hands reforming Him.
To bring Good Times so long desired,
And with Bad Times to cope,
No carnal talents are required—
But simply Faith and Hope.
When under Drought and Debt we groan,
Say, what can Genius do ?
Good solid Piety alone
Can pull the Country through.
A man may have the Patriot’s Heart,
May have the Statesman’s Brain—
If unredeemed he plays his part.
He plays his part in vain.
But, if he have the Gospel grip
The State is saved, although
He know no more of statesmanship
Than cows of muskets know.
And if through him the State is sold
And left in evil case,
’Tis better to be poor in gold,
Dear friends, than poor in grace.
Your sinful statesmen they may show
Proofs of Prosperity—
When Armageddon’s trumpets blow
How vain it all will be !

«
Gillette’s much - heralded “Sherlock
Holmes,” at the Adelaide Royal, proves to be
a good acting play, with very little of the book
about it, excepting for bits of dialogue, in which
Holmes guesses what Dr. Watson has had for
breakfast. It is nearly all pure melodrama from
th&beginning, where Alice Faulkner is tortured
to make her give up the “papers,” to the end,
where Moriarty is removed in handcuffs. Of
comic relief there is hardly any, and the feminine
interest is of the slightest, though at the end
Sherlock allows Alice to embrace him, which is
foreign to his character. The great scene
in the gas-chamber is well worked up, but
had injudiciously been altogether given away
by preliminary pars. So the whole audience
knew, when S.H. smashed the lamp and was
tracked by his cigar, that he would simply place
it on the window-sill and get away to the door
while they rushed it. The character of Moriarty
is well worked up from the few hints given in the
book, and at the end he is ingeniously made to
foreshadow another and a fatal meeting on the
Continent. This leaves an opening for another
Holmes drama later on. The acting all round is
excellent. The play is written in the American
language, which is becoming more condensed and
more unlike the English language every day. But
it is a good show, and a safe draw anywhere. It
ran for one week only, the scenery having to go
on to Melbourne, and the co. filled up with
“Secret Service.” Meanwhile, Holloway, at the
little Tivoli, is being (if possible) more gory than
before.
* ¥f at *
On Saturday last, at Sydney Lyceum,
Bland Holt staged “ Riding to Win,” a
drama that rests a good deal of bad senti-
ment and many illogical, not to say foolish,
actions, on good scenery, a bicycle race and
a dog. All of them bear up under the dread-
ful weight—especially the dog. He is a discern-
ing animal, for whenever the characters are
obliged to be more tiresome than usual, he strolls
round biting a stick in an ostentatious manner,
and so drowning the remark that every cloud has
a silver lining. At other times when told to leave
the boards, he takes most of himself to the side
scenes, but waves-a lonely tail at the audience, as
if loth to leave his admirers quite bereft. As
usual the stolen notes are found on Baker, and the
only man who could explain the mystery has gone
to the S’African war, Boerland being of late a
useful dumping ground for the inconvenient stage
person who could smash up the plot in Act I if
not hurried away. Baker has got so accustomed
to having the notes found on him that he doesn’t
mention who gave them to him, or try to clear
himself in any way; he merely says, to himself
that he won’t cast suspicion on the African man,
who, later on, varies the usual proceeding
by getting drowned instead of shot. Baker then
takes his subterranean voice and his load of
obloquy to the banks of the Yarra in order to give
Bland Holt a chance of putting real water
behind the footlights. Boats float on and off
the stage in a realistic manner, and the circum-
stance that one oarsman, on opening night,
rooted up two trees and one rock, is an incident
that present writer would not dream of men-
tioning.
» * * *
A girl in pink who wants her good name
restored, and otherwise proves troublesome to the
villain is pushed into the river that she may
be rescued by the dog, who does his work in a
frolicsome spirit, afterwards, with great sagacity,
refusing to rescue the villain. The next act or so
partly occupies itself with the pink girl in a dry
condition and accompanied by pa, the boat-
builder. She demands her lost name over again,
and villain Albert Norman agrees to give her
his through the persuasion of the boatbuilding
father’s revolver. This leap into matrimony is
alluded to on the programme as the way of the
transgressor, which is hard. Meanwhile, Baker’s
name is cleared by the discovery of the African
man’s drowned diary, and Frances Ross’s father,
in consequence, remarks that he will make a new
will, which fatal determination naturally results
in the murder of this old gentleman after
he has gone to bed by sitting upright
in a chair. When an actor means to go
to bed in a very realistic manner, he takes his
boots off before sitting down in a chair ; but this
is only done on great occasions. Baker and
Frances Ross, having no money and no furniture,
and nothing but Baker’s blighted character to live
upon (it gets blighted more about this time),
decide to get married and settle down in a large
kitchen, where they starve in a manner that
reflects discredit on the Melbourne Charitable
Aid system. Stimulated by some potatoes which
Bland Holt cooks publicly, Baker determines to
win the great bicycle race, and it is understood
that if he does, all the clouds will roll away.
Consequently, a good deal of scenery, with a
policeman in it, is seen rushing past some half-
dozen slightly moving bikes, till at the end
Baker’s machine goes a few inches in front of the
others and wins. Another scene, consisting of
hundreds of people, mostly’ dummy, cheering
Baker, flashes on and off the stage, and peace
descends at the boathouse, where the villain is
arrested for the murder in a hurried manner, and
rather as if it were an afterthought on the part of
the playwright. “ Riding to Win ” is a picture-
sque, jerry-built, and inconsequential drama that
would tumble to pieces if the bicycle race scene
were taken from it, or the Yarra, or the dog.
* * * •
Frances Ross, as the daughter of the Old
Identity who dies in his boots, is at her best in
Act 1., and probably as good as the part will let
her be in the next three or four acts. It isn’t a
very distinct part, though there is a good deal of
it. She and Baker merely have to carry a load of
unjust suspicion and poverty up and down the
stage, and make no great effort, till the bicycle
race, to shake the load off. They are simply two
persons tossed to and fro by a heartless world, and
denied billets, till Baker has a chance to reinstate
himself by sitting on a bike while a policeman
whirls past him. The load of misery falls on
Baker without reason, so that it is just as well it
falls off the same way. Bland Holt, of the
Second N.S.W. Contingent, shares the usual
comedy honors with Mrs. Bland (who appears as
Sarah Pecker), while Miss Harrie Ireland acts
well as an irrelevant blind girl, but none of these,
or the numerous other persons on the programme,
really matter to any great extent. It is not that
they matter so much less than usual, as that the
scenery matters more.
* * * *
“The Lady Slavey,” now in her sixth
week, continues her career of domestic service at
Sydney Criterion, but there are rumors that she
will soon cease to be a moral lesson and to love a
millionaire for himself alone, and that the unpaid-
for O’Neill residence will shortly be a thing of the
past. Charles Kenningham and Miss Marie
Alston join the Sheridan company for the next
production.
* * * *
“San Toy ” gives place to “The Belle of
New York ” at Sydney Palace this Saturday, and
Mr. Williamson’s Musical Comedy Co. will be put
to the test of reviving a piece which was originally
produced here by a strong American co. “The
Belle ” is a very bright absurdity, full of smart
Yankee humor and catchy music, and so favorable
has been the impression made by the Palace co.’s
work in . “ San Toy ” that the public is quite pre-
pared to believe, and to hope, that it will do
justice to the star of Gotham.
* * * *
The “ Chinese Honeymoon ” has pro-
spered greatly at Sydney Royal all last week.
Why it should have been so The Bulletin makes
no attempt to guess. The first warm, dry breath
of summer has blown on the dreadful mother-in-
law, and she has looked even older, drier, and
more like a ringbarked Joss than she looked
before. The huge, shining countenance of the
Chinese Emperor has shone a little extra as the
weather grew warmer. Josephine Stanton’s
comely legs are still buried in the two loose, flap-
ping perpendicular tunnels of her Chinese cos-
tume, and other things are as before. No ex-
planation has been tendered as to why the cockney
waitress conceived the idea of wearing a reddish
streak across her face, as if she had unwisely
put her countenance on the edge of a hot stove.
The “Chinese Honeymoon” is probably an
attempt at Gilbert’s peculiar variety of inverted
humor, but in inefficient hands the joke that Gil-
bert used to turn upside down merely subsides on
its back, and remains there like any other drunk.
In particular the Irish-looking admiral, who is
despatched in a warship to find a nice girl who, on
seeing the emperor’s photo, and being informed
that he is a Chinese bill-poster in low circum-
stances, will agree to marry him for love alone,
is not funny ; the joke round which he revolves is
an industrious and hard-working joke and that is
all. Mr. Pineapple’s ill-selected complexion is not
amusing either. And as already hinted the rush
on the stairs of the Royal is a very large rush
indeed.
* * * *
Re-appearance of Miss Dorothy Yane
(now Mrs. Menzies) on the Sydney stage brought
an unusually large audience to the Tivoli on
Saturday, and there was an unwonted air about
the theatre of haughty lorgnettes and floral com-
pliments. Miss Vane came back to the stage
temporarily with the purpose' of collecting
money for the sufferers by the Mt. Kembla
disaster. Her salary (£5O) goes to the
fund, and she makes an appeal each night,
which promises to realise over £5O. So her
donation will be a handsome one. A song, “ The
Earth-encompassed Miner,” modelled on Kip-
ling’s “ Absent-minded Beggar,” was sung with
sweetness and sympathy by Miss Yane; but an
item from the “ Geisha ” was not at all successful,
The “gems” of comic opera, as it is nowadays,
are but poor stuff outside of their setting. Cinque-
valli (whose season approaches its close) and the
Drews are still strong attractions at the Tivoli.
* * * *
The “Mikado,” staged by the Railway
and Tramway Musical Society in Sydney last
Monday night, wasn’t, of course, the “Mikado”
at all; but in all other respects it was not so
deplorable as might have been expected. The
insult to Gilbert was greater than the odium cast
on Sullivan, but the Railway tram drowned
both insults every minute or two, and added
a slaughtered top note to its other victims.
The Railway institute is a tall, unknown building
situated somewhere near the cemetery that is to
become a station. The co. which appeared there
is not bad from the amateur standpoint; it
possesses one or two good voices, and every
person was word-perfect, which is better than it
sounds.
* * * *
“A Woman of Pleasure,” the new and
expensive spectacle at Melb. Royal, is a some-
what different development of lunacy in melo-
drama. The suffering heroine and her noble-
hearted Jack have been left out of the fiction,
and the only noticeably virtuous character is a
parson who walks around professing an earnest
Christian readiness to biff creation. As a matter
of fact, the shepherd seems incapable of raising
a biff when a wicked major smacks his reverend
nose with a loose pair of gloves. Providence,
however, preserves the smitten lamb from further
violence by sending a casual villager to attack the
majah from the rear, and two acts later that
typical military person is shot by Inman because
he wants to escape from a burning troopship in a
small boat reserved for women and children. This
is after the Majah has eloped with Inman’s
wife in a balloon, owing to natural love and affec-
tion for the lady, she having compelled Inman to
marry her by threatening (in the prologue) to
get him hanged if he didn’t. Otherwise, she
seems a well-meaning wife enough. Her character
depends a good deal on the Majah. When he is
too dead to elope with her in another balloon, she
returns to her lawful husband. The penitent
wears a pleasing winter costume, trimmed with
ermine, and the old baronial hall (for Inman is an
earl who kills people by accident as well as design)
glows with the glare of the blazing yule log, and
Anderson’s ballet sings a Xmas carol to show that
everybody is forgiven.
• * * •
The comic relief consists mainly of Miss
Georgie Leighford, as a stout lady in “rationals,”
and Bert Bailey as her brain softened offspring.
The female portion of the audience shrieks loudest
at the humor of Bert Bailey’s trousers. He retires
behind a piece of scenery for the purpose of having
his pants pulled off, and subsequently he reveals
glimpses of bare leg and shirt-tails to the shrieking
sisterhood. A successful run is assured for any
melodrama whose principal funny man will get
robbed of his under-vest. Probably “A Woman
of Pleasure ” will flourish on account of its scenery
and shirt-tails and other accessories. Anyway,
the omission of the virtuous heroine entitles
Anderson’s latest to all the flourish it can get.
** * *
“ Mice and Men,” introduced to Australia
at Melb. Princess’s last week, strikes one im-
mediately as being a woman’s attempt to write a
play on the lines of the “Sweet Nell” success. It
is lighter and slower in its movement and interest
than “ Nell.” The climax can be foreseen almost
from the start of the story, and there is a painful
lapse into tediousness in the fourth act. The
characters, without exception, lack the force to
compel much sympathy or dislike from the audi-
ence, nevertheless the figures are gracefully drawn
by Madeleine Ryley’s feminine hand, and the play
is a pretty poetical achievement, anyhow.
* • • »
The nearest approach to sinfulness in
“Mice and Men” is made by the giddy young
wife of middle-aged Goodlake, an English squire,
a cross between Squire Western and Peter
Teagle. Mistress Goodlake seems to have been
trying to seduce Captain George Lovell, the
nephew of her husband’s old bachelor friend,
Mark Embury, “scholar and philosopher.”
Mark Embury’s learning and philosophy are not
apparent, except on the programme. He makes
a close friend and confidant of Goodlake, who
is a noisy fool, and he talks of nothing but girl
and matrimony. After having arranged to get his
military nephew sent abroad, far from the endear-
ments of Goodlake’s wife, philosopher Embury
starts his own plan for arriving at connubial bliss.
He orders a batch of foundling girls to be sub-
mitted to him on the understanding that he is to
adopt one as his ward, and either marry her, when
his philosophy prompts him to the deed, or return
her undamaged to the care of the parish beadle.
When the batch of wenches is brought along, on
approval, by the beadle, the philosopher’s learned
eye picks out the best-looking wench —a 16-year-
old foundling, with a dimple in her cheek, and
hair that is comparatively long and curly, because
she never stood still whilst the matron was trying
to cut it short. The philosopher confers the name
of Peggy upon his dimpled experiment, and un-
wisely leaves her alone in his study for 10
minutes. Those 10 minutes blight his
future. The nephew, before going abroad,
climbs in at the window, and his brief
conversation with the adopted foundling,
leaves both parties “mashed’’ for evermore.
Peggy at the end of two years is so far frisky that
she goes to a masquerade ball unknown to her
hopeful guardian. The Captain goes there to meet
Peggy, and Mrs. Goodlake goes there to renew her
designs on the Captain. Also, old-man Goodlake
and Mark Embury, going there to bowl out Mrs.
Goodlake, happen to bowl out Peggy. Then the
philosopher becomes convinced that Peggy, who
has promised to marry him, would rather marry
the converted nephew instead. So he furnishes a
nice new honeymoon-house, and on the day before
the day when Peggy was to bestow herself and
her dimple on old Mark she learns that his
nephew is to be the man. Whereupon Peggy and
the nephew stroll into the house together, and are
heard singing to a harpsichord, whilst poor old
Guardian, in his becoming clothes, walks medita-
tively down the garden path like a philosopher
who has missed the last ’bus. Goatcher painted a
lovely scene for this closing act of renunciation.
• * * *
The minor details and padding of the thin
narrative are bright and decorative, otherwise
this practical country would have had little use
for “Mice and Men.” Even as it is the three
intervals of 10 minutes hang heavily. The work of
the Musgrove company is very good on the whole.
Harcourt Beatty, as the unwise, unconvincing
scholar, is a grave reminder of the same actor’s
King Charles. Sydney Stirling plays the part of
the Captain in a minor key, but with excellent
discretion. Miss Minnie Sadler is hard and effec-
tive as the wife who fain would go astray,
whilst Albert Gran (Goodlake) is the only
one of the principals to overact his character.
Alfred Tapping, Lawson Bailey, Miss Far-
leigh and others fill their minor places easily.
Miss Nellie Stewart fills the chief place easily,
except on the few occasions when she is required
to express deep emotion. Then she is compara-
tively weak. The heart-sore Peggy, asking her
elderly admirer to be kind enough to marry her,
doesn’t strike the true note of pathos, and conse-
quently the amphitheatre laughs. Not remark-
able, seeing that the pathos of the situation is
strained to the limit of theatrical art, nevertheless
there are actresses who could bring a lump into
the amphitheatre’s throat to stop that laugh. For
the rest of Nellie Stewart’s performance, it is a
new revelation of girlish charm surpassing her
daintiest work in “Sweet Nell.” She looks 16 in
the first act, and 18 afterwards, and the most win-
some of Peggies all the time. Present scribe can’t
imagine any other artist carrying out the same
contract in such satisfying style. Australia is
only now beginning to see Nellie Stewart at her
best.
#* * «
At Melbourne Opera House the main item
is Sandow, supported by Wilton Bros., Professor
Maccanncertina, Florrie Gallimore, and the Tom
Nawns, likewise others too numerous to mention.
The strong man’s first appearance in Melbourne
was a bigger success than some people expected.
Sandow runs his show briskly and impressively on
the silent system. Nothing breaks the silence,
save the dull thud of the Teuton professor’s bar-
bells when he drops ’em on his platform and
knocks splinters off the sides thereof. Sandow is
very strong. The constant strain of his mus-
cular condition probably accounts for a tired
expression on his handsome face. When he
draws in his breath and raises the furrowed glory
of his stomach to the region of his chest Sandow
has a waist that the boniest gii-1 might envy, and
when he strikes an ordinary ancient Greek atti-
tude with one shoulder and one calf in the fore-
ground he is surely the most beautiful figure of a
human being that ever gleamed in the limelight.
No part of the performance looks so matchless as
the Man himself, hence the managerial risk in
engaging Sandow. The bloated land-owners and
corpulent importers who usually book seats for
expensive shows are hardly likely to bring the
family along to consider the awful difference
between the average representative citizen and
Sandow.
* * * * '■?
Among the crowd of commentators,
medical and otherwise, who gathered at Sandow’s
private “ recital ”in Melbourne, the other after-
noon, veteran L. L. Smith was the keenest. An
old showman himself, and the son of a showman,
he couldn’t swallow the German professor’s theory
without a lump of salt. All fine muscular develop-
ment is directed by the brain, says Sandow.
Physical exercise is of little avail unless the mind
is bent on turning the work to the greatest
muscular advantage. Sandow having repeated
his familiar doctrine, little L. L. suggested that a
blacksmith develops great strength by the merest
mechanical labor. To which Sandow replied that
the average smith is not nearly as mighty as he
would be if he devoted thought to the making of
muscles. “Then,” asked L. L., “how do you
account for the muscles of a ballet-girl’s legs?
They come in the course of her training without
any assistance from her mind. Perhaps you don’t
know anything about ballet-girls’ legs ? ” Sandow
evidently wasn’t prepared to discuss the question
of a coryphee’s understandings, and L. L. didn’t
follow up his advantage. It goes without saying
that Sandow, with all his combinations of mental
and physical strain, has never developed certain
leg-muscles, which attain abnormal dimen,
sions in the woman who from childhood has been
taught to pirouette. If at the end of six months’
practice he were able to come down the stage on
his toes he would still be a comparative weakling
in that particular line of muscular exertion.
** * *
“ The Lady of Ostend,” which occurred to
the editor of London Punch when he felt about as
funny as the age of that illustrated paper will
permit, is making fair progress at Melb. Bijou.
The venerable mother-in-law joke was resurrected
in Burnand’s comedy, and the mother-in-law of
farcical domestic life sat so heavily on the spirit
of modern humor that King Edward VII. con-
ferred a knighthood on Burnand for his crime.
Fortunately, the old, old female brought a redeem-
ing circumstance with her. There is a father-in-
law who suits himself cheerfully to the droll cha-
racter of Oily Deering. The author didn’t count on
Oily for this part, but Deering gets there all the
same, and in his hands the stereotyped parent
with sexagenarian leanings towards vice becomes
a would-be-profligate of the best quality. Grogan
MacMahon, as the weary son-in-law, suffers from
a needlessly bald head. A faint pretence of a few
surviving hairs appears to be painted on Grogan’s
topmost nakedness, and to that extent his per-
formance is strictly original, but the effect doesn’t
offer a handsome apology for Grogan’s cheating
of the wig-maker. It only suggests that his head
would look quite indecent if he were to wet the
corner of his handkerchief and wash the painted
hairs away.
** # *
Her Majesty’s (Melb.) is closed just now.
On Saturday the house re-opens for the Melbourne
appearance of “Sherlock Holmes,” with Cyler
Hastings in the name-part. “ Sherlock ” has
been produced in obscure sections of the Common-
wealth, with our old friend Plimmer as the
apocryphal detective, but Mr. Hastings,
especially imported from ’Murka, is understood to
be the one and only Sherlock whom Providence
and J. C. Williamson have selected.
■ ♦
One might say of Max Schluter, that he seems to be a
first-class violinist performing on a very second-rate
violin. If the instrument is fine in tone, then the skilful
Danish artist doesn’t make the best use of it. Anyhow,
Musin of yore produced warmer effects with his violin,
though his platform personality was not so fetching as
Schluter’s.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Maoriland State Land Bank closed its
best year up to date (the seventh year of its existence) on
31st March, 1902. This office began with a large debit
balance, and acquired other debit balances later on, by
borrowing its capital in England at 3 per cent, and con-
sequently having to sell its loans considerably below their
face value. In this respect it struck a bad principle ; the
example of the Victorian and South Australian institu-
tions, which raised their money locally, and by paying a
slightly higher interest saved the big loss in discount, is
much more commendable. Still, the Maoriland institu-
tion is making reasonable progress at wiping off the
unlucky deficit:
Loss Loss on
on Loan Deficit.

These are cheering figures, when everything is taken into .
account. If the loss on flotation of loans is spread evenly
over the whole period covered by the loans, each year
being charged with its due proportion, then the Maoriland
State Land Bank can claim to have realised so far a net
profit of about £46,000, which is a very handsome amount.
Only £7208 interest and £1617 principal were overdue at
the date of the balance-sheet.
The management continues to be remarkably
cheap. The gross cost of management (which is much
greater than the net cost, as a good many items are
recovered from borrowers) has been as follows •

It is impossible to get these calculations down to mathe-
matical accuracy, inasmuch as occasional temporary
advances make the average capital difficult to arrive at.
Still they represent, even in their approximate state, a
remarkable evidence of economical working. The Maori-
land State Land Bank has now £2,366,700 out on mort-
gage, and its luck still holds good. Doubtless there will
come periods of loss and depression, but the institution
appears as well-fitted to cope with them as any other this
side of the line.

Many doubtful claims have been advanced on
behalf of duplex pumps. Some makers state that their
duplex pumps are “ double the capacity of single pumps
of same diameter and stroke.” An A. S. Cameron single-
acting pump can be run safely and comfortably at a speed
that would knock a duplex pump to pieces. But even at
a low speed, duplex pumps seldom attain even their
theoretical capacities, and the reason for this can be
readily explained. Apply to the Agents, Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, for
further particulars. The A. S. Cameron pumps are noted
for their simplicity, durability, and absence of outside
valve gear.*
* » • *
A revolution in smelting furnaces seems pro-
bable. The aid of gasoline has not been recognised.
Ernest de Beer, 439 Bourke-street, Melbourne, supplies
forms to suit assayers, which are marvellous in their
efficiency.*
» * *
The great cost of compressing air for rock
drilling, especially for small mines requiring to use only
two or three drills, has hitherto prevented mine managers
adopting machine drilling. The introduction of the
Gardner Electric Drill has removed this objection, small
mines can new be equipped with machine drills which
will do as much work as the air drills, and at the same
time provide the lighting of the surface and underground
workings at less cost than for air drills alone, while the
cost of operating them is less than one fifth of air drills,
doing similar work, and the necessary power being so
small oil engines can be employed. Usually, however, any
mine using machinery will have sufficient boiler power
available to spare the three or four horse power required
to operate a couple of electric machine drills, and so
reduce their working costs and save time. The Gardner
Electric Drill is in use on many mines, and a proved
succes s. The Austral Otis Company of South Melbourne,
who are the Australian agents, will be pleased to give all
information to anyone interested.*

The Government of New South* Wales have
entered into a contract for the manufacture, supply, and
delivery of 13 miles of Mephan Ferguson’s patent Lock-
ingbar or Rivetless steel pipes. These are to be 32 inches
diameter, in 25 feet lengths, and will be used for convey-
ing water from Nepean River to Prospect Reservoir. This
type of pipe has been used by the Government of South
Australia for water-supply purposes for some time past,
and has given great satisfaction ; also by the Govern-
ment of Western Australia for the Coolgardie water
scheme, for which about 320 miles of Lockingbar pipes
have been delivered.*

* * • •
The new find at Edjudina (W. A.) is attracting a
lot of attention. The reef is from 9ft. to 25ft. of solid
quartz carrying visible gold. About a dozen leases have
been pegged, and although no work has been done, Kal-
goorlie buyers are keen on the scent. Several options
have already been secured on behalf of English co.’s
which are burdened with wild cats. In addition to big
main reef there are several leaders, one of which is pro-
ducing splendid specimens. This is best surface show
discovered for a long time past, and high prices have been
paid for options. Edjudina is the district where the
Great Fingall was originally located, but the reef now
found is eight miles distant, and, although close to the
cart-track, has been overlooked till recently.

* * * •
Seeing that it is an understood thing now in
Australia that no State should be expected to do any
public works of the slightest importance, except out of
loans—what is it proposed should be done when loans
can’t be obtained ? A time must come when they can't be
obtained. The debt of the Commonwealth has gone up
in 20 years from £2B to nearly £6O per inhabitant, and
this country has hardly done more than make a beginning
yet at its necessary public works. There must come a
day when we will owe as much per inhabitant as the
British lender cares to trust us with. Then we must
either relapse into barbarism, build no more roads,
sewers, waterworks, bridges, telegraphs, railways, tram-
ways, &c., and let the old works gradually cave in and
perish—or we must find out that we can do all these
things without loans. We must find that we can do that
some day, unless Australia is going to be a failure among
nations. Why not find it out now?

The Modern Permanent Building and Invest-
ment Society (Melbourne), one of those large institutions
(its capital is £198,897 paid up, so it is bigger than a good
many banks) for which the southern city was famous in the
land-boom times, closed its 32nd year on 31st July, 1902.
It wasn’t a very good year, still it seems to mark a turning
point in the history of an institution whose luck, of late,
has been considerably out, and the turning point is in
the right direction:

This is the first time for a very long while that the earn-

The Modern Permanent Building and Invest-

One notable economy is that it insures its own pro-
perties against fire, and, after meeting all losses, it has
accumulated, in five years, an insurance fund of
£957. This is an idea that some other institutions might
follow with advantage. There is doubtless still a con-
siderable amount of depreciation to be provided for on
the Society’s £448,000 of propert3' and mortgages. Con-
sidering that the profit of £2384 which the business
realised last year would only have paid £1 ss. 2d. per
cent, on the £189,269 of capital that is alleged to be
intact it can hardly be otherwise. Still respectable pro-
gress is being made, though there is no prospect that the
big Society will get on an even keel again and become a
dividend-paying institution for a long time. It is pro-
posed to make another arrangement with the debenture-
holders under which they will receive 2s. 6d. in the £
next year, and Is. 3d. in the £ annually thereafter.
• * * *
Notice of two Bills dealing with the time-pay-
ment-furniture disease has been given in N.S.W. Assem-
bly. One provides that no woman may be imprisoned for
refusing to obey an order of the court to surrender the
time-payment “ sticks.” If her husband, or any other
male, is a party to the agreement, he may be imprisoned,
however, though the husband is not responsible unless he
is a party to the agreement. Why a man should go to
gaol under circumstances where a woman doesn’t go it is
ridiculously hard to imagine, and as a man’s time is
worth more, as a rule, than a woman’s, the idea is waste-
ful as well. The other Bill proposes to enact that if the
instalments fall in arrear the time-payment man can only
seize the goods upon refunding 90 per cent. (18s. in the
£) of the amount already paid on them. This is
calculated to do a lot of good in the case of the worst
variety of t.p. cormorant. Also, it may make him sell
better goods, or give shorter credit, or do something. If
a man buys £lOO worth of goods, and in the course of
18 months has paid £9O and then defaults, the seller,
under this system, would have to pay back £Bl to recover
the sticks. The £9 profit would give him 6 per cent per
annum on the nominal value of his goods (or more likely
12 per cent, on their real value) for the time they have
been out of his possession, and leave nothing for depre-
ciation. And the ordinary time-payment gim-cracks
perish so rapidly that they probably wouldn’t be worth
£3O after 18 months. Anyhow, as the time-payment man
probably only paid £4O or £5O for the goods when new,
he would shudder pretty hard at the idea of refunding
£Bl to get them back when they were old. Probably the
t.p. gentleman will devise some way of dodging the law-
supposing the Bill becomes law. If he doesn’t, the
destruction of the t.p. industry doesn’t matter much. It
leads to fraud, strife, bailiffs, unsettled habits, rickety
furniture, and many other evils.

The Economic Permanent Building Society
(Melbourne) has done much better this year so far as its
trading profit is concerned, but the loss on realisation of
property still continues :

The Economic has paid-up capital £42,640, so, after de-
ducting the deficit, there is £40,836 still nominally intact.
The gross income last year (£2,285) represented £4 6s. Id.,
and the net return (£1045) was equal to £1 19s. 4d. per
cent, on the book value of the investments. Also, the
profit of the business (if it hadn’t been swallowed up by
losses on property sales) would have been sufficient to pay
a 2 per cent, dividend and leave something over. Under
these circumstances, and considering the great improve-
ment in the earnings of the business, the position is very
far from hopeless. Good progress is being made at paying
off the liabilities, recent figures being :
OWING TO DEPOSITORS AND ALL OTHER CREDITORS.
1897 .. .. £23,542 1900 .. .. £20,779
1898 .. .. 22,822 1901 .. .. 16,356
1899 .. .. 22,380 1902 .. .. 12,374
If the earnings keep up to the present figure, or make a
further improvement, it might be worth while for the
Economic to simply pay off the rest of its liabilities
gradually out of profits, to write down its properties by-
degrees as profits admit, and to cease for a while forcing
sales on a depressed market, and to nurse the assets
instead. The position doesn’t look anything like bad
enough to call for any violent processes of realisation. If
the Economic could persuade its shareholders to put up
some preference capital and go into active business again
it would be better still. Australia is dotted over with
institutions of various degrees of solvency, which are
doing no new business, but paying off their depositors
and realising their assets by slow degress. Naturally,
they grow smaller and smaller, and in a very small in-
stitution the proportion of the earnings which is
swallowed up by working expenses is necessarily very
heavy.

Owing to Depositors and Other Creditors.

The Queen Building Society suffers from the same trouble
as many other convalescent institutions. With the sale
of properties and repayment of deposits it is becoming a
very small institution, and as a very small institution
requires an office, directorate, manager, auditor, gas-
meter, &c., &c., just like a large one, the expenses,
despite all economies, are apt to be disproportionately
large. The Queen’s charges appear to be low, still it
costs £578 in office-expenses alone to earn £2150. And
repairs, rates, survey fees, law charges, insurance, &c.,
are not included in office-expenses.
* • * *
There are great difficulties about arranging for
the purchase of one dilapidated institution by another,
for the buying institution always wants to take the assets
of the other at present bedrock value, and allow nothing
for its goodwill or its prospects. But if about six wrecks
could agree to share one office, and to have one manager,
directorate and auditor, with a view to ultimate amalga-
mation, a great deal might be done. When any one of
the six wrecks had any spare funds, they could be
devoted to buying up, in the open market, the deposits
of that wreck whose deposits were going most cheaply,
or in paying off whichever mortgage carried the highest
interest. By econoniy of management it would be
possible before very long, to buy up or extinguish
all outside liabilities without sacrificing the assets,
and any money the wrecks owed would then be to
each other. Then the assets could all be independently
valued and formed into one new institution —they would
still make a good large one—and to the shareholders in
each of the old institutions would be issued shares in the
new one corresponding with the net value of their assets.
Then the new institution could start active business with
good prospects. The saving by economy of management
would be great. A saving of interest could be effected by
having one bank account so that one institution’s tem-
porary credit balance could be set against another’s tem-
porary overdraft (the one institution simply paying
interest to the other). Also a good deal could be saved
by applying the joint funds to the extinction of the most
expensive mortgages or the purchase of the cheapest
deposits. The joint concerns could run their own fire
insurance fund. Any institution which had a reasonable
semblance of solvency about it would be qualified to join.
The principal difficulty is that, as most of the managers
and directors would lose their billets, they can’t be
reasonably expected to take any great interest in the idea.

The Castlemaine Brewery and Quinlan,
Gray and Co. (Brisbane) closed its year on 31st July with
its affairs on a new basis. During the last 12 months
it wrote off 12s 6d per share, equal to about£9o,ooo, or half
its capital. Also, the bulk of its reserves disappeared,
and a call of 2s. 6d. a share was made. These were heavy
disasters for the shareholders, who have received no divi-
dends for a long while, and are still receiving none, but
it was certainly best to face the losses. The recent
record has been as follows :
Added WAminol

About £90,000 capital written off.

The co., in refixing its affairs, followed a very common
and very objectionable system of accountancy. The
balance-sheet for 1901 showed £154,262 capital paid-up
and £13,928 reserves ; the one for 1902 simply shows
£73,047 paid-up capital and £1250 reserves brought
forward. There is no continuous account, or connecting
link, or exact statement of the amounts written off in
the interim, as there should be.
The Castlemaine Brewery and Quinlan, Gray,
and Co. still looks like a concern that has a good way to
go before it gets quite out of trouble, though a great deal
has certainly been done. The biggest liabilities have
been the overdrafts at the Q.N. Bank, and a large sum,
bearing 4 per cent, interest, owing to the people from
whom the present co. bought the big brewery. These
two liabilities have stood as follows :
1898 .. £107,691 1901 .. £74,409
1899 .. 104,762 1902 .. 50,391
1900 .. 90,970
The improvement is a very substantial one, but until the
remaining £50,391 is cleared off, and also £11,700 owing on
mortage, the shareholders have no right to expect much
in the way of dividends. Another point is that the
largest asset reads as “ Freehold properties, buildings,
machinery, plant, goodwill, &c., £88,907.” There was an
impression that the goodwill item was going to be wholly
written off. If this' was done the objectionable term
should disappear. If it wasn’t done, it should be done ;
and, meanwhile, the goodwill should be stated separately
to show what the paper asset amounts to.

The Australia Hotel Co. (Sydney) closed a
rather good year on 30th June, 1902, but good years only
come occasionally in the hotel business, which is precari-
ous at best. In 1901 the huge caravanserai only showed
a profit of £651, and there was no dividend. For 1902
the profit is £3709, which allows of a 10 per cent, dividend
and leaves a balance over. The Australia Hotel Co. has
a paid-up capital of £25,000 and £17,727 reserves. In its
balance-sheet “Lands, Buildings, and Machinery” are
valued at £150,000, and there is a £125,000 mortgage on
them, so the mortgage owns most of the place.
* * * *
South Australia takes a good large share of the
cake for pigmy municipalities. About half of the district
councils don’t collect more than £3OO a year in
rates. In the last financial year Kingscote raised £6O
(office expenses and other salaries, £32 !); Dudley, £B5
(office, &c., £35); and Dalrymple, £BB (office, &c., £55).
The local governing bodies want reforming quite as badly
as some of the State Govts.

a one to be called Excelsior is something like irony.
* * • *
A letter from Tarcoola (S.A.) goldfield : “ Our
storekeeper (there are five of them and two pubs, to a
population of scarce 200 souls) has posted on his door a
cutting from Bulletin, of two months back, concerning
prices, &c., at Ebagoolah (N.Q.). The comparison cuts
both ways. Tarcoola prices : Bread, 21b. loaf, 7d.; flour,
spuds and onions, 3d. per lb.—sometimes £d. more;
mutton, because the sheep can’t be got to an Ade-
laide market, is but 6d., bone and all; sundries,
such as a reel of thread, packet of needles, bar
of soap, all Is., which is also the price of a nobbier.
All tin stuff, meat or jam, runs into 14d. or 15d. per tin,
and salt is 3d. per lb. Miners’ wages are 10s. shift,
though there are such hybrid, cocky sort of wages as
Bs. 4d., Bs. 9d., 9s. 3d., 9s. 4d. paid also for different kinds
of labor. After paying his board the dissipated miner
doesn’t have a great lot to go on the tear with. When
the camels’ trains are late—and they’re that pretty regu-
larly—men are knocked off work for want of candles. In
the last three months nearly half of the men employed have
lost from a week to six weeks, ostensibly on account of
shortage of candles. The Block’s manager reports it
every other week apologetically, but when approached by
an enterprising party with an offer of locally-made candles
no business resulted.”
* * * •
S. Australia’s latest little boom is in compressed
fodder. The John Darling firm has put £12,000 into
plant at Gawler, 25 miles from Adelaide; and another
co., formed for the purpose, is spending nearly £20,000 a
few miles further along the north line. The low price of
wheat tells, of course ; but the increasing uncertainty of
the seasons has most to do with the wholesale cutting of
crops for hay. Farmers prefer to make sure of the little
they get from the fodder-merchants rather than risk total
failure by waiting for the grain to ripen. As to this
change of seasons, by the way, the State’s weather-office
prints an interesting table in its last annual report.
First the mean rainfall for the 14 years (1880-93) is ascer-
tained for every district; and then it is shown that in all
the districts, in all the years since 1893, the mean has
been only four times reached. In ’96, ’97 and ’99 no dis-
trict got up to the average, even though the average of
some districts—the Northern pastoral, for instance—is
under 9in.
* * * *
Broken Hill’s mind is fixed on having a State
something or other. It long sought a smelter to deal
with the product of the smaller mines ; but the Govt.
Geologist jumped on that proposal enthusiastically, and
pointed out that, if it didn’t pay the big co’s to smelt
locally, it certainly wouldn’t pay anyone else. That Mr.
Pittman was a good prophet was shown a little later by
the disastrous experience of S. Aus. in connection with
the St. Augusta plant, which, though it ran for only
four months, sucked the hard-up State of about £lO,OOO.
A recent request, into which the same officer has been
inquiring, is for an ore-dressing plant; and here there is
not room for such a vigorous curse. (1) It will not cost
so much to build. (2) Nor to run ; it can be kept on one
shift a day if there isn’t enough ore for more. (3) Every
man’s parcel can be treated separately, or nearly so. (4)
There wouldn’t be any need to lay in stocks of coke, ore,
and fluxes of varying value. (5) It would be able to treat
silver-lead, copper, or tin. The important point about
which there is a doubt is as to whether there is enough
ore suitable for concentrating, and available to keep a
plant going even one shift a day. If the officer is reason-
ably satisfied on that point, the plant should be provided.
Broken Hill is a for elderly men, capable
of doing some work, but not enough to satisfy the big
co’s ; and this plant would give them a chance to earn
bread and cheese from many little outside copper and
lead shows.
While he was up that way, the Govt. Geologist
should have reported on the Moorkaie phosphate pro-
perty, a few miles outside Broken Hill, which an Adelaide
co. is booming. There has been remarkably tall talk
about the deposit of rock phosphate alleged to be in the
claims ; “ the richest in the world ” is not the strongest
of the things officially said. Broken Hill, people (who
certainly don’t know much about artificial manures),
sniff at it, however, and locally it smells quite unlike the
smell that might be expected after reading the names of
the unimpeachable Adelaide directors.
• * * •
Even the lives of a cat must come to an end at
last; and the B.H. North Central will, when the end
comes, have the satisfaction of knowing that it cheated
the liquidators longer than most. Liquidators aren’t,
however, as a rule, called in until there is nothing left to
liquidate, and in this case there is a locked-up deposit,
which, with interest, still amounts to £1670. Of 134,299
issued shares no less than 97,677, forfeited for unpaid calls,
were sold in Sydney lately in one line for lid. a share.
It seems most likely that what the buyer was after
was the deposit and not the alleged mine. In that case
the grave is near at hand. The mine manager in report,
dated Aug. 8, recommends that “if the directors should
think it advisable to start work again,” a certain rise and
drive should be continued ; but as the rise and drive were
in material “of no value” the directors probably won't.
The man who paid lid. for the shares will get about 7d.
or Bd., even in these bad’times; so it wasn’t a bad little
spec.—that is, if things go as writer expects.
* * * *
A N.S.W. dredge has established a record.
The plant has been running for three weeks only, and the
return was so good as to show a substantial profit. The
first cheque drawn was for a dividend. It is a privately-
owned concern, and the owners don’t wish publicity given
to their good fortune. There may be sound sense in this
from a public point of view. If it were proclaimed where
the dredge is working, no doubt there would be flaming
prospectuses issued for dredging co.’s to work ground
alleged to be in the immediate vicinity of the successful
co., although it might be 16 miles away.
* * * »
Mining in N.S.W. has never before been at as
low an ebb as it has for the past 12 months, but there are
indications of an improvement. Several of the larger
gold mines appear likely to re-assert themselves. Baker’s
Creek, which has been under a heavy cloud, looks like
coming to the front again. The parent company has got
on to a fairly good run of stone. Proprietary company
has connected with its No. 13 level in high-grade ore.
Lode having been thus located in this property, there
should be no difficulty in picking it up in higher sections.
Mitchell’s Creek mine said to be showing up well, the
new reef being better in quality than the old one, and the
old one has been equal to 15dwt., consistently, for years.
Myall’s United bound to have some good returns when
the new lode is opened up. Neeld’s mine at Wyalong is
producing wonderfully rich sulphide from the bottom
level.
» * * •
Prince of Wales mine, Gundagai (N.S.W.), on
its last legs. It has been in possession of tributors for
some time past, but latterly they have failed to make it
pay, and as no development work was possible owing to
the lack of funds, there was no hope of the co. pulling
the mine out of the fire. It has been decided, perhap?
wisely, to close it down. In its early stages, when the
shallow formations were being worked, co. paid two oi
three divs.

SOCIETY
Mrs. de la Juveny, the Melbourne lady
who is prosecuting Parson Worthington, seems,
by her own account, to have had a good deal of
mystic fun for the money she says he got from her.
An interesting “exhibit” in the case is a metal
pyramid inscribed with symbols of Egyptian
worship and dedicated “ To the lodge of the Rosy
Cross, from Isis.” The lady says she took this
monument of hokus-pokus on the understanding
that she was to present it to a visionary Temple
of Isis when she was installed therein as higli-
Sriestess. The Mahatmas had seemingly directed
Irs. de la.Juveny to advance money in view of
her promotion. They sent her a pink parchment
bearing numerous instructions, such as: “Fly
swifter than Light. Be Still as Night. Do not
excite him. (Signed) Mooc, the two halls.” It
seems to have dawned upon the priestess-elect that
“ Mooc ” was a probable Egyptian equivalent for
Muck, and now she has excited the hokey-pokey
of the Egyptian temple by breaking her stillness
and taking out a warrant.
* » * •
There is something like a panic in the best
male circles in Auckland (M.L.) One night re-
cently a heavily-veiled lady, of tall, aristocratic
figure, stepped into a chemist’s shop and asked for
something that would remove a brute of a hus-
band. The flustered chemist said he was out of
prussic acid just then, but expected six or eight
kegs in next week ; if the lady was in a hurry she
had better get something at the ironmonger’s.
She thanked him in a low, musical voice and left.
As she. never came back he expects that she got
something at the ironmonger’s. And now no man
in Auckland who is married to a tall wife with a
graceful figure feels quite safe.
A WEARY WORLD.
The Lady of the Incongruous (who was paying
an afternoon call and was, as usual, reciting
her troubles): “/ have just heard from my
daughter, and she tells me she’s engaged to a
newspaper man, and now my hens is stopped
layin’—and eggs so dear, too—life is full of
cares l ”
Broken Hill, in addition to all its other
troubles, looks with daily - increasing gloom
on its water supply. Two years ago an
expert, rightly or wrongly, denounced the
water as unfit for human consumption, and
it is scantier now, and older, and has had
two years more dust blown into it. At the
bottom of any old-established dam there is liable
to be an ancient and fish-like layer of water—one
not to be lightly, disturbed or recklessly shaken up.
Broken Hill authorities still estimate that there is
six months supply on hand—that is to say if the
water is consumed to the bitter end. But then
who wants to be the last man—the one who drinks
the last jugful when everybody else has fled, and
then locks up Broken Hill, and stacks the key
in the backyard of the Council Chamber, and
abandons a dry lost town to the ghosts and goats ?
Broken Hill, in its alarm, asks the State to find a
supplementary water supply—there is a chance
that Private Enterprise may give out, and then
State Socialism is always fallen back upon. But
where the State is to get that extra supply from
just now it is hard to say.
N.S.W. Statistician Coghlan supplies some
curious information about the home or tent or
dug-out or bag-and-kerosene-tin hovel of this
young nation. It may be generally assumed as a
good sign when large stone or brick houses
predominate; when houses are mostly small
and built of wood, bark, tin, galvanised iron,
bags and slush, it suggests poverty and
discomfort and rudimentary habits. Tasmania
and Maoriland don’t separate wood houses
from metal in their returns, but, so far as
possible, here are the dwellings of the people :
Stone Wood, Lath Tin, Bark,
or and Canvas
Brick. Plaster. Slush, &c.

South Australia is the only State where the stone
or brick house predominates—how S.A. came
honestly by all these substantial habitations is un-
known. Queensland, Victoria, N.S.W., Tas-
mania, and M.L. are still in the wooden stage.
Westralia, still further back, is in the hessian and
bark and mud stage, and its habitation is still
open to the predatory goat and the unprincipled
’possum.
Westralia also crowds the largest proportion of
its population into one-roomed shanties —no less
than 28 per cent, of its dwellings are of the one-
roomed persuasion. Queensland has the next
worst record, tents being reckoned as one-roomed
houses ; Maoriland makes the next worst showing.
In N.S.W. and Victoria more than half the houses
contain from five to ten rooms each, but they are
the only States so situated. South Australia has
the largest proportion of uninhabited houses, with
Maoriland and Tasmania next. Taking all the
elements into consideration, they suggest that
Australia lives rather promiscuously as yet, and
doesn’t dwell in marble halls to any great extent
so far.

The Victorian courts have just granted
permission, asked for at considerable cost by two
barristers and two solicitors, to re-open (with
much costs) a will case which was before the courts
(also with a good deal of costs) in 1875, the will
itself having been made in 1864. A will has to be
pretty old before all hope of getting costs out of
it is lost.

Apropos the troubles, re-
cent and looming, in A us. rail-
way services. In Yic. it was
made clear that the engine-
drivers are the king-bolt, or
main driving rod, or whatever
the boss part of the machine is ;
and that recalls the policy of
J. H. Smith, who was S. Aus-
tralia’s one experiment in im-
ported railway managers.
Smith, who had a large capa-
city for underground engineer-
ing, recognised the drivers’
position ; so he aimed steadily
at getting them to look on
themselves as the aristocrats of
the service, not to lightly min-
gle with the rest in a common
trade organisation. The scheme
was working all right until it
was discovered that a little
more political pull would be
better for the railways (or the
politicians); and so Smith had
to go. He is manager at the
London end now for the Sul-
phide Corporation ; his engi-
neering friend, ex-Gov. Km-
tore, helped him to that.
At the Sydney Water Police Court, on
Wednesday, Deputy S.M. Donaldson fined James
Reid Heath £2 and costs, or a month, for allowing
a dog to worry a cat. The same day Ethel Field
was sentenced to seven days on a charge of wilfully
neglecting to provide adequate and proper food,
nursing, and attention for her nine-months’-old
child. The medical evidence showed :
Tne child was emaciated, extremities cold, and face
wore a pinched expression. Its clothes were damp and
partly wet. He weighed the child with its singlet on,
and it weighed from 171 b. to 201 b. It was a large-boned
child, and should have been much larger if it had been
properly looked after. In his opinion the condition of
the child was due to want of proper food and attention,
which would be likely to result in bodily suffering, and
would also result in injury to the child. The milk in the
bottle was very much the same as when Mrs. Day handed
it to him. He noticed that the child had a
discharge from the right ear, and inflammation
of one of the eyes. One of its hands was sodden,
and the superficial skin was peeling off. The mouth-
piece of a bottle the child had was in a very filthy
condition, whilst the ear, eye, and hand all needed
medical attention. Upon examining the bottle handed
to him by Mrs. Day he found there was a deposit of tea-
leaves in it, and the specific gravity of the milk was in-
sufficient. It was a solution of condensed milk, and very
weak at that. He had attended the child since, and it
was better now, but it would be some time before it
reached its normal condition.
It doesn’t follow that the cat was valued too high,
but by comparison the child was valued much too
low. In the erratic English table of weights and
measures yards makes one rod. It seems, also,
as if 4£ infants make one cat.

GLADYS (sobbing): “Ye — ye — yes. He saw pa,

Melbourne in general doesn’t know that
it is about to have a Commonwealth Exhibition
at the building in Carlton Gardens, under the
management of the veteran Joubert. All the
available space has been let, and when the forth-
coming show is fully advertised it will be quite as
good a shillingsworth to the local people as the
white-elephantine Centennial Exhibition of 14
years ago, which was opened at the wrong season
of the year, and ill-managed all the time. Since
then the big building has come to be identified
with several annual fStes and “carnivals” that
draw thousands of people through the turnstiles.
Also the State Parliament is a permanent side
show at the Exhibition, and the Aquarium has in-
creased its business by 100 per cent., or more.
Judged by the growing popularity of the adjoining
Aquarium and the Museum attached thereto, the
historic place where Australia’s fattest sons
scrambled for free drinks during the Jook-o’-York
period is now a much more promising pitch for
a three months’ exhibition than it was in 1888.

Anne-st. Presbyterian Church (Brisbane)
has sent a begging-letter to Carnegie, asking a
dole from his blood stained millions for its organ
fund. The church is said to be one of the richest
in Queensland. A church that has so little scruple
about how it gets money ought to be rich.

It was no great surprise to most that the
R.C. mission-vessel Eclipse came to sudden and
total grief at the Soloman group, t’other day.
When the reverend (but untrained) skipper,
Father Rouillac, brought her to Sydney from the
South Seas last year, with the help of a couple of
native “boys,” it was due to luck, and the watchful
eye of Providence. When about to leave Sydney
this year, the local marine authorities wouldn’t
allow the Eclipse to depart without a certified
pilot, and refused to consider the watchful eye
of Providence as a fair equivalent. But, as soon
as he got clear of their sphere of influence, Father
Rouillac gave the said authorities’ pilot his ticket
and continued his way alone. Shortly afterwards
he was tightly stranded, and, notwithstanding the
efforts of the opposition mission’s boat to get him
off, he stayed there till his vessel fell to pieces.
The watchful eye of Providence is an accident that
may happen once, but it seldom happens to the
same man twice.
How the population grows
older! In 1901 there were in
N.S.W. 6604 fewer people aged
under 5 years than there were in
1891. As a setoff there were
26,720 more people aged 60 and
upwards. This is the sorb of
thing that happens to every new
nation. It gradually swaps the
baby for the oldest inhabitant,
and the cradle for the set of
false teeth.

Australia’s share in the late war has
developed the soldier of fortune. Two swaddies,
who gained a certain amount of credit in the recent
campaign, left recently en route to Venezuela to
apply for a job in the local trouble. Promotion
is sometimes rapid in South American republics,
so they may possibly return in a year or so full-
blown Brigadier-Generals. On the other hand,
when the soldier of fortune applies to one party
for a job, and decides that the salary offered
isn’t good enough, that party always under-
stands that he is going to call on the other party
and see if it will offer better terms. Then a rock
accidentally falls on the applicant en route.
HER GRAVE.
[For The Bulletin.]
The flowers upon her grave scarce breathe,
So sweet a flower lies hid beneath ;
As if they feared their growth might stir
The sleepy earth that covers her.
R.O.
A CONFESSION OF FAITH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sight of the sunlit, open sea,
A full free breath of God’s fresh air—
To watch the white gulls circling free,
And muse upon the mystery
Of Nature’s wondrous harmony,
Is sport diviner far to me
Than nodding 'neath a pulpit where
A fat priest mumbles o’er a prayer.
I love the headland bold to climb,
Where showers of spray salt kisses fling ;
The cavern’s boom is mo re sublime
Than organ’s peal or clanging chime ;
More soothing than the matin rhyme
The song old Ocean croons to Time—
The chant the lifting rollers sing
All uncontrolled by priest or king.
Close by my side a full-lipped girl
Whose tingling touch can banish care ;
To watch with me the smoke-drift curl
Across the bay where sails unfurl;
Her joyous laugh as sea-birds whirl
And tides in rock-holes swish and swirl—
For me is music far more rare
Than fat priests mumbling o’er a prayer.
In churches dim let others pray,
When gold-threads in her wind-tossed hair
Flash like the jewelled sunlit bay;
When eyes, divinest blue or grey,
Reflect the Glory of To-day
Like lakelets glimpsed through arched way,
The world is welcome to my share
Of church, and priest, and mumbled prayer;
JOHN DRAYMAN.

The alleged Mexican immigrants, who are
also called Greeks, Argentines and Gypsies, and
look like descendants of Nebuchadnezzar, or the
original seed Aryans, after harrassing Customs
officers and police constables all over Victoria,
have at length reached Melbourne, and are now
camped in the yard of a Port Melb. hotel with
intent to depart from the Commonwealth by the
next German mail-boat. Writer inspected them
and their haberdashery and surroundings, and
wouldn’t give tuppence for the whole outfit,
Moreover, he wouldn’t sail on that German
steamer if his passage were paid, with a saloon re-
turn thrown in. The Gypsies possess two ragged-
looking prads, two waggons, and four large but
fearsome-looking tents that even a squad of Domain
loafers would disown, also one green parroquet.
That’s all that’s visible. The kiddies and one or
two women seem to wear little outside and less in,
and the men, black-haired, swarthy, with shifty eyes
and much silver jewellery, simply smoke the eter-
nal cigarette and dwell, vegetate, and occupy the
situation. Yet sundry Customs officers have seen
much gold in the possession of the unaccountable
wanderers, and they were represented as being
desirous of acquiring land and settling down. All
the real estate they have acquired they seem to
carry on their persons, for greater security.
* * • *
Bundaberg was one of the most enthusi-
astic pro-gore towns in Queensland during the late
war, and an attempt was made to boycott a local
merchant who was suspected of not being suffi-
ciently bloodthirsty in his opinions. But the
beer and fury have evaporated, and the old enthu-
siasm is as flat now as a billiard table in a dead
calm. On Coronation day, Bundaberg didn’t
celebrate worth 2d., nor did it illuminate, curse,
yell, or do anything extra. Even an order from
the local boss warrior that B company should
meet and fire an explosion only called together
eight tired-looking men, and the only thing that
exploded was the boss warrior himself.
THE BUMP OF DISAFFECTION.
PHRENOLOGIST: “Now , gentlemen, you will ob-
serve that the bump on this boy’s head is very
large, thus showing that the lad loves his
parents. Isn’t that so, my boy?”
BOY: “No”
PHRENOLOGIST: “What! you don’t love your
parents?”
B OY: “ Well, I like my mother, but not father.
That bump is where he hit me with a cricket-
stump.”
“ J.” tells a story which seems foolish
enough to be true: —
As the constables guarding the gate at the State Go-
vernor’s residence, Itose Bay, Sydney, cannot leave every
three hours to report to the ofhcer on duty at Paddington
(it being official etiquette to assume that the Governor is
so unpopular that he would be immediately killed if left
unguarded), the officer is obliged to walk to the bay—a
distance of about a mile and a-half—to hear the constable
say “Right, sergeant! ” Paddington, with 20,000 people
and 22 miles of streets, is now mostly guarded by two
constables—each one with his eye on 11 miles of street,
simultaneously. The rest are away collecting rolls or
guarding his Ex. And Dan O’Connor rose in the Assembly
the other night to ask the old, dull question : “ Where
are the police?” To which the short and reasonable
answer is : “ Trying to be in 178 places at once for about
£2 2s. a week.”

Melb. R.C. organs say some sweet things
about recent Orange celebrations. The Advocate
writes of a gathering of 3000 Orangemen in Melb.

and “ Orange cannaille ” (with two n’s). Vale,

ove as “a pestiferous Orange blowfly.”
Dear Bulletin. —The King’s dinners to
half a million London poor were, as usual, made
the means of an advt. for several gangs of society
busy bodies. Only in the West End sections,
where princesses and other silvertails visited, was
there any real attempt at a meal, and the music
promised for the diners was not, except at the West
End sections aforesaid. Out of the 30 dining-
places in the East End only five had music, and
that provided by some of the worst amateurs on
earth ; but at the dining-places at Covent Garden
the pick of the profession sang—not to the ragged
diners but to the Princelet of Wales. In the East
End the food was uneatable, and various stewards
and parsons, writing to the Telegraph and other
London dailies, said that the dinners, contracted
for at over Is. per head, were not worth three-
pence. Certain boxes of chocolate were provided
for the children of the poor, but in hundreds of
cases these were seized by the silvertails as
souvenirs. It was all mean and petty—but, then,
everything is in this country.— Australian, 15
Smell-street, Dreary Town, Fogland.

- The latest war yarn in Maoriland is to the
effect that there were drunks, fights, disorders,
and ructions at Newcastle (S’Africa) between
Australians, Canadians, and (principally) Maori -
landers on one side, and English soldiers on the
other; and that two Tommies were killed by the
“colonials” and many injured. It is variously
stated that the row began through drink, through
general ill-feeling, and through a small mutiny on
ttie part of the Maorilanders, who objected to
three of their comrades being locked up for
chasing a Kaffir girl. Anyhow, about two dozen
officers and men state that there was a drunken
and devastating shindy of some kind, while the
officer in charge naturally says there wasn’t, and
Treasurer Joseph Ward—Joseph the Oily—is
shocked that such dreadful statements should be
circulated,

Latest “stone-throwing mystery ” at Bal-
larat (Vic.) didn’t remain a mystery long. Even
the police know enough now to hunt up the
likeliest girl between 10 and 20, on or near the
premises, in such cases. In this instance there
was a departure from the usual motiveless folly of
these enterprises. Some £fio wortli of jewellery
was stolen by the servant-girl of the house, who
invented a tale of burglary, and then established
a theory that the burglar habitually came back
and threw stones at the house to divert suspicion.
It is only simple justice to enfranchised woman to
admit, once and for all, that it is a libel to say she
can’t throw a stone. She has, in this country at
least, mastered the art of throwing stones on her
own household roof with an accuracy and secresy
that are worthy of all commendation.
The common report is that
N.S.W. Govt, is letting the old,
dirty, dilapidated wharves which
it resumed at Darling Harbor, to
the previous occupants in their
old, dirty, dilapidated state —that
the improvements are at a stand-
still, the new streets put off inde-
finitely, the great Owe’Sullivan
doss-houses postponed or defunct,
the foreshore railway hung up,
and the bubonic rat back carrying
on business at the old address.
All these things are said to have
happened, or to be happening,
for want of money, which appears
probable enough. The resumed
property is only about half paid
for yet. Even after the recent
£3,000,000 loan the loan-account
is overdrawn another £3,800,000
or so. The property-holders are
being offered 4 per cent. Govern-
ment securities if they will agree
not to press their claims. The
rents of the resumed properties
have been spent for the ordinary
purposes of Government. It is
persistently rumored that the re-
cent sudden spasm of retrench-
ment only came about when
London financial authorities had
been sounded about another big
loan, and had reported that the
time was inopportune.
If N.S.W. annually raised a
million or two of revenue for
public-works purposes it wouldn’t
need to care how the London
market felt, but as it never at-
tempts such a shocking innova-
tion, the resumed area appears
likely to settle down, pending
some more very big loans, into
the same old dirty, neglected, and dangerous bit
? f Property that it always has been. The sorrow
is that its dirt, rats, dilapidation, and untidiness
w ;n be set down to the credit of State Socialism,
whereas they should really go down to the credit
of the awful policy of dependence on loans.

when Melbourne Age, in the same issue in which it

The “Newspaper Train” Select Com-
mittee of N.S.W. Parliament’s proceedings grew
suddenly hilarious last week when Mr. Arthur
Griffith cross-examined Mr. Sam Cook, of S.M.
Herald:—
Is the Daily Telegraph published in Sydney ?—I
believe it is.
How long have you believed that ? —I don’t know that
it has been a matter of belief. It has been a matter of
knowledge.
Was it not a practice for you to ask members of your
staff whether the Daily Telegraph was a paper published
in Sydney ? —I don’t remember that I ever did anything of
the kind.
You don’t remember treating it with lofty contempt ?
I don’t think so, either lofty or low.

Dr. Shields focussed the Drayton Grange
tragedy to a point when he swore, before the
Royal Commission :
One of the primary causes of sickness was the intro-
duction of infectious diseases from the shore. Proper
inspection could have prevented this, but one doctor
looking at the tongues and feeling the pulses of 300 men
at one parade, lasting half-an-hour, was not sufficient.
Measles were so prevalent amongst units of the First
Battalion that it was at one Lime proposed to take the
men ashore and camp for a couple of months.
Lack of discipline among the men, lack of
efficiency among the officers, helped to surround
the tragedy with sordid and squalid details, but
the cause of it was that the Imperial transport
authorities sent away an overcrowded transport
knowing that it had measles on board. With even
greater callousness, the same authorities sent
another batch of done-with Australians in a boat
which had smallpox on board. The Fates on that
occasion were kind, and there were not some
scores of deaths ; but that wasn’t the fault of the
“good old War Office.”
* * * *
There should be a mighty upheaval in the
Domain, and on the G.P.O. steps, and in many
regions of the out-back. Slater’s Inquiry Agency
now advertises freely in London that, in view of
present bad times in Australia, it “is prepared to
pay the passage home for any friend or relative
who has been induced to leave Great Britain, and
has been unsuccessful.” Slater is the mischief-
making person who has a standing advt. suggest-
ing that wives and husbands should let him find
out “whether the club is responsible for late
hours, and if shopping alone occupies so much
time.” Where his profit comes in this new game
is hard to see, but there is his offer, to finance all
the wandering sheep who have been packed off to
Australia and want to get back.

Re the Legitimising Bill now before
N.9.W. Parliament:
Here in Maoriland such an enactment has been in force
since 1898 and has been extensively taken advantage of.
The Act simply enables a man to legitimise any children
of his born before his marriage to their mother, and thus
secure to them the same rights as if born in wedlock. It
will surprise many to know that this humane and reason-
able law was in force in the Papal States before the
abolition of the Pope’s temporal power. In Maoriland we
have got rid of many of the mediaeval barbarities in respect
of illegitimates. Thus in the matter of inheriting
property, illegitimates have all the rights of legitimates,
providing none of the latter stand in the way. Also,
under our Employers Liability and Deaths by Accident
Compensation Acts, an illegitimate or the parent of one
can recover compensation; though, curiously enough,
there is no corresponding provision in our recent
Workers’ Compensation Act.
