* • * •
In regard to the shearing trouble, Mr. F.
Jago Smith, a pastoralist, writes to the N.S.W.
daily press :
I think that a reasonable arrangement would be for
hand shearing to be paid for at 225. 6d. per 100, and
machine shearing at 20s. The question of union men
shearing with non-union men to be sunk until settled by
the Arbitration Court. And to those pastoralists who do
not like giving an increased price for hand shearing, I
would point out that sheep certainly do cut 50 per cent,
more wool than when the price was 17s. 6d. And also
that most of the sheds which would come under the
225. 6d. rate are small ones—which, of course, are not so
profitable for the shearers—and therefore a better price
than at the large sheds is justifiable so far as they are
concerned.
This from a well-known wool-grower seems to be
a very strong argument for the justice of the
shearers’claim for a “rise.” If the shearer was
paid 17s. fid. for cutting 51bs. of wool, it is surely
not unreasonable that he should ask 225. fid. for
cutting lOlbs. of wool, i.e., for practically double
the work.
• * * *
The attempt, by a section of parsondom,
to make a martyr of sorter Sykes, who refused to
do Sunday-duty in Melb. G.P.0., failed lament-
ably. Sykes was ‘ ‘suspended,” and three reverends
waited on P.M.G. Drake to get special considera-
tion for him, as a “conscientious objector.” They
were informed that Sykes had, on a previous occa-
sion, done Sunday work and taken extra payment
for it. The parsons replied that he had done so,
was sorry for it, had given the money to charity,
and wasn’t going to do it again. Further inquiry
showed that, on the occasion in question, Sykes
had been offered a substitute, but that he declined,
and voluntarily did his personal breakage of the
Sabbath. Also, that Sykes had given half, not
all, the money to some charitable institution. The
parsons then retired from the negotiation, and
Sykes was told that he could either stay in Mel-
bourne, and do his turn of Sunday work, or go to
some country place, where his conscience wouldn’t
be lacerated.
COMMON.
“ And do you really believe, Mr. Jumtup, that
we are the descendants of baboons?”
“ Well, I wouldn’t swear to that, but I feel sure
that we have a common ancestor .”
“ F.C.” remarks that some men become
easily communicative :
A clerkly-looking individual, with a hand-bag 1 , strolled
into our back-yard, t’other noon, where the little wife
was hanging out clothes. “ Good morning ! I’m getting
particulars for next year’s directory. What is your name,
please?” “Sarah Croson.” “Thanks. There’s no Mr.
Oroson, is there ?” The little woman winked at daughter
Linda, on the doorstep, and replied, “ No ! I’m all on my
own.” “Ah ! (he put his bag down) So am I,—now.
(Pause.) Good wives are hard to get—and when they get
a decent husband they don’t know how to appreciate
him.” He was started by now and went ahead full sail.
“ I’ve been married 13 years and was never out of a billet
till six months ago, and because I was out a month my
wife left me.” “ Dear me. That’s bad. Any children ?”
“ Yes, that’s the worst of it. We had two, bub she took
them with her. Now that I’ve got a job she wants to
comeback.” “ And I suppose you’re glad to get her?”
“ Indeed, no. I won’t have her back. I’ve offered to
take the boy, and I’m looking out for a smart house-
keeper. I wouldn’t mind her having one or, perhaps, two
children,” and he glanced at kiddy Linda. Just at this
interesting stage I came home to dinner, and Linda sang
out “Hullo, father!” whereupon the confidential grass-
widower cast a reproachful look at the little woman, and
left without asking any further particulars for the
directory.

Cable item from Capetown, to which
hundreds of Australians are now making their
way with a vague idea that there is a Promised
Land thereabouts :
Crowds of immigrants at Capetown are unable to get
work. The labor markets are glutted and the parks are
filled with penniless “ dossers.”
It is bad enough to doss in a familiar park,
where you know the holes and the sheltered spots;
but to go looking in the dark for a hole in a strange
park is Gehenna.
* • * *
Management of Melb. old Trades’ Hall has
a nice Philistinic sense of the fitness of things.
For years past, the old building has been one of
the most interesting sights of Melb. A big,
wooden, barn-like structure, it was the home of
the Labor movement of Victoria —almost of Aus-
tralia. There it was that the most bitter battles
of labor were fought and won, and on all the
beams of the open roof, browned in harmonising
tints by generations of democratic pipes, showed
faintly the early attempts at decoration, each
piece of timber being adorned with some motto,
such as “The laborer is worthy of his hire,” or
“Muzzle not the ox that treadeth the corn.” But
a few weeks back, it seems to have struck some
genius that the ’all didn’t look half as neat as it
should, so painters were called in. The whole of
the historical marks were swept away, and the
interior colored a lovely pink and blue, like an
advt. for somebody’s dyes. The hall was a land-
mark in the history of Australian unionism.
It is a vidgar and meretricious endeavor to copy a
tenth-rate private bar.

FACILE PRINCEPS.
SEMPER IDEM.
BUCHANAN WHISKY.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 9, 1902.
My Dear Arini.—
A record attendance at A. J.C. Spring meeting.
The tramway service was disorganised on Satur-
day through a car running off the line, and
crowds of people had to walk from Moore Park.
All the dailies compliment Mr. Clibborn upon
“the smoothness with which his arrangements
worked.” Yet I was quite sure I saw an almost
hysterical man in the office at one of the entrances,
telling the clamorous crowd around the window
that the tickets had run out, and that he had
no one to leave in charge whilst he went to get
another batch. Some Americans were among
the waiting group, and they exchanged glances
and smiles, and the Australians standing round
blushed (for the club) like a December sunrise.
The poor ticket-seller presently induced a big
policeman to mind his stall whilst he went to
get more tickets. But perhaps it was all hallu-
cination. On Metropolitan day the supply of
racing books also ran out, but the tea stayed on
tap. The demand for the fearsome A. J.C. brew
never exceeds the supply.
Melbourne visitors took up a big share of stand
and lawn ; they are disgusted that there are no
society evening functions during race week, Two
real Somebodies brought over four new evening
dresses each, only to learn that there would be no
occasion for wearing them.
Beautiful dressing abroad on Metropolitan day.
Money, taste, a sky of cobalt-blue, a temperature
of 65, a big race meeting —of course there was
beautiful dressing; crepey, softly silky, lacey and
chiffony as to material ; and striking every tone
and semi-tone in the chromatic scale. A
majority of the gowns were tucked horizontally
from neck to skirt. Hats, flat and flowery,
drooped over the eyebrows, with a cascade of
velvet loops or chiffon tails, or flowers, or some-
thing indicative of seed time and harvest
falling over the back hair. One woman had
several strings of poppy pods, another had trails
of hops, and yet another many ears of green
barley. The damsel who, in ‘San Toy,’ stands
round as Captain Ko-Fan in gold clothes and
a helmet whioh is part long-handled fly-switch
and part inverted funnel, gave the country people
a treat, for she must have had 100 yards of tucked
and flounced and floating and trained blue
mousseline de soie in her excessively expensive and
lovely gown.
The needlework on many of the frocks gave one
pause to think pityingly of the makers. An
elaborate design of, say, peacocks’ tails and
hibiscus blossoms cut from expensive lace, string-
colored for choice, and this inlaid (“ appliqued,”
if you like the word better) with a jewelled edge
on to a background of something which looks
about as substantial as a cloud of cigarette smoke.
You call this a motif— and wonder about the bill.
A few early Victorian pelerines, trimmed with
rose-petals, were abroad. Lady Hutton wore one.
A daughter-in-law of the late W. B. Dailey was
one of the best-dressed : a pink soyeuse inlet with
the new Paris color-insertion—a Suzanne
costume. A well-known brewing sooiety lady
in billows of biscuit-colored Saint d’esprit,
inlaid with medallions of lace (plenty
of motif) was another Suzanner. What a
lot of women one sees at races dressed like
debutantes, but aged about 50 or so, with gold
toupees and carmined and whitened faces, the
skin on which looks as if it had been done up in
curl-papers, so creased is it. A tiny girl of about
three could hardly be distinguished from her
mother’s closed sunshade, both were such bundles
of lace and chiffon. She lay down on the lawn
and slept, and a sister, some years older, with
stork’s legs, steeplechased to and fro over her
what time Bay Eagle was flying over his jumps.
Bay Eagle was the cause of a truly ugly little
scene which occurred close to me. A girl of about
nine, with a sulky underlip and sombre eyes, was
standing on a seat at the back of the stand intently
watching the race. Her mother stood at her back
peering over the child’s shoulder. As the horse’s
win seemed assured the little girl showed strong
feeling of an angry description, and when he shot
past the post she swung round swiftly, caught her
mother by the latter’s transparent yoke with the
left hand and viciously punched her face and neck
with her sweet right fist, exclaiming, “Didn’t I
want ss. to put on that horse—it’s your fault I
haven’t won; why didn’t you give me that 55.?”
That mother has a rod getting nicely pickled for
herself in that child.
They are telling this story of a planter and a
girl. He was a German Hebrew, who grew dates
in Fiji or grapes in Iceland, and visited Sydney
lately, and was much in the company of a dis-
tinguished musician, whereby he secured what
Mark Twain, calls a “halloaing acquaintance”
with persons whom otherwise he might have only
known by sight. The musician had two Sydney
ladies to dinner one evening at his hotel, and the
planter was a co-host. One of the guests is the
daughter of a deceased scientist whose mono-
graphs are of European value, a good linguist and
musician, pretty and prettily dressed, and—a
governess. To her the planter was extremely
courteous. Later, the fact of her being a governess
was made known to him, and the planter man
was shocked. “In my country we do not speak
nice to a governess,” he said. Of course the girl
was told; and she’said, “Do you mean to say
that I dined at the same table as a planter? Why,
in my country, we do not speak at all, let alone
‘nice,’ to an agricultural laborer.”
Marguerite King, the daughter of a former
Soustralian Minister for Education, has taken her
degree at the Royal Academy of Music, London,
in the quickest time, save one, on record.
She is a very pretty girl, and the British
Australasian has a portrait of her. Her
mother, who teaches music in Adelaide, is very
like the pictures of Mary, Queen of Scots—before
Elizabeth got hold of her. Miss King is a niece
of local mining-man J. Wliear Roberts.
Longstaff wants to run out from the painting of
Royal portraits for a spell among his native wattle,
he says. Perhaps he may manage it next year.
He has just finished a portrait of Mrs. J. H.
Scantlebury. . Mr. Jack Scantlebury has now de-
cided to stay in London, where he has taken a
mirror-plated, gilt-edged, velvet-cushioned office,
said on more or less reliable authority to be.
papered with bank-notes. Mr. D. K. Inglis,
another Sydney mining man, who left here a year
ago, is about to return for climatic reasons—
English winters do not suit him.
His Ex. State-Gov. Rawson with his wife and
daughter will go for a trip out of Sydney on the
17th. At Germanton the Gov. will open his first
railway; after that a visit to Table-top Station
and Albury. To be hoped the route is not quite a
dust-pan strewn with bleaching bones. As the
nature of democracy abhors a vacuum at Govt.
House, our own Ex.’s place in Sydney will be
taken by the Tennysons from Wednesday till Fri-
day. It is hoped in Federal circles that this short
stay will avert the gory revolution which is
understood to be brewing in Sydney. The
Tennysons will attend Max Schluter’s concert
on Wednesday. The Danish violinist had a great
reception in Melbourne; the critics were flatter-
ing, and State-Gov. Clarke entertained him twice
at Sunday musical evenings. Melbourne is now
subject to attacks of sub-acute mania of admiration
for Miss “Stonington” Clarke. Her naturalness,
camaraderie and frank inclination for the society
of litterateurs, journalists and musicians, and the
independence with which she declines to wear
always the vice-regal mask and domino, have gob
her a host of admirers amongst people who usually
look down on Govt. House. But we’ve got a fine
girl of our own to barrack for at Cranbrook. Since
an embargo was put on the titled British aris-
tocracy for State governorships (by the reduction
of screws) vice-regal worth has risen considerably.
The experiment might be tried on the big Federal
post too.
The very idea of sovereignty in its most con-
densed form was that “Will my people ever
forgive me ?” whioh was cabled out as having been
spoken by the almost-dying King when he came to
after the operation ; yet, as Sir Frederick Treves
and the other attendant physicians have not hall-
marked it, and the London dailies have since told
us that “ the King asked for George ” directly he
recovered from the anaesthetic, one is forced to the
conclusion that the phrase was merely a caterwaul
of the Press Agency. Pity it isn’t true, for it was
a memorable phrase.
Marriage of Lord Beauchamp sets one specu-
lating on possibilities. If several “ifs” had or
hadn’t if-ed, we have still had him as
Governor, and, with him now Lady Lettice to lead
the fashion in “picturesque dressing.” London
papers credit her with that, and with strong
religious and musical leanings. In the two latter
tendencies she is in perfect sympathy with her
husband. _ Judging by the lady’s portrait she
believes in what Huneker calls “the eternal
charm of fluffiness,” and she probably moves “in
a large, curved way.” Earl Beauchamp is a rich
man, but his bride has a fortune of £160,000. You
can buy the very best of fluff and picture hats on
the interest of that. Lady Lettice’s sister, who
married Lord Shaftesbury, was with the Hope-
touns, you remember, and had a like fortune. As
Li says in “San Toy,” some boys have all the
luck.
Wedding of Miss Queenie Levien, only daughter
of Lawyer R. H. Levien, to Mr. E. K. Tully, a
solicitor of Brisbane, took a nice, pretty, and
popular girl out of the legion of the local un-
attached last week.
A man called on me recently and, with great
fervor, asked me out to afternoon tea, and begged
me to put a paragraph in the paper stating that
he had got his divorce, and not to forget the
lawyer-man who helped him to break his chains,
“because there were heaps more of his mates
who might want the same good turn done.”
With tears of emotion in his eyes, he pressed the
offer of tea or any other drink upon me, and
asked me to rejoice that he was free once more.
And why not ? The public is informed when a
man gets married, and his friends are asked to
sympathise with his happiness. And lots of
people are more anxious to be unmarried than
they were to get married. What reasons against
divorce receptions, breakfasts, flowers, and con-
gratulatory speeches ?
Mr. Goring - Thomas’ concert duly came off
before a large audience on Wednesday, the 3rd.
Three or four people of more than usual ability
appeared. Miss Daisy Thomas, a light soprano
with a clear, bright voioe, did some old Italian
and the legend of the fountains from ‘ ‘ Lucia ”
with plenty of spirit and nerve. A Mr. Arthur
Pratt, among the men, with a fine, ringing bari-
tone voice, carried off the honors with a “Tra-
viata ” air and something from “Elijah.” Miss
Corbett, after a little more work, ought to do
something considerable with her big soprano voice.
She was encored for the grand aria “Roberto, tu
che adoro.”
A roar as of a crowd that had backed the
winner greeted Dorothy Yane (Mrs. Menzies)
when she appeared at the Tivoli, Sydney, in aid
of the Mt. Kembla fund, on Saturday. In her
first song,_ “ A Monkey on a Stick,” she wore her
pretty Geisha dress ; for the second she was in a
sort of Charity-girl costume of brown tunic and
stockings. Her tuneful entreaties to pay, pay,
pay, backed by her baby face and blue eye 3,
brought a shower of money, equal to £95 in two
nights, which, with her salary of £5O, will go to
the charity. She closes her meteoric season on
Friday night.

general hospital that an appeal has been made to
women financiers of other charitable institutions.
A strong working committee, with popular Mrs.

systematic canvas for a penny fund is to be tried.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The Town Hall tea, with its speeches, in aid
■of Miss Sutherland’s homes for neglected children,
was worth attention, as usual. It might almost con-
vert a miser to practical philanthropy to see all
those practically ‘mitherless bairns,’ many of
them in arms, behaving on a public platform like
wise old warriors and singing like a choir of well-
drilled linnets. Poor kiddies, it was past their
bed-time, but not a whine of impatience escaped
even the smallest and baldest of the lot. Before
Miss Sutherland got them many had been schooled
to endurance. Patient children! Think what
pain makes a young child patient! One tiny
•chap proudly clapped the vocal efforts of himself
and mates. ‘We mustn’t tap,’ remonstrated
little ginger-pate at his elbow. The self-applauder
was palpably proud of his mannish trouser-
pockets. A lady stole near to gently stroke his
thin, white cheek. ‘ These ain’t your pants ! ’
said the owner defiantly, thrusting his paws
deeper into his paradise of pockets. Just now
Miss Sutherland has 10 babies in her room
o’ nights, and it costs her an almost impossibly
small sum to clothe her family of 150. Gov.
Clarke spoke kindly at the gathering, while his
wife smiled and nodded at each individual baby,
and tried to be nice to pre-occupied Miss Suther-
land, who, in her care for the kiddies, forgot to
respond.
“ Thanks to the fashionable interests and
acumen of Pastor O’Connell, of Carlton, his
flock’s Gypsy Encampment started off on Satur-
day at the Exhibition, under the smiling auspices
of some thousand attendants and visitors. Gov.
Clarke was at the starting-gate, and touched the
spring in quite a tactful and experienced fashion.
As he sat down, after a lofty tribute to the energy
of the R.O. community, his better half caught his
eye, nodded approval, and smiled her pleasure.
Archbishop Carr gave plenty of compliments in
return, and talked of the unfailing amiability of
the Gov., his ‘good lady’ and his daughter, and
all that. Then the platform party buttered one
another up—and butter is so dear these drought-
stricken days.
“The Gypsies that are encamped at the Exhi-
bition just now, fleecing the public’s pockets, are
not a strikingly beautiful lot. Most of the pretty
girls seem to be on strike with the A.W.U. this
shearing season. As a matter of fact, bazaars
grow out of fashion, and it becomes increasingly
difficult to run smart girls in for stall-holding.
On Saturday Mrs. Chadwick was the most frocky
of the fashionables, in blotting-paper pink and
modish black, and Lady M'Eacharn showed her-
self the most substantially helpful to the solicit-
ing Gypsies. Capt. Vallange, with haughty mien,
followed their Exes., and dipped into his pocket
whenever Lady Clarke called for cash.
“ There was very little flutter among Melbourne
Hospital patrons and the usual smart committee
for the Castles’ sing-song in aid of that institution.
At the preliminary meeting, held in the Town
Hall, last week, the long-maned Scherek took the
chair, and Lady Williams, Miss Violet Clarke,
and two or three others strolled in, looked round
at the Sahara of vacant chairs, took bundles of
tickets, and wandered out again. It was a most
unattended meeting. People now buzz of nothing
but Melba.
“Mrs. Sandow’s diamonds are as much talked
about this week, in Melb. as hubby’s marvellous
muscles. The sight of those rising ridges on the
glowing pink skin of the modern Hercules is
almost sickening to the feminine eye. It is ab-
normal, extraordinary, queer. The hard waves
of flesh slowly heave up, and the light-headed in
the audience grow dizzy. But the charming Mrs.
&andow and her marvellous diamonds inspire
green envy. The muscle king and his dainty
spouse are an extraordinary couple.
“All the champion she-golfers of this State
met in contest at Geelong the other day, at the
biggest link tournament of the season. Out of
about forty crack players six started scratch.
A local player, Mrs. Harry de Little, won ; the
former Australian champion, Junoesque Mrs. C.
Fairbairn, only figuring as the sixth on the list.
“Nellie Stewart has never looked more quaint
and winsome than in the garden scene of ‘ Men
and Mice.’ A long lavender brocaded cloak (of
Puritan build and simplicity), a shovel bonnet
(like an inverted flower-basket), and a set of
chocolate-colored ringlets, give the sweet Peg of
this piece a delightfully piquant loveliness, far
exceeding her rakish brillianoy in Sweet Nell.
“Nellie Stewart tootles a love strain in ‘Men
and Mice’ as she squats on a chair, back to the
audience, to draw on her shoes and stockings.
The sight of the pink-tipped toes that she dis-
plays for about five minutes disturbs the peace
and calm of the onlookers (mainly male), but as
she proceeds to cover the bare legs in the orthodox
fashion, the women get excited. All glasses are
glued on the provoking back. Only those at the
side can see the performance. Nellie wears no
garters. She gives the top of the hose a cunning
twist instead.
“Rumor saith that King Ned has ordered a
recent portrait of Mrs. Maesmore Morris to be

“An account of the petulant conduct of the
wife of .an Australian personage in London at
Coronation time has just got about. The lady in
question put on so much unnecessary frill at the
hotel in which she and her husband were lodged,
and was so highly colored as to complexion and
plumage, that she was presently known throughout
the house as Aurora Australis. One evening,
dressed in a tremendous new gown, and apparently
anxious to have the hotel know of it, she swept
about the rooms and the corridors and perched out
on the doorstep till everybody within sight was
on the broad grin. Then, discovering signs of
derision, the lady took a commanding position on
the stairs, and expressed her opinions loudly and
publicly, till the management rushed for her hus-
band and prevailed on him to remove her, bag and
baggage, with the accent on the baggage.
“ The preparations for a very-gilded marriage
are temporarily suspended, pending settlement of
the claims of a rejected and dejected country lass,
who considers that she has a prior claim to the
bridegroom, and, it is whispered, wants several
thousands as balm for her wounded heart.
“We have no entertaining consuls at present
in this State. Once the De Jardines threw open
their ornate residence on French anniversary
days, and At-Homed the public, but they have
departed, and their successor, the be-medalled De
Passek, only dines the Beauty and Fashion of
Melbourne at an exclusive round table. The
Yank consul, bachelor Bray, was to the fore, of
yore, with July 4th and other starred-and-striped
occasions, but through public mourning, or Royal
appendicitis, or something or other, these were off
this year. And the other day the birthday of
Queen Wilhelmina was allowed to depart un-
heralded, although many eager eyes were turned
towards the Netherlands Consul Bosschart’s home
—an hospitable roost. Consul Bosschart should
really have set the Dutch flag flying that day. He
is one of the readiest and most comical characters
in all the mouldy official world—the first to
wire, to write, to despatch messages of con-
dolence, greeting, sympathy, and congratula-
tion. He must sit with his eye on the news
of the day and his busy pen in his hand.
He is a bushy, fussy, breathless, beet-root-tinted
Consul, full of good nature and varied information.
* I lof my littel children,’ he says—‘ two Persians,
two Australians. That is right—chertainly,
chertainly. Equal representation chertainly.’
‘ 1 There was not the usual vice-regal opening of
the Daffodil Show this year, and a big, high-
shouldered Scotch president gave the flowery
Paradise a modest blessing himself. But good
wine needs no bush and daffodils can dispense witli
vice-regal compliments. There was a big crush
last Thursday. The Byron Moores, the Alister
Clarkes, and the pretty Anderson sisters were
prominent among the haughty culturists, who,
with a sort of inark-the-sparrow’s-fall-eye, traced
every tiny petal in the whole show. The
decorated dining tables furnished plenty of new
ideas. _ The prize-table gained a gorgeous effect
by a riot of yellow blooms and pure, crisp Ice-
land poppies, set off with the radiance of many
electric rose globes. These luminous lamps
lolled oil their petalled sides like big languishing
over-grown roses, beneath and around the centre
epergne—a splendid riot about the garden’s wine-
cups. Another table showed a design in orchids
and mauve chiffon draperies ; cold, but elegant.
“Archbishop Carr does his best to scare his
flock from mixed marriages. When Kathleen
persists in marrying Donald, or Michael takes
an angel who is in danger of being Lost in the
Hereafter, the ceremony, if carried out by an
R.C. priest, is shorn of all grandeur, all
solemnity, and rendered as brief and curt as the
legal contract. By such means Dr. Carr hopes to
strike terror into the mixed marryer, but only
succeeds, as a rule, in sending him or her to some
more accommodating church.
“The Clarkes are popular because of their
pleasant air and absence of ‘side.’ But at a
recent tea and scone show Gov. Clarke drew the
line. One of the ladies of the party, finding his
Ex. genial and chatty, raced off for her youngest
hope (a chick of a few summers) and pranced her
on her knee, and was for passing on the infant to
be rock-horsed by the vice-regal knees. His Nibs
objected ; not because—it is stated on unreliable
authority—of ‘side.’ but because he has been
advised by Mr. Chamberlain that it is unconsti-
tutional for a Governor to dandle the children of
the territory over which he rules—that privilege
being reserved for M’s.P.

makes the uncomeliness of her worshipping and
Eatient spouse the principal whetstone to sharpen
er wit. She alludes to him as ‘My HSsop,’
‘The Fossil,’ ‘Bete Philosophe,’ ‘Dear old
Juggins,’ and a dozen such playful titles, flavored
with the same spice of malice. The men hail

ordinately. Another dame invariably alludes to
hubby as ‘ Old Trousers,’ and the world shakes its
sides laughing, while wondering however such an
uncommonly smart woman came to ‘ throw herself
away ’ so. Yet he had the money which resoued
this amgel from slavery at a chiffon counter.
Astonishing how much a wife can surpass her
husband on her husband’s money !
“The French and Italian artistes of this con-
cert-ridden city are summoned to ‘Stonington’
via the ’phone these days. Her Ex. and offspring
like to keep the foreign wheels of their tongues
well-oiled, and they use foreign languages as often
as they can command occasion. The artistes
(mostly singing) are well treated at ‘Stonington,’
and their visits are profitable to themselves and
their hosts. This may have some effect upon cer-
tain Sassiety dames, whose treatment of artistic
personages has been long a matter of hurt to those
aesthetic souls. The Clarkes have made music the
vogue ; perhaps they will set a fashion in civility
to bards and working musicians.

Edward Fisher Pittman succeeds Duncan
M‘Lachlan, who succeeded Harrie Woods as
N.S.W. Under-Secretary for Mines. Mr.
M‘Lachlan went to the Federal service and Mr.
Woods into private business. The new Under-
secretary is a Melbourne native, a little over 50
years of age. *
The care and worry of N.S.W. Auditor-
Generalship do not appear to seriously shorten
life. Captain Mayne, who held the post for
years, died the other day at Burwood (N.S.W.),
aged 95 ; and the present venerable occupant of
the office counts well over 80, and can still spot a
faked balance-sheet.
J. R. Campbell, alias Gleeson, the impostor
who made a business of suing Governments for
bogus railway-injuries, and who was bowled out
when, after long shamming paralysis, he went
into court on a stretcher and claimed £10,000
from Queensland Government. Sketched from life
in an ante-room of Brisbane Courthouse.
Judge Boucaut (S.A.) used the slangy but
expressive word “barney” twice in the Hall
divorce-case. His Honor'is quite unconventional.
His street dress often consists of a tweed suit and
a slouch hat, and he has frequently been heard to
say on a hot day in court : ‘ ‘ Gentlemen, I think
we will dispense with all this tomfoolery ” —taking
off his wig.
An orange pip gives some people appen-
dicitis, and others can convert the appendix
into a shot-pouch with comparative impunity.
A resident of Powlett River (Vic.), when a boy,
was addicted to chewing leaden shot pellets. No
ill effects were felt until recently, 25 years after-
wards, when symptoms resembling those of appen-
dicitis occurred. The appendix was removed in a
private-hospital in Melb., when it was found to
contain 42 pellets. The patient is now in good
health.
Geo. Wylde, who has heroically, but un-
successfully, entered the lists of every S.A.
election for several years past, is a real sticker.
Twenty-three years ago he laid the foundations of
his present residence mN. Adelaide. Since then
he has gradually added to the place, every piece
of work (inside and outside) being the work of his
own hands. Windows were boarded-up for years
until Wylde learnt a little of the glazier’s business
and put .matters right The house is still incom-
plete.
“John Downer, the most comical-looking
and interesting spouter in the Senate, ” writes jt
galleryite, “is missed from the House these days by
the sentries. It is -T.D.’s peculiarity to indulge in
smiling’ contortions as he swallows the ill-flavored
flowers of an opponent’s bitter speech. When
lie smiles most during an impassioned harangue,
he is most galled, and members by degrees have
found it out. So that when he leers now at one
who has had the temerity to attack him, and flings
his smiling sarcasms at the knave, members know
that S’John is squirming, himself, from a poisoned
Shaft of wit. Women think, On the other hand,
that there is nothing in political circles so potent
as the bland mandarinic smile of Senator
Downer.”
Strange, in a way, the command that W.
Gr. Spence, president of the A. W.U., has over the
bush-workers of N.S.W., Yic. and S. Aus.
Spenoe never did any bush-work himself, and is
hardly more like the wild and
woolly person that a typical
bushman is supposed to. be than
W. E. Abbott is himself. He is
a rigid teetotaller, smokes hardly
any, has scores of health and other
fads, and is rather “old-woman-
ish ”in speech and manner. But
he is a great “sticker,” never
loses his temper, is suave and placid,
and has studied human nature even
more than books (phrenology and mes-
merism are among his fads); and, as he
has also a ready tongue under the most
perfect control, he is invaluable in a confer-
ence. As a labor organiser he graduated at
Creswick (Vic.), as miners’secretary ; his first
visit to N.S.W. in connection with a strike was
when, in ’BB, there was a row on the Barrier. He
went up there with J. B. Burton (ex-Mines Min-
ister, then general president of the miners); but
the trouble was over before he got through. Most
of the rows lie has had a tongue in since have been
a lot tougher.

Fancy the apostle hoarding up that!
So assured are some people that Premier
John See purposes an early visit to England that
poets and prose-writers are already sending him
large bundles of manuscript to get published for
them in Fogtown. A Judge who tripped to
England a while back declares that the principal
part of his luggage consisted of manuscripts
which, in an ill-judged mood, he had taken charge
of for Australian authors.
A Melbourne pressman :
“ Shiels loves misery,” writes somebody in last week’s
Bulletin, and this essentially feminine trait reminds an
observer of many womanish characteristics in the Vic.
Treasurer. His petty quarrelsomeness, his overweening
desire to make mysteries out of prosaic facts, his affecta-
tion of illness and his eternal neurosis are indicative
rather of the petulant schoolgirl than the strongman who
should hold such an office. Does not Sophocles say on
the subject: “ Women ever love to brood o’er sorrows
and indulge their woe.
Aleck Peacock was given a night by Melb.
Trades’ Hall people, the other week, and presented
with his own portrait in oils. This was the most
gushing event that has occurred in Lygon-street.
Finley and the disgusting Tocsin incident were
all forgotten, and the men who had sworn to
pursue Alexander the Howler to his political
death competed hotly in pouring eulogies upon
the friend of labor. He took it meekly, like
one conscious of his deserts. Peacock may have
been well done in oils before, but he was never so
thoroughly done in butter.
Apropos the assertion that Lord Salisbury
was sent to Australia in his youth for his health’s
sake, an old Yic. identity remarks that he
“never saw a sick man with such an ap-
petite.” In fact, young Cecil seems to
have distinguished himself solely as a
mighty trencherman while in exile. The
ex-Premier has for decades past suffered
severely, from digestive maladies, which
at last seem to have knocked him out. To
his Australian gastronomic experiences
may be due much of this trouble. No
man can load his stomach with half a ton
of damper-and-mutton at a time and
carry that stomach far on the road to im-
mortality.
A Melb. telephone girl has had the
audacity to tell Minister Lyne to keep his hair
on. Plain Bill ordered a judicious reprimand.
The sting lies in the common knowledge that Bill
is engaged in a desperate struggle to keep his hair
on, and his track is littered with bottles bearing
the strange device—the name of any hair-restorer
can be inserted here at regular advertising rates.
Geo. Lansell, the successful Bendigo mine
owner, recently celebrated his 79th birthday, and
is still hale and hearty. He originally went into
mining in consequence of the local Borough
Council turning him off the land on which he
carried on a very evil-smelling soap-boiling busi-
ness, and considered himself at the time a ruined
and ill-used man. He is now the richest resident
in Quartzopolis.
Mr. Wood, N.S.W. ex - Minister for
Justice, has given up amateur soldiering. In
the Boer war days, when Lyne was Premier, Mr.
Wood was quartermaster and ached to follow
Kenneth Mackay to glory. But Lyne loved him
and held his coat-tails, and consoled him by
making him a lieut.-col. with extreme rapidity.
Now that Lyne has gone from the Premiership,
Mr. Wood resigns from the army with more or
less joy.
Sydney Pitt-street Congregational Church
(where Parson Tremayne Dunstan is not to be
found just now) is discussing the advisability of
borrowing £l5OO on mortgage for church purposes.
General impression (gathered from the frequent
increases of “screw” that the last shepherd was
favored with) was that this church was a very
flourishing institution indeed ; certainly the pastor
seemed a very flourishing institution up to the
time of his lamented departure.
The doughty Dowie is about to come with
a rush here, like the Salvation Army of 19 years
ago. His local agent, Overseer Voliva, is a
commonplace spouter, but begins to exhibit his
hand in Melbourne as an organiser. He has just
received 200 rather impressive dissolving
views from Dowie, showing aspects of Zion
in Chicago, and Zion City, 42 miles from
Chicago. The cause has spread from Mel-
bourne city to Fitzroy, Ascot Yale and
other places. It will leap directly. Voliva
has had a fine set of robes made for the
Melbourne choir. Dowie sends lots of
money, and they take up no collections.
“RARE BIRDS ” (FITZGIBBON).
Melbourne. Tocsin, on Deakin :
Deakin is to the House what a man’s face is to the man.
He grows on one. He is quiet and courteous, but master
of the House. Members are regretting the time Barton
will return. Deakin is Watson’s chum, Braddon’s friend,
Spence’s confidante, Kingston’s supporter, Bamford’s
adviser, and Ronald’s counsellor. He makes everyone
his special acquaintance, and is liked by everybody. If
he only had Higgins’ backbone he would go far.
The same “if” that overshadows Sliiels and Drake
and so many others. And, meanwhile, Bent is
going about Melbourne with a backbone that is
no use to him, he having neither capacity, tact,
nor manners—only backbone.
The most deplorably foolish Gent One on
record has just been “struck off” in England.
Having paii away certain trust-moneys, and
being called upon to show vouchers for them, he
produced receipts signed by Smith, Jones, and
Brown, verified by an affidavit taken before
Robinson ; and he himself wrote the signatures
to the whole lot in his own undisguised hand-
writing. His counsel stated that the money had
beyond question been properly paid, and the
ultimate foolishness was done only to save expense
and trouble. As the expense, if any, could easily
be charged to the client, his desire to avoid trouble
must have been a record laziness, and the Court
felt that a man who was so tired as all that was
better out of temptation, so he went off the
Rolls.
Frightful libel on a deceased Australian
judge, by one who knew him :
The late Judge was considered one of the
soundest lawyers that ever sat on the judicial bench. He
was also one of the grossest and most d sgusting feeders
that ever sat above the salt. He was particularly fond of
butter, and invariably ate it with a spoon when he found
it goo 1. At present writer’s luncheon table he once ite
over half a pound and then asked for more. At the
Custom House Hotel, Maryborough, one day I saw him go
round the table, lift the covers—it was just before the
lunch bell rang—and finally spit into a dish of stewed
oysters. Then he emptied a plate of butter into a dish of
mashed potatoes and ate them with a spoon. On another
occasion, while carving a round of beef, he paused,
inserted the long blade of the carving knife down his
back and scratched his cuticle with extreme placidity,
after which he resumed carving. He was a hog, but a
very sound hog, and the judicial profession was a con-
siderable loser when he died.
Lord George Hamilton, a cabled proba-
bility for the Governor-Generalship, is a clever
Parliamentary tactician, and learnt most of his
tactics from his mother. At his first election
for the House of Commons, his mother, then
Marchioness of Abercorn, went through the con-
stituency betting liberally against her son’s
chances, and it became worth most people’s
while to give the callow youth a vote. The
family was all-powerful among the Conser-
vatives of the North of Ireland, but tenant-
righters, Home Rulers, and Fenians were banded
against it, and only for his hard-gambling mother
they would have smashed George.
The arrest in Sydney of Parson Worthing-
ton, of the Unitarian Church, who was taken
over to Melbourne on a charge of obtaining a
large sum of money by false pretences, is the
latest reflection that has been cast on the church.
Worthington had a lengthy career in Maoriland
and other places before he came to Australia.
He is a stately, eloquent, plausible, old gentle-
man with a gorgeous head of hair, and came
from America. He isn’t supposed .to have
been always a Unitarian, but Unitarianism is
a loosely-hung-together sort of creed, and almost
any eloquent gentleman with a splendid head of
hair and a fine boiled shirt seems to suit it.
K. Murchison, lately dismissed from the
Lands Board by N. S. W. Minister Crick for having,
as alleged, said that “the Minister was not fit for
his office, ” had been 12 years a member of the
Coonabarabran Land Board. Murchison denies
having used the alleged language, and demands
an inquiry. The Minister refuses to investigate,
dismisses Murchison, and tells Parliament, “I
satisfied myself he used the words, and won’t
bother about an inquiry. ” There is a fine, high-
handed method about Crick at times, but, all the
same, Murchison will get his inquiry if he keeps
asking for it. He has only got to persist, and.h*
will develop into a spectre that will haunt the
See-Crick Ministry all its days.
Cabled that Dr. Jameson, of the “Raid,”
is “the coming man” in S’Africa. What he is
coming to is not stated, and it is certain that no.
one likely to come to any good could say, as
Jameson did t’other day in the Cape Parliament,
that “ the Raid was an abominable blunder ; but
fair-minded men might forget.” No one on either
side in S’African politics can ever forget that it
was Jameson’s insane foray that made the war
inevitable, and Jameson himself cannot have
recovered his mental balance if he supposes other-
wise. Also, the cableman must be quite as mad
to suppose that a political party—especially one
which has such a difficult game to play as that
before the Cape Imperialists—will trust itself to
Jameson’s leading. Bad enough in all conscience
to have such a whirling and ill-balanced person as
a follower.
Gunga Brahm, probably the best wrestler
in Australia to-day, vas recently haled before
Melb. City Court for threatening boss Hindu
Mukand Lai. The latter in his evidence de-
scribed their relative strengths as “I am like a
hollow house ; he is like a solid pyramid.” This
poetic imagery is latent in all Orientals; the
Australian versifier who contrives to make money
with weak lines about the wattle should thank
Providence for not having allowed him to be born
in India—for where every man is a poet there is no
market for poetry. At a recent census at Lahore
no less than 20,000 returned their occupations as
“ poet,” and there are scarcely as many as that in
all Australia. Anyhow, The Bulletin only
knows about 19,800. Gunga Brahm combines the
callings of wrestler, manufacturer and priest, and
it was his giving way to two or three more or
less clerical weaknesses tliat is said by his country-
men to have been responsible for his defeat last
year at the hands of Buttan Singh.

YOUNGER: “ What do you think of the hew
blanky mare, uncle?”
This is the kind of eloquence that does
not appear in Federal Hansard. The woolly
Simon Fraser (elected simply in his capacity: as
boss Orangeman) is talking on the Electoral Bill
in short explosive jerks, and his general style is
as follows
Fraser (loq.): “This is going too far—too far —too
far, I say ” (rising inflection), “too far! It will lead to
imperrrsonation— to imperrrsonation—and to, and to—
to imperrrsonation, and—and—and other things.”
Dawson : “There may be impersonation under any
system.”
Fraser : “There is imperrrsonation—plinty of it—
plinty of it —I saay, in elections now—in elections now!
There is plinty of it. I know thart —I know thart.”
Dawson : “ Why are the offenders not prosecuted ? ”
Fraser : “ Because it is not known—it is a secret—it is
a secret. Oh, I know thart—l know thart—l know thart
wull ! ”
Dawson: “ Then you must be the impersonator your-
self !”
Fraser : “ Oh, don’t they come out of the polling-
booth boasting of it —boasting .of it—boasting of im-
perrrsonation so that everyone can hear them.” (Sits
down suddenly in a strong Scotch accent, leaving the
Senate much edified.)

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the case of ethnolojical and
natural-history items , etc., alivays give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
If Sydney papers and politicians have done
their best to minimise the drought they also did
their best to exaggerate its “ break up ” at theend
of August. In the far west, which has been
longest without rain, there were only scattered
showers; in places fin. fell ; in others, only a few
miles away, not enough to measure. Most of the
tracks are still impassable; and cartage between
Broken Hill and White Cliffs—latter, among the
larger backblocks settlements, is the greatest suf-
ferer—was not affected. The price a week after
the rain was £l5 a ton for the 160 miles. At
Broken Hill, whose water supply is causing un-
easiness again, not a gallon was caught in the
reservoir.

“Jungle”: Things have* come to be so
bad in Victoria these times that farmers and others
in the Northern districts are trucking what stock
they have left to Gippsland, about the only corner
in the State not destitute of feed and water. There
are sad tales of loss and suffering on the journey,
and it’s nothing for a man to lose half beforeN
reaching the Promised Land. Considering that
these stock have mostly consumed their own value
in hay, etc., already, they will cost a lot before
the rains bring relief.

“Sirhap”: What a lot of knowledge is
running to waste in Queensland, and quite over-
looked by our politicians. It is a wonder that
they do not offer a prize for a ‘ ‘ new way to pay
old debts.” Here is Philp hurting his political
and business position by cutting down the civil
service—his friends—so that he can struggle to
attain that political delight, an equal re venue and
expenditure. He is worse than a bubonic scare,
and he tinkers, doing' great harm, yet he cannot
come within coo-ee of his goal. Yet, the means
and the end are so plain that a blind man could
not fail to see them. Here it is, and it is called
Bowen. That place has the best northern harbor
in Queensland ; is big enough for all Britain’s
fleet, and safe enough, too. Around it are several
thousand square miles of agricultural country,
extending from the coast to the Leichhardt range’
70 miles straight. Then coal; the great Peak
Downs coalfield terminates but 63 miles from it,
topped by the blaok-soil agricultural lands of the
Bowen River. Philp does not need to repurchase
from the Q.N. Bank at absurd prices land for
closer settlement, for this well-watered black-soil
country is all available. Then its mining resources:
it has seven gold-camps near it, and beyond the
Leichhardt Range, watered by the Sellheim River,
is a gold and mineral field, dormant, for it has
never been exploited, and good enough for W.A.
The town is well-suited for a seaport, being high!
dry, and well-drained naturally, with fertile land
all around it. I suppose the circumference of its
bay would be 30 miles, and every foot of it is fit for
building sites; then three-fourths of the town is
State land, also the bulk of the suburban and
country lands. A railway from Bowen, 127 miles
long, south-westerly, costing £234,000, would pass
through many thousand acres of first-class agri-
cultural black-soil land on the Bowen River, which
is the only permanent tributary of the Burdekin,
traverse the coal-lands there, and terminate at the
Mt. Wyatt-Sellheim gold and mineral field, where
it would junction with the to-be transcontinental
railway section from Clermont to Charters Towers
—and a better payable line, when its country is
developed, would be hard to find in Australia.
Yes, there are millions knocking around Bowen if
VPhilp would but forget the grovelling selfishness
®f his beloved Townsville, festering by its unlucky
mudhole a few miles further north.

without going outside N.S. W.

It recalls a bush marriage of long ago. All
preparations had been made for the weddin nr at
Unumgar Station (N.S.W.), but a difficulty
cropped up when the parson arrived from Ipswich
his license not being valid in N.S.W. A ride to

ECCLESIASTICAL ARCHITECTURE IN AUSTRALIA.
The Congregational Church , Boulder, Westralia.
Photo, by H. Merrilees, Boulder City.
upon. Horses were soon run up and
saddled, and the whole party set out on the
journey through the bush, the last three
miles being the mailman’s track, up the steep,
scrubby side of Mount Lindsay. Under the lofty
plateau that crowns the mount the cavalcade dis-
mounted, and, hitching the horses to the bushes,
assembled on a little clear spot by the scrub; and,
while the bell-birds made music around, the
ceremony began. When it was about half through,
the discovery was made that a horde of leeches
had invaded “ the church,” and were swarming up
the legs of all parties concerned. The remainder
of the ceremony was put through with speed.
Then the party dispersed round the corner in two
sections, one of which swore and the other
screamed ; and the animals were picked off and
stamped upon. The principals are still living on
the Richmond River, N.S.W.

Dear Bulletin, — Be the controversy about
“ The Ragged Thirteen,” your correspondents evi-
dently allude to the band of robbers that struck
Borroloola—then the Alsatia of Queensland —in
the beginning of 1886. Heaven only knew where
they came from, but as gold was just at that time
discovered on the Ord (W.A.), it may be pre-
sumed they were making for that place. To
say that they committed no depredations is not
an absolute fact—while they stayed around
matters were made exciting for the law-abiding
population on the M'Arthur. The principal
storekeeper (M‘Leod) had £6OO stolen, and perished
in his pursuit of the thieves. The township was
held up by them for 24 hours, and no human
being dared venture out of doors. One of the gang,
known as “The Orphan,” erected a gallows on an
adjoining station, owned by two brothers
(Germans), and, after slaughtering their cattle,
sold the station beef in Borroloola and kept the
proceeds. A man named Lenehan was found
murdered on an out-station of Broadmere, and his
death was blamed on the blacks, but there exists
a doubt. This should fix up “The Ragged
Thirteen ” trouble. They were not, strictly
speaking, outlaws, but as the nearest policeman
was at Camooweal, 150 miles away, they could
afford to do as they pleased.— Ben Bolt.

The necessity for artifi-
cially-feeding cattle is rapidly
spreading over the continent.
It is even down in Western
Victoria now. As soon as
Gippsland has adopted the
Eraetice Australia will be one
uge open-air stable. Near
Hamilton (Vic.) the station
hands cart the hay into the
Eaddocks, and, keeping the
orses moving, throw it off.
Result: a long line of hay, and
a quickly-forming line of cat-
tle. The little remaining she-
oak timber may soon be valu-
able for feeding purposes:
Years ago the “bullockies”
used to “sneak” she-oak for their workers, and
the art of lopping the trees without making noise
enough to attract the attention of the station
boundary-rider was then much appreciated.
» » * *
“General ” : It is quite a usual thing, on
cattle stations, to “ throw and tie ” a wild beast.
When let up, the animal will drive quietly enough
with “coachers”—quiet cattle. Some men become
very expert at this game. On Wyaga (Goondi-
witidi) there was a man who would take after the
wildest beast—bulls not excepted—catch it by the
tail, jerk it off its feet, dismount quickly, straps in
hand, and tie its legs—all single-handed. Then
he would sit on his victim’s ribs, light his pipe
and have a smoke, pending the arrival of
“ coachers.”
“Pankaroo” writes :
“Arbor Day” in connection
with Victorian State schools
was duly observed recently,
and writer was interested,
when touring the country a
week or two back, to see
sundry stunted and sickly-
looking exotic trees struggling
vainly with surroundings that
are almost too much even for
the hardy native stringybark.
The latter can give most other
trees points in re fossicking
for a iiving with a minimum
of water and soil, and it is the
height of absurdity to imagine
that slips of trees, fresh from
delicately - tended nurseries,
could survive such conditions.
As regards this Arbor Day
foolishness, anyhow, my
opinion is that instructions in
rudimentary forestry would
better meet the case. The
average Australian bushie or
farmer has no knowledge of the
value of growing timber in re-
lation to rainfall, and his idea
of land “ improvement ” con-
sists in ruthlessly downing the
timber holus-bolus instead of
sparing such trees as are likely
to be valuable in the future.
Year by year our rainfall is
getting less, but it appears to
be forgotten that the denuda-
tion of our hills of their cover-
mg forests is largely the cause. Timbered 'mountain-
ranges are invaluable rain-inducers, and the sooner Govt,
sets to work to educate the rising generation to this end
the better will Australia be able to “ Face the Dawn.”

Writes “ Combo,” from Northern N.S.W.:
In July Review op Reviews, Mr. Edward Dowling voices
a complaint against the “White Australia” legislation. He
says that, as there are man}' full-blood and half-caste abo-
rigines on the North Coast rivers of N.S.W., where sugar
is cultivated, the limitation in the rebate means that
some of the above will be debarred from their usual em-
ployment, and must therefore cause considerable hard-
ship. Mr. Dowling, as a member of the Aborigines Pro-
tection Board, should know better. All the aborigines
employed in the cane-fields of this State could be counted
on the fingers of one hand. The Northern rivers’ black
has too high an opinion of himself to indulge in “ toil ”
for any lengthened period. His “ usual employment ”is
shifting beer (or rum that never paid excise). He will do
a casual job for a casual meal, but would sooner take the
meal and leave the job. One small mill-owner on the
Clarence had a penchant for this class of labor when he
could get it, but “ pay-day ” was characterised by such
scenes of debauchery in the blacks’ camp as would make
any member of the Protection Board thankful that the
rebate clause was passed. What few blacks are left on
the Northern rivers are well-cared-for by the Board and
local residents.

“H.S.R.”: Re the proposed visit (in
search of Australian information) of Boer agricul-
tural deputies:—
Let them be taken from, say, Tenterfield to Gooudiwindi
following the Dumaresq River from where the main
road strikes it at Mingoola. They would have Queensland
on the right and N.S.W. on the left. They would witness
a devastation almost as complete as that of the Transvaal
and Orange Free State with the exception of the burnt
farms. Whole herds of cattle have almost totally
perished, the river stinks with the dead, and the fish are
dying. Many people are ruined, and selectors in many
cases have not ahorse left to work their ground when the
rain does come. No one who has not witnessed it could
realise the existing desolation and misery. He would
indeed be a vindictive Boer who would ask for more
vengeance on Austral.ans than the drought has inflicted
* * * *
Of McArthur River station (N.T.), where
the wild blacks spear the cattle sometimes for the
sake of only the gall-fat, it is said by the looaj
stock-inspector that, starting 20 years ago with
14,000 head, it now musters only 10,000. In no
year has a greater number been sold than were
branded in that year, and in several years none at
all ; and there have been practically no losses
through drought. This and the Victoria River
country, two of the best-watered districts in the
territory, suffer more than any others from the
natives’ spears.

An attractive trip to what is known as the
Riviera of America is announced by the Oceanic Steam-
ship Co. The cost, including steamer and all hotel
expenses, involving an absence of about 53 days from
Australia, is but £6O. for this the traveller is conveyed
from Sydney to San Francisco and back, and is allowed
10 days’ stay in America, with choice whether he will
spend his time in San Francisco or at the Hotel Del
Monte, Southern California. The first excursion was in-
augurated by the Ventura, which carried a number of
patrons from Sydney, leaving ‘2sth August. The next
sailing is the Sierra, September 15th, and the excursions
will be continued every three weeks thereafter. Patrons
desirous of extending their time in America can do so at
their own expense beyond the time specified.*

“J. M. J.” : Be,
birdinimiory. ‘A.H.T.’
(8., 23/8/’O2), in put-
ting “D.W.” right,
himself makes a blun-
der. He is right in
saying that the hen
lyre-bird does not mimic, but wrong when
stating that her only note is a long drawn-out
quis-s-s-s-e. The natural call of the lyre-bird of
both sexes is kur-r-r-r—kur-r-r, repeated time
after time. The quis-s-s-s-e is also common to
both sexes, and is their note of fear. When
suddenly startled they give it a couple of times
and jump up on to some handy bough. Lyre
birds cannot fly upwards, they jump upwards
from limb to limb until they can see out, when

(Mrs. Lyre-bird lays only one at a time) is dark-

nest is like an enlarged wren’s with a verandah
over the entrance, under which the mother bird
sits once the young one is hatched. Very few
people have seen them.

“Nymsie”: The much-despised bunny
has proved a veritable Godsend in many parts of
Vic., where local trappers find that it pays better

than to truck to Melbourne storage sheds— now

in supplying the demand, for a bunny running to
41bs. orslbs., cleaned, is cheap at 6d. to 9d., the
vendor retaining the pelt. Even the scraggiest
and boniest meat is now sd. per lb.
lie “ Hagram’a ” query (8., 16/8’02),
Wilson Dobbs writes :
To adequately determine by English lettering
the phonetic values of aboriginal words is the
patent difficulty of native Australian linguistics.
There are so many sets of word-sounds for the
same things among the various tribes, and the old-
time hearers appear to have all depicted their con-
ception of such sounds by different symbols.
The phonographic records of the Spencer-Gillen
Expedition will greatly help in establishing a
scientific basis for trans-literation. As an attempt
at accounting for “ Coolangatta ” I submit the
following : —Works dealing with the Native lan-
guage—like the late Prof. Morris’s “ Austral-
English ” and Mr. Joshua Lake’s Appendix to
Webster’s “International Dictionary” thereon—
classify two seemingly separate sounds, rendered
“Uooramin” and “Currajong” as practically
synonymous words. For they are said to signify
one or other of the many plants and trees possess-
ing tough bark or fibre, from which Australian and
Tasmanian aborigines made cordage, nets or
netting. By the former term were the most com-
mon of these trees typified; by the latter the
several species known to us as Stereulia were more
specially specified. We need not deal with the
modern division of black, brown, green, and Tas.
currajong into their special botanical equivalents ;
nor illustrate, except in passing, the diversity of
spelling—such as Kooramin and Kurrajong, and
so on—with which both words have been treated
by different writers ; but proceed to note that at
least two other similar aboriginal word-sounds
apply to trees or tree-growth—namely, Coolamin
or Kooliman, and Coolibah or Koolibah—both also
spelt in various . ways. The first-named is a large
vessel —almost like a boat—made of bark or wood
for holding water, the last an indigenous tree
growing in Queensland and N.S.W. Does it not
seem evident then that the sound represented
by the prefixes Coor, Curr and Cool, or Koor,
Kurr and Kool, etc., collectively referred to trees
generally ; and the suffixes denoted some slight
shades of distinction thereof ? And that such
names of places as Coolabah, Coolamon, Coolan-
gatta, Corrowong, Currawang and Kurrajong in
N.S.W.—Corryong and Koorooman in Vic., with
Currauiulka, Koolunga and Kooringa in S.A., and
Curramulla and Kooralbyn in Q., respective! v—
among others—probably implied well-wooded
paitsofthe country, wherein each “ wore their
rue with a difference.” Then, as a further proof
of varied sound-values, take the township termed
Tallangatta in Vic. (about 25 miles east of
Wodonga). The native name is “ Tool-an-gatta,”
and is said to mean “ where Currajong trees
abound.” Now, a creek is near-by called Corry on"
Creek—Strzelecki has Cowrang Creek—so, taking
all into consideration, Tallangatta seems but
another mode of spelling Coolangatta.
* * * *
“ Via” : Those who pin their faitli
to artesian irrigation as a cure for drought
will be disheartened to learn that, in many
cases, the mineralised water destroys the
roots of all vegetation. Some of the bore
water is not mineralised, but such deposits
are mostly shallow, and the initial cost is
very severe. The difficulty might be partly
avoided by having surface reservoirs,
“karenes” (Persian covered 'channels) to
prevent evaporation. But, after all, the
drought is Australia's poor relation, and a
hungry one at that.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC) CONTRIBUTORS.
G.H.S. : You’ve got a good idea of line work, bub you
should use Indian ink. Gag too poor, anyhow.
Beechworth (Vic.) Benevolent Asylum
affords outdoor relief to no less than 110 Chinese.
The Chinaman, as a matter of fact, is one of the
most consistent paupers in the community. He
gets credit for thrift partly because it is a secretive
virtue, which consists in burying money away,
and he looks secretive; also, in many cases, he
looks like a man who would be capable of burying
almost anything. Besides, he wears, by nature,
the inscrutable aspect of a person who has mone-
tary responsibilities weighing on his mind.
M. CHAPPELL, winner of the Dunlop Road
Race, Warrnambool to Melbourne.

The Senate has proceeded in a federal
spirit by modifying many of ita original
proposals. If it is not to be met in the
same manner we must approximate to a
deadlock. And those who talk lightly
about or who advocate a deadlock can
scarcely have realised the serious conse-
quences of this policy. And if they are to*

equity lawyers, supported by the votes of
the anti-Federalists, we may indeed expect
to hear a talk of separation.
