LEADING ART JEWELLER,
809, 811 813, 815, & 817 GEORGE-STREET,
SYDNEY, NEARLY OPPOSITE RAILWAY STATION, _


Sydney Art Society Show.
FIRST NOTICE.
The two art societies of Sydney having united, the
first combined exhibition was opened to the publio
this week. As was to be expected, there is a
great falling-off in the proportion of absolutely
worthless pictures. As was to be feared, there are
included many whose hanging can only be
explained on the ground of rewarding worthy but
mistaken industry, or of exercising the gentle, and
under most circumstances praiseworthy, spirit <_f
compromise. There is nothing absolutely start-
ling in merit; there is much showing a very high
level of talent; there is a little which is absolutely
ludicrous. The National Gallery has purchased
with some freedom, and seems to have, on the
whole, exercised very poor judgment.
• • 9 »
The fault to be found with some exhibitors is
that they have attempted far too much. This
applies conspicuously to Lister-Lister, who is
to be credited with some of the best canvases in
the room, but is responsible in all for a round
dozen, of which some are distinctly below the
mark of his talent. There is not one that is bad,
but how much better the judgment if he had sent
in only, say, “Cloud Shadows,” “The Flowing
Tide,”“Waning Day,” and “Halcyon Days”!
“Moonrise ” doesn’t quite solve that great pro-
blem of painting moonlight on the water ; in
“ Low Tide ” there is a green which doesn’t strike
the eye as natural; “Lengthening Shadows”
is too red. But in “ The Flowing Tide ”
the color on hills and sea is superb, and
“Halcyon Days,” is a decorative sea-scape
of dainty grace. Piguenit, good, sane, sound
Piguenit, is but poorly represented. “The
Fisheries, River Darling,” is distinctly good, but
it is only a small sketch. “The Summit of the
King William Range ” is practically his only
landscape. It has all the rich, harmonious coloring,
and the solid draughtsmanship of this artist, in the
foreground, but a white mist-cloud, with some
light in it, is a shade too “flaky ” and stiff. It
hasn’t that luminous quality which is noticeable
in the treatment by Peter Graham of a similar
effect.
* * * *
Gordon-Coutts has two ambitious canvases, each
E itched in the same low key. Mr. Coutts must
eware lest he be charged one day with mono-
tony. Granted that in both the pictures under
review the intention is to pourtray melancholy or
mystery, that intention in one at least, “The
Story of the Bunyip,” might have been better
served by a bright gleam somewhere. In this
canvas, too, the figure of the old man is rather
theatrical. In “Lost but Found” the fallen
soldier is unconvincing ; but the girl’s face is excel-
lent, and the atmosphere good ; there is some
clever detail work in the foreground. Mr. Julian
Ashton confines himself to small sketches in and
around Sydney, and is to be congratulated on
some very fine work. “ Garden Island—after-
glow” is a happy translation into paint of a
familiar and beautiful atmospheric effect. In
“ Domain Gates—Early Morning,” however, the
architecture of St. Mary’s Cathedral is made to
look strangely “sausagey.”
• * » . »
Arthur Collingridge is another who exhibits far
too many pictures (12 again—was that the limit ?)
As in the case of Lister-Lister there is not in-
cluded anything that is bad, but there are some
obvious pot-boilers mixed up with one or two land-
scapes which show with clear charm the atmos-
phere on the hills of the N.S.W. tableland—that
glowing pageant of deep purple, soft mauve and
gold. “Harvest Time, Bathurst,” “The Wreck”
and “Peace” are his best canvases. J. Salvana’s
“Autumn on the Hawkesbury” has been pur-
chased by the Art Gallery Trustees—why, it
would be hard to say. The attempt to picture
light on roughened water is only an attempt, the
foreground is chromo-lithography, and the effec-
tive bits in the d'siance don’t atone for other
faults. A. J. Fischer’s work is very meritorious,
“The Track Through Deadman’s Hollow” being
a specially good bit of work.
A very ambitious picture is Sid Long’s fla-
mingoes a tour de force which at once
challenges attention, and, on the whole, justifies
the courage of the artist. A still pool—broken
and splashed in one corner hedged with
the dark-green Australian forest, touched by the
oblique red rays of the sun ; a group of red fla-
mingoes, and two red-headed nude women. The
birds are splendid, the water and the light
good, the flesh of the women rather flat and
ugly. Another ambitious picture is Howard
Ashton’s. “ Centennial Park.” The distant hills
across Botany Bay are very fine, with real depth
in the valleys. But the whole coloring is a shade
too high, the grass too lush, the hills too blue.
Garlick’s “Drought Stricken” shows his skill as
an animal painter, but doesn’t .show the drought.
A pathetic failure is “Dread Drought,” by H.
Moore-Jones. The painter had a true color-vision
of the horrible drought, the angry red of the sky,
the green scum of the festering pools, the dark
purple of the eye-sockets torn by the crows; but he
absolutely fails to paint it into a symbolical
picture : his canvas is a crudity which is perhaps
exhibited as a suggestion for someone else to work
upon. Mr. Souter can’t honestly be congratulated
on any of his color effects. Picture of a “ Beautiful
Princess,” for instance, is not that at all, only
a stippling of some weirdly gorgeous clothes.
Dattilo-Rubbo is not too successful; he shows a
rich taste in color, but his composition (in “ Raga-
muffins,” for instance) strikes one as stiff and arti-
ficial. Norman Lindsay has one effort in color-
marked by the almost savage strength of this
artist’s black-and-white work, and showing good
knowledge of color-value in a bold scheme reminis-
cent of Griffenhagen’s “Judgment of Paris.”
Gerald Fitz Gerald has nothing to thank the hang-
ing committee for. With some work which is
distinctly good, with other that is very promising,
he is allowed to show such a crudity as “ Storm
Blown,” wherein the trees have obviously been
closely modelled on mahl-sticks, and the cattle on
the furnishings of a Noah’s Ark toy.
• * * *
In portraiture there is by no means a good show.
A portrait to be high art should reveal something
about the subject which was not obvious before ;
otherwise it is merely a photograph in painting.
Unless Fred Leist’s “Noeturnienne” is counted
as a portrait, there is nothing of any special excel-
lence. That picture is one of the very best in the
show. Edith E. Cusack does some careful, meri-
torious work of high technical quality, but with-
out a gleam of inspiration ; and Sid Long’s por-
traiture in “Dons and Leila” (the daughters of
Mr. A. S. Gordon), whilst distinctly good, is
nothing more. Leist’s portrait of war-corres-
pondent Frank Wilkinson is poor.
* * * *
Among the work which deserves notice as
reaching a fairly high level and suggesting promise
—“ APageantof Spring,”Elliott Grliner ; “ Pip,”
victoria Allen; “The Sea after a Shower,”
Annie H. Nicoll; “Mountain Pass, Katoomba,”
C. E. Stewart; “A Bleak Day on the Hawkes-
bury,” Nina W. Mulligan.
* * * *
Black and White (which is of a high standard)
will be dealt with in a fixture issue.
* • * *
The exhibition of the Art Society is not, of course,
to be taken as an exhibition of the best work of
professional artists.. It is partly that, partly the
second-class and third-class efforts of those artists,
and partly the efforts of students—painters who
have yet to learn very, very much of their art, and
who, perhaps, hope to learn and also incidentally
to sell, by exhibiting. Admittedly, Aus-
tralia stands only on the threshold of
Art as yet; and it. would possibly be
a bad interpretation of its mission if the State-
subsidised Art Society were to set its face against
the exhibition of all immature and trifling work,
for, by exhibiting, the young artist gets stimulus,
and, probably, also education. But the 1902 exhi-
bition suggests that the Society might do a great
deal more than it does at present in inspiring and
directing to a right purpose the immature art
talent of the community, if it were to resolutely
set its face against over-presumptuous crudity and
against banal trifling with Art; and if it were to
encourage, so far as possible, the choice of Aus-
tralian subjects, and the cultivation of distinctively
Australian sentiment.
• • • »
A certain amount of nightmare in purple and
ochre must be allowed to every Art Exhibition,
for the sake of the artists, who derive noble satis-
faction from the incapacity of the public to appre-
ciate such High Art. But it might reasonably be
insisted that before being pretentious a painter
should be quite sure of the elements of technique ;
that practice should precede presumption. This
rule is necessary in all Art; a writer must not
attempt to be Meredithian before be knows his
conjugations; a painter shouldn’t be allowed to be
mysterious and incompiehensible and High until
he has proved his knowledge of outline and color
values. Thereby exhibitions would be saved from
many pictures which no amount of argument will
convince the average lay-person are anything but
bosh.
» * * •
As to encouraging an Australian direction to
Australian effort, that would seem to be reason-
able enough and easy enough. There is a very
varied and very wonderful life in this country to
engage the painter’s eye—seascapes, landscapes,
cities, human types. Why should an Australian
exhibition by Australian artists include minor
English landscapes minor studies of foreign
flowers, minor repetitions of subjects which have
been so much better handled before? Of course
anything of the first order of Art should be
eagerly welcomed, whatever its subject—a land-
scape in Turkestan, a portrait study of a
Corean. But in the little efforts at art —
the amiable landscapes, the pleasing and in-
offensive flower studies, the ladylike expression
into Art of the cut lemon—in these the price of
tolerance should be Australianism. For the matter
of that a good deal that is in this exhibition ought
not to be given wall-space at all—Australian or not
—for the reason that-it is, whilst pretty enough,
too trifling, too banal. But there is much minor
effort that might be encouraged, on condition that
it engrosses itself on Australian subjects purely ;
then it might be tolerable as producing suggestive
essays in Australian painting, and in time be led
to better things. Weak echoes of the English
treatment of familiar subjects can lead to nothing.
The Parochial Conspiracy in N.S.W.
This (extracted from a letter signed “William
Onslow”) is the especial variety of slush with
which Sydney Telegraph is filling itself just at
present:—
If the Freetrade and Democratic committee that did
such good work in 1800, and others in sympathy, were to
lead a movement in favor of secession from Federal bond-
age, the country would respond as one man. Federation,
is pressing us to the ground. It needs us to take
prompt steps to recover our political freedom. The
proceedings of the Federal Parliament through the first
and present endless session have demonstrated beyond
question the jealous hatred of New South Wales felt by
the other States. This, added to the perfidy of some of
our own representatives, has brought about a condition
of things utterly unendurable. And this is but the pre-
cursor of the condition in which the other States have
determined to ultimately place us. The maladministra-
tion of the Customs, for instance, is an incubus far
greater than that which threatened Great Britain in years
gone by, but which was successfully repelled by the inter-
position of that grand old man, John Hampden. The free
and just laws which our forefathers fought and bled for
are being torn to shreds by the newest Parliament in the
world. The action of the Commissioner of Customs in
sending men from another State to take charge of our
Custom-house was a menace and degradation to us. It
was the act Of a despot, and one which in times gone by
would have provoked rebellion. Throughout the discus-
sion on the Federal tariff it was made apparent that the
chief aim of the framers and the supporters of the tariff
was to cripple New South Wales. The jealousy against
this State is deep-rooted and of longstanding ; and it was
the knowledge of that fact which deterred so many of us
from voting for Federation when the plebiscite was taken.
It would require too much of your space to enumerate the
many acts of the Federal Parliament which have been in-
sidiously directed against this State ; but I may instance
the determination, almost openly expressed, to flout New
South Wales and cheat her out of her rights by estab-
lishing the Federal Capital in Melbourne '
* » • •
This is the sort of poor weeping unmanlmess which
has always characterised a certain parochial class
in N.S.W., and by which that class has done its
best to create the impression that the Cornstalk
is npt quite half a man, but only a wailing
apology. A considerable amount of such litera
ture infests the press just now, but this particular
moan, which is fairly typical of all the rest, may
be answered in a few words :
(1) There is not a single instance quoted to show where
the “jealous hatred of N.S.W.” has manifested itself.
N.S.W. certainly sent sundry parochially-minded dele-
gates to the Federal Parliament, who are always whining
about some “ wrongs” or other, but no concrete evidence
of these wrongs has ever been put forward. Perhaps the
great efforts made by the Victorian members to establish
large ironworks in N.S.W. (Victoria and the other States
paying most of the bonus) is what is meant.
(2) Not a word about any evidence of a desire to
“ cripple the industries of N.S.W.” (unless the effort to
establish N.S.W. ironworks is meant) appears in the
Federal debates, and the writers of letters like the above
couldn’t produce that evidence if the salvation of their
poor little souls depended on it.
(3) N.S.W. supplies the Premier and two other mem-
bers of the Federal Ministry, and in proportion to popula-
tion, it is far better represented in that Ministry than
Victoria is. It also supplies the leader and deputy’leader
of the Opposition, so it has the prospect of supplying the
next as well as the present Premier. The other States—
with their alleged “jealous hatred” and all the other
trash—cheerfully follow the N.S.W. leaders—Freetraders
and Protectionists alike. The N.S.W. parochialists
wouldn’t be satisfied unless their State had the lead in
everything, and the other States gave way.
(4) N.S.W. gets exactly the same tariff as Victoria and all
the other States. It can live and prosper under the same
conditions as they do, unless it is claimed that its people
are more helpless than other Australians.
(5) The “ fodder duties” grievance, about which so
much has been said, was a lie and a sham from the
beginning. If N.S.W. wanted to import fodder free, it is
its own State Government which gets the duties, and it is
its own State Government which refuses to refund them.
If N.S.W. won’t make its State Government refund the
duties, then it evidently doesn’t want to import the
fodder free.
* * * *
(6) The Federal Parliament has been sitting a little over
a year, and almost all that time was taken up by the
stonewalling of the tariff by a knot of N.S.W. importers
and their agents, which is quite sufficient reason why
nothing much has been done about fixing a Federal
capital in N.S.W. It was never agreed that the child who
cried for the moon was to get it first thing, and N.S.W.
never stipulated that the Federal capital was to be built
in its territory instantly, before even the Federal Govern-
ment knew what revenue it could count upon.
(7) Despite all the wild and whirling talk about the
“ party ” which wants to permanently fix the Federal
capital in Melbourne, it is impossible to point to a single
politician or paper demanding anything of the kind.
That “ party ” is about the silliest jim-jam that was
ever evolved by a diseased and childish imagination.
(8) The Federal tariff certainly raises more revenue
than the old N.S.W. State tariff did. And N.S.W.
rendered that additional revenue necessary by granting
old age pensions without taxing itself to provide them.
N.S.W. gets credit for granting old age pensions, and a
section of its people curses the Federal Government for
finding the money to pay them.
(9) And the great grievances seem to be that the Gov.-
General had to reside chiefly in Melbourne while Parlia-
ment was sitting (it has been sitting not quite one
session). Also that certain customs house officials,
released from duty in other States, were sent to Sydney
instead of extra local men being appointed to swell the
cost of Government while these old hands were pensioned
off. No N.S.W. men were sacked to make room for
them, and the sin was exactly the same as when N.S.W.
sent men to Broken Hill or Bourke, or sent a N.S.W.
Premier to Melbourne. By sending these men over
N.S.W. gets some extra population and some extra tax-
payers.
• * * *
The parochial-minded gang in N.S.W. is only a
handful, but it makes enough row to constitute
itself a public disgrace. It was this gang which
insisted that the poor, sea-sifck and broken-down
Gov.-General, who wanted badly to get ashore at
the first port, should come right on, so that its
petty-minded vanity could be gratified by having
the first official landing at Sydney, a piece of
childishness of which a naked savage might
be ashamed. To its diseased soul everything
that the Federal Ministry does or can pos-
sibly do is only another evidence of a dark con-
spiracy to steal N.S.W.’s deficit, or something.
N.S.W. has got a far larger share, in proportion
to population, of Ministerial power than Victoria.
Its men lead both the Government and the
Opposition. It has more representatives than
Victoria. It has the same tariff as the other
States. It has, so far, secured the lion’s share of
the Federal money expended on public works.
It was only by the bitter opposition of its own
representatives that the Federal Parliament was
prevented taxing Victoria and the other States
heavily to pay a bonus for the establishment of
iron works in N.S.W., from which works N.S.W.
would have reaped practically all the profit, and
Kingston, of S.A., and Turner and Deakin, of
Victoria, fought hard to confer this boon on
N.S.W. The Federal Government proposed to
vote money to enable the Gov.-General to keep
up state and pomp in Sydney, and was reviled
for it by the Sydney Freetrade papers. It
went round, almost the first thing, inspecting
sites for a Federal capital in N.S.W., but
it didn’t build that capital in a night—and it
couldn’t be expected to. And N.S.W. refused to
help itself, as it had absolute power to do, in the
matter of the fodder duties, and its parochial con-
spirators cursed the Federal Govt, because N.S.W.
wouldn’t help itself. And each of these things
made the parochial cranks madder, till their
diseased mental condition has beeomq an absolute
danger to the community, and they ought to be
locked up in a cool public-house till further
notice.
At Poverty Point.
“ H.P.”: The drama holds the mirror up to
Nature? There is hardly any modern play without a
woman fainting. How many readers of this paper have
ever seen a woman faint under stress of emotion merely ?
Has one ? Mark how sparing Shakespeare is of this cheap
device. Rosalind faints at the sight of blood. I do not
know that there is one faint in Shakespeare, except with
a sensible physical explanation
“ G.” : A great institution in the ’Frisco theatrical
world is the “ amateurs’ night.” Once a
year a leading music-hall is given up to
the amateurs of the city who aspire to
professional engagements. Big audi-
ences are the rule, and the performers
have to submit themselves to their
judgment. Some aspirants don’t get be-
yond the first bar of their songs ere a
ioud howl from the audience drives them
off the stage. Others are not so bad,
but great fun is got out of the
show by the guying to which
they are subjected. On the
other hand, the show is of
much value to youngsters
of talent who have been
wasting their cake-walks on
the desert air. Why doesn’t
some Australian enterprising
manager introduce the idea
here? [Possibly because,
owing to the fact that 33 i
per cent, of the adult
population are aspirants
for the stage, the per-
formance would have to
start 8 p.m. July Ist and
end with the financial
year.]
Jack S—, of the dismal
city of Newcastle, is the
Commonwealth’s champion
practical joker. Not long
ago he took three seats in
the dress-circle of a Sydney
theatre. The curtain rose,
but the “circle" was only
conscious of a rough figure
seated in the front row be-
tween two respectable ladies.
His coat was buttoned to the
neck, and had an extraordinary
bulge all down the left side.
He sat quietly—biding his time .
—until every opera-glass was
focussed in liis direction. Then,
very leisurely, he pulled out a gigantic
galvanised-iron telescope, shook it out
over the balcony till it was 6ft. long,
and steadily returned the fire of a
haughty old dowager in one of the
boxes. Long Tom’s victory was com-
plete-gallery and stalls roared and the dress-circle
quivered with indignation as it hid away its opera-glass
lest it should “ draw fire.”
Sarah Bernhardt opened her recent season in London
with a “ Francesca de Rimini ” play, written for her by
F. Marion Crawford, and translated into French by some-
body else. The experiment was just moderately success-
ful. Crawford’s “situations” designed for Sarah are
said to be more tragic than convincing.
The probably untruthful anecdote about Mrs. Pat
Campbell and her stage carpenter is not a bad one.
During her American tour Mrs. Pat made a point of
getting photo’d always with a valuable poodle in her lap.
She went nowhere without the notorious poodle, also her
nerves were turned to advt. account. She suffered so
terribly from nerves that the roadway in front of the
N.Y. theatre where she was appearing had to be covered
with tan bark. At the end of her profitable tour, says
the story, Mrs. Pat asked the stage carpenter whether he
or she had been accountable for the success. The hard,
uncompromising scene-fixer grunted—“ Neither, it was
the dog and the tan bark.”
“P.”: The “ Autobiog. of Olara Morris” is the
best of all the theatrical books. Mary Anderson, in a
preface, says “ This rings true,” and so it does. A story
of pluck crowned with triumph. “ Even Shakespeare
would be delighted with this,” is my thought. All the
grace of the Bancrofts, with infinitely more spirit. There
is not a more delightful figure in Clara’s magic pages
than Wilkes Booth. Her first N.Y. appearance is the
exact counterpart of Edmund Kean’s in London. There
is the same contempt, by a brilliant and fashionable
theatrical co., for the dingy new-comer. She achieves an
overwhelming success, and they are all at her feet.
Dear Bulletin.—First night of “ Iris” at Sydney Royal
I sat down on the tail of a lady’s dress. I did it on
purpose, with the fixed intention of tearing the dress,
and lam sorry that it didn’t tear more than it did. I
didn’t apologise to the lady, and don’t contemplate doing
so. I had a d.c. seat at the end of a row. Just after the
play had commenced, six people came down the gangway
rustling loudly and talking considerably, and wanted to
get past me to reach their seats. I stepped out into the
gangway to facilitate things. Then the elderly gentle-
man remarked that he would go in first and the four
ladies could sit together, and , Ferdinand on this side of
them. The eldest lady thought it would be better if
Ferdinand sat in the middle. I was still waiting. She
started to go in, but drew back, and said Ethel
Maude had better take the end seat. Ferdinand
was non - committal. I was still waiting. Then
she decided that she would take the end seat, and
the old gentleman could sit next her, and Ethel Maude
could sit next to Muriel, and Ferdinand could sit between
Muriel and Gwendoline Florence Henrietta. Ferdinand
only looked tired. I was still waiting. The lady who was
with me grew interested, and tried hard not to laugh.
The old lady went into her seat, telling Ethel Maude to
follow her. Ethel Maude offered the seat to Muriel, say-
ing it was the best seat, and one where the view was not
obstructed by a pillar. The absolute indifference of
Ferdinand almost amounted to chloroform. I was still
waiting. The old gentleman proceeded to get in, came
back for programmes, and fell over Ferdinand. I was
growing unpopular by this time for blocking the
view. When he captured the programmes and gave
one to Ethel Maude, and one to Muriel, and one
to Gwendoline Florence Henrietta, he really got in.
Muriel went next. 1 was still waiting. Then Gwendo-
line Florence Henrietta said that she would go next, and
Ferdinand would follow, so as to sit between her and Ethel
Maude. Ferdinand’s uninterested condition amounted
to a disease, and he should have taken something for it.
I continued to wait. Gwendoline Florence Henrietta
really got in, which left in the gangway only myself,
Ferdinand and Ethel Maude. Then Gwendoline Florence
Henrietta came out again, and Said Ethel Maude had
better take that seat. By this time the party had
crawled back and forward in front of my' lady
companion about six times. Ethel Maude went in
and sat down. This left, in the gangway, myself, Fer-
dinand and Gwendoline Florence Henrietta. Ferdinand
struggled along next and took his seat. I was still wait-
ing. The party in the gangway was reduced to myself
and Gwendoline Florence Henrietta. Gwendoline went
in. She stood up and hesitated right in front of my lady
friend, and with the train of a dress that must have cost
£25 festooned over my seat, and opined that, after all, it
would be better if she sat on the other side of Ferdinand.
Then I deliberately sat on the flowing train of Gwendoline
Florence Henrietta, and put both my feet on it also, and
when she started to move again it naturally tore. It
didn’t tear half so much as it should have done, but it
providentially tore. Gwendoline Florence Henrietta
looked at me, in a tone of thunder and lightning. I
looked at her, u ith an expression which was intended to
convey that if Ferdinand wanted satisfaction 1 would
gladly shed his blood at the first interval. Then Muriel
and Ethel Maude changed seats, and Ferdinand decided
to sit between them, and things began to settle down.—
Yours profanely, Titus Salt.
The Hylands, who follow Victorian country race-
meetings with a circus, of which trained horses are a
strong feature, have been reared in the atmosphere of
hoop-la life. Twelve years ago “ Professor ” Hyland,
sire of the constellation, eked out a living, riding
buck jumpers and bullocks in a tent, while the
missus, with one eye on the kids and another on the
“beans,” kept the door. “Dad” Hyland followed his
bent, and now he has a tidy, satisfying family show,
which he advertises by running some of his 1 ‘ neddies ” in
the chief events at the local races, often landing a pot.
Most of the “circus” nominations are in the name of
Miss Hyland, who is the star of the Hyland arena, in
tights and spangles ; and Hostage, a grey by Wellesley,
obtained place of honor in the Australasian “ stud
book ” as having started oftener than any other horse last
season. Hostage faced the starter no less than 51 times,
and caught the judge’s eye 12 times for his fair owner.
Tennyson and O’Gorman once toured Australia’s
music.,halls as a “team.” While they were here the
poet Tennyson “ crossed the bar,” and the news was
spoken of in a dressing-room of Sydney Tivoli. “My
word,” said a well-known coster-depictor, “ what’ll
O’Gorman do now ? ”

made in the year 1658, with reflections much upon the
late times; and it being the first time the pay was
doubled, and so to save money, my wife and I went into
the gallery, and there sat and saw very well.” They
always did it, in every age. ’ 1

POLITICAL
POINTS
Net result of Mr. G. H. Reid’s recent
Victorian country tour was one new branch of the
Freetrade League, with Jive members—three
squatters, one draper and one undertaker.
If people didn’t realise before to what
condition politics had come in S. Australia they
ought to realise it now when the Opposition leader
proposes to abolish free education. After all,
worse things might happen than for Darling to
have his way. If anything would finish the Tory
party that would.
The S. Australian Jenkins Iscariot can
only command a majority so long as he sticks to
the Opposition’s policy. When, recently, he forgot
himself and proposed to take over a foundry, his
Minister in the Leg. Council could command only
three votes besides his own—and it looked as
though he would almost as soon have put his own
on the other side.
N.S. W. public is being gradually prepared
for a disconcerting report on its “ magnificent
railway system.” It is now announced that there
will shortly have to be voted a very large sum to
bring the supply of locomotives up to a proper
standard of efficiency, and there are rumors of
great arrears of repairs to be caught up, and such-
like matters.
The Owe’Sullivan got three Western
railwav Bills (Cobar to Wilcannia; Bogan Gate
to Bulbodney ; and Temora to Barellan) through
all their difficult stages in N.S.W. Assembly one
night last week. No one knows for certain where
the money is to come from, but the Owe’Sullivan
reckons that will be managed somehow. Anyhow,
his eye is fixed westward and Australia is Facing
the Dust.
The Federal Iron Bonus Bill, after many
vicissitudes, has been referred to a select commit-
tee to be talked to death. The votes of the
N.S.W. members supplied the majority which
practically wrecked this chance of creating a great
N.S.W. industry ; though Victoria would have to
find a good part of the bonus, while N.S.W. got
the benefit of the industry, the Victorians voted
pretty solidly against hangingthe business up. The
opinion generally expressed was that there was no
special hurry. If the next £3,000,000 loan doesn’t
fioat, however, it will become apparent that there
is a great deal of hurry.
Another objection of the Victorian Fatman
to the Factories Act has been knocked endways
by some just-published census returns. Said
Fatman wearies the public ear by his perpetual
declamations against the iniquity of an Act that
restricts the employment of boys and drives them
on to the street corners. The 1901 census shows
that there were in Victoria 72,890 boys between
11 and 20, against 299,336 men between 21 and 65
—barely one boy to four men. Now in no trade
is less than one boy employee to four men
allowed. In many trades the proportion of
employment is fixed at one boy to every three
men, while in some there is no restriction what-
ever.
THE IDENTIFICATION OF BRITAIN WITH THE LOST TRIBES OF THE HOUSE
OF ISRAEL. (AN AUSTRALIAN VIEW.)
A Glebe alderman told the Greater Sydney
Parliamentary Committee last week that a vote
taken in his municipality showed that the great
majority was against joining the Greater Sydney
movement. A ballot-paper, said to be the one
used in connection with that vote, was published
recently. It contained a list of imaginary
and ridiculous horrors that were to fall on the
Glebe if it joined Greater Sydney, and a wholly
unreliable statement that the Parliamentary in-
quiry had proved these imaginary horrors to be
real, which isn’t the sort of thing that one expects
to find on an unbiassed ballot-paper. If a Glebe
alderman, when he went up for re-election, hap-
pened to find the municipal ballot-paper adorned
with baseless statements that he was an infidel and
a swindler, by way of inducing the ratepayers to
vote for the other man, he might possibly see the
point.
The Commonwealth Parliament has con-
ducted the most important business of its existence
during the past year in the absence of the Premier
and the leader of the Opposition.
The Tasmanian Assembly looks with a
tired eye on Lewis’s new Parliamentary Reform
scheme, and says that it doesn’t matter whether
it is passed or not, because the Leg. Council will
throw it out in any case. Not one voice is
luted to suggest that the Council had better not
throw it out or there will be trouble. They don’t
know what trouble means in Tas. Assembly, and
don t expect to find out.
S.A. Council” of Churches declares itse’f
strongly opposed to the Amended Tote Bill. S.A.
badly wants the money that Amended Tote Bill
will yield, but still if it abolished all the privi-
leges extended to the churches in the way of
exemption from taxes, and levied 5 per cent, on
the collection, it might make good the loss. Then
the lote could be abolished, and things amicably
hxed up. But so long as the greedy churches try to
dodge their fair share of taxation it should not
object when the gap in the revenue is filled by
a lote-tax.
The Adelaide lawyers who applauded at
the onslaught of their leader, “Johnny ” Gordon,
on the alleged loafing in the Govt, workshops, got
their pill when Judge Boucaut announced that the
Hall divorce-case, which had been spun out for 19
days, and wasn’t nearly finished then, should have
lasted only 12 days. When a lawyer spreads 12
days’ work over 19, and still isn’t near the end of
it, he loafs a darned sight worse than the Islington
workmen were accused of doing.
Treasurer Waddell, being asked the other
night in N.S.W. Assembly if any part of the
revenue from the resumed area of Sydney had
been set apart as a sinking fund towards meet-
ing claims, replied, like a man who speaks
regretfully of the evil deeds of a third party,
that there was no provision for anything
of the kind in the Act. It sounded much
better that way than if he said that the Govern-
ment to which he belonged had taken darned good
care to put no such provision in the Act.
There will be presently a healthy row
between Sydney and Brisbane over the Clarence
and Richmond River trade. These districts are
closer to Brisbane than to Sydney. They have
now steamboat communication with both cities,
and they may before long have a railway to Bris-
bane—a good part of the line is built already.
Then tire trouble will begin. ‘ ‘ The highest point
to which human hatred can reach,” says historian
Freeman,” has commonly been found in the local
antipathy between neighboring cities.”
Melb. Argus gravely asserts that the fact
of 14 Vic. Leg. Councillors being returned, the
other day, without opposition, proves that the
House of 48 Rich Landlords has the full con-
fidence of the people. The Council has a grand
opportunity to show that it knows it possesses the
full confidence of the people by allowing its con-
stituents to choose any one of their own number
to represent them. Then, if they still returned
the old gang, the Council would have some con-
fidence to be proud of.
N.S.W. Assembly lately spent two con-
secutive nights asking why tl'ie police don’t cap-
ture more criminals. The most visible reason is
that the police have had so many other duties put
upon them, because the State is too mean to pay
for these other duties, that only about a third or a
half of the very small force is commonly available
for police work. Another is that a patriarchal
bobby of 60 or 65 (retained in the force after the
proper pension age because the Government stole
the pension fund which the police themselves sub-
scribed) often can’t overtake a spry criminal aged
25. There are more reasons, but these are two to
go on with.

MRS. NOWED : “Are there no more faithful

MRS. THINGORTWO : “ Oh, there must be — lots.

The bitterest enemy of the old age pension
is the elderly cookie. He used to get his tinker-
ing and odd jobs, running up to 60 hours a week,
done by patriarchs of 65 and 70 at a couple of
shillings a week and a bite. Patriarchs now ask
7s. 6d., or other princely remuneration, and cockie
sheds indignant tears.
Tasmanian Premier Lewis has now about
as distinct an idea of where he is as if he had been
put in a bag and whirled round three times, and
then left in the middle of a deep forest at mid-
night. The Assembly threw out his first Reform
Bill. Then it threw out his house-tax. Then,
after a long struggle it decided, by a majority of
one to re-consider the house-tax. Then the Upper
House smote the new Land Assessment Bill
hard. Now the Upper House is thinking whether
it will smite the second Reform Bill. A fine old
pirate like Dibbs of N.S.W. would ere this have
told both Houses that they were unfit to carry tripe
to a bear and thrown his resignation at them, but
Lewis plods on. The way of the reformer (for
Lewis is really trying to do some good work) is
hard, unless he is a very strong reformer. Tnen
the other fellow’s way is hard.
In its issue of 25th Aug. S.M. Herald
disgustedly pointed out that while the customs
revenue collected in N.S.W. was 455. 4d. per
inhabitant, it was only 395. 2d. in S.A. and
38s. sd. in Victoria, and spoke thus :
It has always been supposed that Victoria and South
Australia are the States which, by their high duties, have
fostered manufactures. As revenue-producers the com-
parison they make with the other States is not to their
advantage.
It might be suggested, as a simple rudimentary
fact, that a State which had “fostered manufac-
tures,” being able to supply itself with many more
things than another State might, would need to
import less, and would consequently pay less
import duties. If the S.M. Herald could only
realise that there are other tests of prosperity
besides the quantity of bales and boxes dragged
by low-priced labor off a foreign ship on to a
wharf that is built with borrowed money, it might
get a clearer view of the situation.
A new wail from the Free-
trade, Anti-Tax, Natural and
Unnatural Association people is
“Why do the world’s emigrants
go anywhere but to Australia?”
Perhaps one reason is that if a
man comes here with his wife and
three children (five in all) they at
once become responsible fora debt
of £26o—their share of the public
liabilities. Another may be that
there is very little for an immi-
grant to do, save “ go on the land ”
—almost the least productive land
on earth—and send the produce to
England, there being no manufac-
turing population of any size to
sell it to. Why the iron-worker,
cotton - spinner, woollen - spinner,
brass-worker, tin-worker, copper-
founder, silk-spinner, shipwright,
&c. don’tcomehere should be plain
enough. They, go where there is
plenty of work for them in their
own industries.
Hawkers’ licenses in N.S. W.
number 1668 ; of these, according
to official statement, 747, or nearly
half, are held by Assyrians and
other aliens. Hawking was once
the second line of Australian pioneering, but the
business is being eaten out of the hands of the old-
time white man.
Cowley, the Queensland nigger-labor mem-
ber, wants to sell the railways as a means of
making things hum up North, and he alleges that
there would be no difficulty in getting American
capitalists to buy the lot. If Cowley reads up the
terms that American companies demanded for
making railways in very much better country
(they received an area equal to half Queensland,
with all minerals thrown in, also cash assistance)
it will probably dawn on bun that his imaginary
American capitalist would want something for
taking the Queensland railways at a gift. Victoria
imported an American expert once to tell how to
make its lines pay, and all he could suggest was
to write off a lot of the capital as lost.
Though downed for the
time being, the Vic. public
servant has many opportuni-
-1 -s for quiet revenge against
th Kyabramapootras and
faming community gener-
ally for making him
sweat for their misdo-
ings. Already there are
signs of the get-back.
Viticultural expert Du-
bois t’other day called
Minister Taverner’s at-
tention to the handsome
way in which Vic. vigne-
rons are treated by con-
trast with other States.
Govt, has been supply-
ing them for some years
past with phylloxera-
resistant vines gratis,
whilst N.S.W. derived
a satisfactory revenue
from that source. Also,
whenever a couple of cows
or horses die, the Govt.-
dependent farmer immediately
demands that a departmental
veterinary shall be sent to give him
free advice. He gets his agricultural
journal, a very costly publication, supplied to him
for the asking, and nearly everything that he
wants, outside ordinary farm labor and the
necessary implements, is provided by Govt.
The Public Accounts Committee ap-
pointed by N.S.W. Parliament to intervene in
the wars of the State Treasurer and Auditor-
General, being M’s.L.A., will (at first) draw no
pay for their services. But it is already hinted
that they will be allowed expenses. The Public
Works Committee at its inception was a gratis
body, but expenses followed and fees followed ex-
Eenses. It is true that if a man works he should
e paid, but why not say so right off ?
The N.S.W. system of appointing the
local grocer, or more often still the local commis-
sion agent, to be district coroner gives some pretty
bad results. In mining districts it shows up
worst. The influential mining manager may be
“ practically on his trial,” and if that is so the
man who conducts the trial ought not to be
dependent for his living on the favor of either the
local bosses or the local workmen. Also, when
an assertive lawyer, and especially one with a
reputation, bumps up against tire lay coroner, not
very confident about the law of evidence and other
things, the lay coroner too often goes down and
takes justice with him.
A Bill is now before Tas. Parliament,
whereby it is proposed to repeal an old law which
has remained on the statute book, and which
makes sheep-stealing punishable by death. The
“prevalence of sheep-stealing in the island” has
long been the standing topic of conversation among
certain indignant old gentlemen of the Upper
House; and when they were suddenly informed
that the death-penalty had existed all these years,
and that somebody might have been hanged for
acquiring an ancient sheep, they seemed quite
angry over lost opportunities, and were only re-
strained, with much difficulty, from voting against
the repeal of the obsolete statute.
N.S.W. Upper House item :
Mr. Backhouse considered that machinery was respon-
sible for throwing people out of work, and what they
wanted was to reconstruct society on a co-operative
basis. He believed the unemployed should ask the
Government to provide them with work with no uncer-
tain voice.
This denunciation of machinery sounds like a
voice from the Dark Ages. Fancy abolishing
machinery—which includes railways, tramways,
factories, shipyards, foundries, ’ steam-power,
hydraulic power, &c.— to improve the condition
of the unemployed ! We want co-operation with
the best machinery that the world can produce—
not co-operation on aboriginal principles.
Extract from that energetic Free trade
paper, Launceston Examinee :
According to a paragraph appearing in the Maoriland
papers, a country storekeeper there recently ordered a
dozen dog-collars from a. Wellington firm. When de-
livered, the goods were found to be of German manufac-
ture, and the storekeeper, being of sturdy British pro-
clivities, promptly returned 11 to the suppliers. The
twelfth he sent to the Right Hon. Joseph Chamberlain,
with a polite note calling attention to the fact, and
asking why British articles of the kind cou-ld not be
obtained on this side of the globe. A courteous reply, it
is said, has since come to hand from the Colonial Secre-
tary, intimating that the matter had been passed on to
the Birmingham Chamber of Commerce, also a letter
from that body saying that investigations were being
made. Now that Great Britain has tied up the dogs of
war, she should be free to turn attention to four-footed
prowlers in colonial back yards.
The newest version of Freetrade is evidently that
our ports should be open to everybody’s goods,
but if anybody except Bull is found sending goods
in Joe Chamberlain should declare war at once.
The final reply of the Federal Government
to the demand for the abolition of the fodder
duties has just come to light. It reads as fol-
lows :
The matter is really one which is in the hands of the
State Government. The money received from these
duties collected by the Federal Government is returned l
to the States, whose Governments can employ it in
affording relief to those in distress. The case is one to
be met by State action without the intervention of the
Federal Government, whose action, if any, must apply to.
the whole Commonwealth. It is not considered neces-
sary that such action, extending to the whole Common-
wealth, should be taken when the relief required can be-
most readily, fully, and fitly obtained by State action.”
Which is solid truth. See, of N.S.W., and Philp,
of Queensland, get the fodder duty revenue of
their own States. If they refund it to the im-
porters, then there are no fodder duties in N.S.W.
or Queensland. If they won’t refund it, then
they either reckon their States don’t require free
fodder imports, or they don’t care.
The difference ! The Canadian and Aus-
tralian Governments recently decided that they
wanted new rifles for their forces. Canada set
about getting them in her own way, with best
advantage to herself, and quite regardless as to
what the Joe Chamberlain Office might thmkabout
ihe matter. The result is that the Canadian forces
are now armed with the new Metford-Ross rifle--
1 iter, lighter and shorter than the Lee-Metford—
which is manufactured within the Dominion. The
inventor established a factory in Canada, the
Government guaranteeing a certain demand. Mr.
Ross, the part-inventor, also offered to supply the-
Commonwealth forces with his new rifle from
Canada, or, if the Federal Govt, would guarantee
a certain demand, as in Canada, to establish a
small-arms factory in Australia, and supply the
local demand locally. The Australian Govt,
couldn’t entertain this idea of encouraging a new
and absolutely necessary local industry, but
humbly and dutifully sent its order and ks
£25,000 cheque to the Imperial War Office. Per-
haps when the Imperial troops are re-armed,
within the next decade or so, we will receive a
few shiploads of the cast-off, obsolete stock in ful.
filment of our order, as heretofore.

The passing of the beard. According to
British Medical Journal, the beard is doomed
to disappear everywhere, except in New Zion,
where Prophet Dowie compels all men to wear chin-
hair. In Germany the beard is denounced as a
fertile source of infection during operations ; and
William the Terrible won’t allow any doctor of
his to wear a beard. In New York the Milk Com-
mission has ordered that hereafter smoothfaced
men only shall be employed for milking cows and
delivering milk, because dust from the stable is
liable to infect the beard and thus the milk.
Baldness will become compulsory next.

Cured by

Shepparton, July 81,1899.
Mr. S. A PALMER (VITADATIO).
Dear Sir,—l have very great pleasure in certifying to

Colonel Cameron, of Tasmania, as de-
scribed in a letter from London :
He preceded his men to London by a week, in order to
make all arrangements for their reception, and he had
sent word that he was coming. When he did come he
was, so to speak, snubbed. But he is not the man to be
snubbed, and so he wrote to the powers that be and re-
ported the man who had tried to fend him off. He got a
reply in due course, and was told to see General Cooke,
who was O.C. colonial contingents at Alexandra Palace.
Cameron went to him by appointment, and was kept
waiting half-an-hour. Then he left and reported the
General to the War Office. Then the General ordered
him to appear before him, and Cameron said that
“ order ” was not the word to use to the representative
of the Commonwealth forces, and that he refused to
recognise General Cooke’s authority. Then Lieutenant-
Colonel Penton, the chief of staff, met Cameron and said
that the General would like to see him. Cameron said he
would not see the General, and Penton attempted to bully
Cameron, who turned on Penton with a look so fierce and
with words so cutting that Penton fled out of the room.
Then there was a great review before the Prince of Wales
and the Queen. And Major-General Trotter was Chief of
Staff to the Duke of Connaught. And he was worried
as the Australian line was not quite straight, so he
attempted to dress the Australians without consulting
Cameron, and Cameron yelled, “Don’t move, men, with-
out my order; I command you! ” and Trotter said,
“ Move into line,” and Cameron said, “ Don’t dare move,
men.” Then said Trotter to Cameron, “ Another word,
sir, and I will order you off the parade ! ” And Cameron
said, “ Don’t move, men, till I give the order.” Then
Trotter rushed off towards the Duke of Connaught and
the others, but checked himself and reined up, being, as
it were, in a state of temporary insanity. Cameron com-
plained to Barton and Forrest, and these two, being in
the toils of Royalty and in the glamour of greatness, de-
serted Cameron in his hour of need; but he refused to
back down one inch. The news spread to the Prince of
Wales, the Duke of Connaught, and all the big guns of
Parliament—Chamberlain, Brodrick, and others —who
were all against Cameron. Still he refused to budge.
And they consulted with Lord Roberts, and Lord Roberts
sent an urgent telegram for Cameron to come and see
him. And Cameron saw him, and the Commander-in-
Chief made a full apology, and wrote a letter to Barton
to say that, whoever was in the wrong, Colonel Cameron
was right.

“ J.F.D.”: Recently met a female “bottle-
oh ”in a Sydney suburb. She was doing a roaring
biz., and confided to me that she had the pull of
the man “bottle-oh,” inasmuch as she wore her
skirts so thick and long that no dog could bite her
legs. Dog-bite is one of the chief drawbacks to
the second-hand bottle-trade.

The Wonder of the Age.
ALL CHEMISTS
THROUGHOUT
AUSTRALASIA.
The Inky Way.
At various times attempts have been made in Australia
to found a Journalists’ Union, always without success.
The various daily newspaper proprietaries, whilst com-
peting, with great apparent eagerness, among themselves
for the public patronage, are firmly united in resisting
any movement which would prevent “sweating” in the
profession. Proposals for the formation of “ clubs ” and
“ institutes ” J(with managers and directors as guides,
patrons, and officers) are always welcomed, but each of
the attempts to form a genuine “ union ” among press-
men has been followed by a prompt “ victimising " of the
chief organisers. But an effort to establish a Journalists’
Union, in N.S.W. at least, should be made now, since
the Arbitration Court would protect its officers and mem-
bers from any invidious action on the part of newspaper
bosses. There is certainly great need for such an
organisation. Wages are trending steadily downward ;
the employment of youths and “improvers” to do
reporters’ work at a fourth of a reporter’s proper remu-
neration increases yearly; and the work expected of a
man becomes more and more arduous. And aside from
the need of union in those directions, there is a call for a
body which will have authority to define the status of
journalism, and weed out the numerous parasites and
cadgers who degrade the calling.
There would, of course, be some difficulties in the way
of a journalists’ union, more particularly as regards
classification. But a division into three classes (a) editors
and leader-writers, (b) sub-editors and senior reporters,
and (c) junior reporters, would be fairly easy. Some
trades unions classify and provide minimum wage rates
for a dozen or more different branches of a particular
industry, and what a Boot Trade Union or a
Tailors’ Union can do should surely be within the
compass of a Journalists’ Union. But, of course, that’s not
the real difficuity. Even among such knowledgable men
as journalists there is a good deal of snobbery and the
idea that a union is not “ genteel ” has stood in the way
of organisation hitherto, though, Heaven knows, there is
enough of grievance to impress the necessity for it.
At present staff members are almost absolutely
at the mercy of the proprietors; some of the
big dailies have an understanding among them-
selves _ not to “ steal one another’s men," which
means in effect that a man dissatisfied with his salary on
a “great daily" in Sydney can’t get a show on the
allied papers in Melbourne, Adelaide, or Brisbane.
All the competing newspapers of Australia are per-
manently united in importing cables and condensing
salaries ; occasionally groups of them sink all differences
to protest against a paper-duty or to engineer a postal or
telegraphic concession. If the bosses can have a union,
why should not the men ?
* * * *
S.M. Herald, which (ye gods!) now actually has a
column headed “ Personal Items," has otherwise fallen
into line with the rest of the big dailies and prints
occasional illustrations. It was during the Federal cam-
paign that Editor Willoughby first put illustrations in
the Argus. Several outraged subscribers at once stopped
the paper. “ Kew Asylum is full of gibbering old men,
who can only rock themselves all day, muttering
softly to themselves, ‘My Argus has got pictures 1 My
Argus has got pictures 1’ ’’ used to be a favorite joke of
Editor Willoughby. And the jest had a foundation of
truth: the Argus illustrations were enough to drive a
man to drink or idiocy.
• • • •
Brotherly love was warm during the Torrey-Alexander
mission between a N.S.W. and a Vic. religious printing-
office. Sydney Christian World issued a penny daily
edition of the proceedings ; the Vic. Southern Cross
issued a 3d. one. The S.O. printed a huge poster saying
that one of the principals recommended it ; whereupon
the C.W. got the same signature recommending it, and
also besought the N.S.W. people not to patronise Vic-
torian opposition. The Eatonswill element, by the way,
is almost absent nowadays from the Australian press-
excepting the “ religious” rags.
» * • •
Mr. Jabez Packer, religious editor of Sydney D.T. and
Baptist local-preacher, was most of the brains behind the
recent Simultaneous Mission boom. The Yanks paid him
£7 a week and expenses to travel around the continent
with them and “work the press.” Mr. Packer had his
first training under Maiden Tribute W. T. Stead, and so
knows how to exploit the “business” side of holiness.
He’s a clever paragraphist, and makes his religious column
interesting with occasional items which are almost
worthy of the smoking-room.
* » • •
The Jury (Sydney), whose main purpose in life was to
abuse John Norton, is dead. John always had a winning
way with the jury.
* • • •
There’s nobody more pitiful than the man who wants to
get his name put in the paper ; unless it’s the man who
wants it kept out.
• v • *
Our famous men, as they are known in S’Africa—extract
from a Mafeking paper : “ Owing to protracted drought,
the Hon. A. Beakin, the Attorney-General of the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth, communicated with Hon. K. John,
Secretary to the Premier of New South Wales, regarding
the proposal to suspend grain and fodder duties in all the
States.”
• * * *
“Revilo”: Maoriland journals have been making an
all-round advance lately. Tri-weeklies are merging into
dailies, and bi-weeklies into tri-weeklies; linos, and monos,
are installed in offices, and so on. Staid, conservative
Auckland Herald is now a penny (Coronation memento
said to be). It is.the last of the Australasian big sheets
to knuckle down to the modest “ brown.” N.Z. Times
has run up a big three-storeyed building and put in up-to-
date machines. The Mail (the Times weekly) is at last to
be illustrated. Since Editor Kelly has been at the helm,
the Times has made rapid progress. Another change in
management recently (about the 50th during the last
decade). “Monty” Bannister is now in charge. The
Bannisters have stuck to the Times through thick and
thin, and put not a few dollars into the concern.
* • * *
The petty spirit of which the Argus can be capable,
was shown in a recent paragraph calling attention to the
fact that an allowance for “ tea ” money was being made
to the Federal Hansard men. The general public does
not understand the severe pressure under which Hansard
staff labors, but the pressman who wrote the par. does.
Fact was that the Hansarders were invited to apply for
this allowance in common with the other House officials.
♦ * * *
Labouchere, of London Truth, prides himself on the
number of his libel suits. He has defended 44 actions to
date, of which 19 were abandoned or settled and 17 won.
In some of the eight which he lost damages awarded
were only trifling. Twice Labouchere has been in dock
charged with criminal libel, and in each case he was
acquitted. To win a case he has often found to be expen-
sive. The Zierenberg action, which he won, cost him
£7OOO, and he could recover nothing from the plaintiffs.
» » • *
Illustrations in “ respectable ” weekly papers in Aus-
tralia run more and more on Police Gazette lines ; some
of the Mt. Kembla pictures, for example, and the fre-
quent publication of photographs of semi-nude corpses in
tragedy cases (the Gininderra case to wit).
• » * •
Sydney Sunday Times cable-service is occasionally
marked by an unintelligent anticipation of happenings.
A recent fable in its columns : —“Sunday Times ‘special
message ’ (London, August 16) : ‘ Later in the day the
Boer generals witnessed the great naval review, being
guests of the Government.’ ” And the generals
(treacherous scoundrels !) didn’t attend. The Sunday
Times cables often make one smile.
* * » »
Mr. H. W. Little, who is always writing to the papers
about High Church Anglican matters, is understood (on
poor authority) to have been responsible for converting
John Norton to High Anglicanism. A little, clean-shaven
man, employed in some capacity on Sydney Evening
News, H.W.L. knows all the mysteries of Ritualism, and
all about “ the Fathers ” and the “ Eastern position ” and
the “sacreing” of a king, and has other most curious
knowledge. He seems to be about the only person in
Australia who takes Anglicanism really seriously.
Quite a number of Melb. pressmen, in their anxiety to
get relief from daily-paper drudgery, have taken up land
and started farming on a small scale. As most of them
start with the advantage of expert advice as to “ the lay
of the land," they are not losing by their ventures. Big
“ Tom ” Gilchrist, formerly of the Argos, but now secre-
tary to the Railways Standing Committee, has been
especially successful, and the wines produced on his
small holding, out Whittlesea way, have gained various
prizes in the last few years.
* * * »
Delane has often been cracked up as the most wonder-
ful editor London Times ever had. Yet the day before
war was declared between France and Prussia, he wrote.:
“ We are now, happily, in a position to state that there is
no longer any danger of a war between France and
Prussia.” He neglected to make any provision for the
dispatch of war-correspondents, and the Daily News, in
conjunction with a N.Y. newspaper proprietary, got
immeasurably ahead of the Times in the early stages of
the conflict.
• • • •
Australian print shops are now exhibiting an imported
newspaper illustration of the Coronation, with Salisbury
(who was conspicuous by his absence) showing plainly.
* * * *
Victorian railway employees during the late trouble had
nothing to thank the Melbourne press for. The Age
printed nothing in their favor. The Argus certainly
did give space to a letter or two from their side. Other-
wise the two papers were eager to prejudice a not too
sympathetic public against the employees. Many country
men blame the press for misleading them as to the true
position, and causing them to waver when they should
have been most solid. This was reflected in the ballot-
papers, which was the possible cause of the withdrawing
of the “ strike ” threat.
• • • *
Out of a column of “ Country News," in a Sydney daily,
the other day, no less than eight paragraphs published
this sort of thing : “ Councillor Dash is in a critical state
of health,” “ Presentation of Bible to Miss Sunday
School,” “Ditto of purse to Rev. Mr. Balderdash,”
“ Mailman broke his leg,” “ Councillor’s son fractured his
arm,” and so forth. One has perforce to wade through a
column of common or oaten chaff to get at one small
grain of wheat. Beyond occasional tragedies, the only
country news of general interest refers to the weather
and the crops; the public has absolutely no interest in
the appearance of a pimple on Councillor Smith’s nose,
or the breaking of a Mayor’s wooden leg through falling
off a clothes-horse.
His Choice.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Married ?—and when ? ”
The man flung down his riding-whip, and,
booted and spurred as he was, flung himself on
the couch, with a spurious carelessness that
showed him not at ease. “Three months ago,”
he answered, wearily.
The woman, standing in the centre of the room,
leaned on a chair for support, and went white to
the lips : “ You—married ! Who married you ? ”
“ That’ll do, Sue ! A she-devil; a maniac ; a
putty-faced fool who didn’t know enough to come
in out of the wet. In short, Sue, I’ve been a
damned fool.”
“ And you’ve come back to me?” She laughed
—an ugly little laugh, that he knew of old. “ A
pretty compliment you pay me. You tire of your
lawful wife’s embraces. You come to me. And,
oh ! Walter, I was good to you, wasn’t I ? ”
She laid her hand on his shoulder. He seated
her on the couch beside him. “Yes 1 yes 1 And
you will be again, little woman ? ”
“ That depends.”
“On what?”
“You won’t go back to her any more ? ”
He winced at the direct question. “ I must—
for the sake of appearances.”
“ And yet you love me better ? ”
“Yes.”
She shook her head, half-doubting. “We’ll
see.” She moved towards the chair again. It
was necessary that she should keep from the touch
of his hands. “ Tell me your plans.”
“ I haven’t any, Sue.”
“Shall I tell you the programme you’ve
planned ? ”
“ Go on.”
“You’re going to run a harem of two. I’m to
be handmaiden.”
“ No ! I swear ” he started from his seat.
“Sit there! I want to talk to you. Don’t
come nearer. You intend to keep up appearances
with your respectable wife, who, according to
your statement, is fitter for my position than I
for hers. I lived six months with you. You
never spoke of her. For three months I haven’t
seen you. You come to me when you tire of her,
and you intend to go on—from my house to hers.
Am I nearly right ? ”
“But, Sue, you won’t mind.”
She colored hotly. “ Won’t I? Then some day
she and I will grow old, and the law that knows
men, won’t compel you to keep me from the Bene-
volent, or worse, while she will be rewarded for her
honorable services ! No thanks! Now this is my
plan! You’ve gone-in for respectability—got
married. It hasn’t suited you well. Somehow I
think my case will be better. Anyway I’ll try
it!”
“What?”
‘ ‘ Respectability.
“ Sue ! I’ll stick to you ”
“ I shall follow my lord’s example.”
“Surely not; you’re joking ! I love you, Sue.
You’re glorious compared with her ! I did not
care for her! It was arranged somehow; a
marriage of convenience; and it suited my people.
But you ! Come—l will not let you go ! ”
He had her in his arms and covered her with
passionate kisses. She locked her hands on his
neck instinctively. Then, remembering, she forced
herself away.
“You love me still! ”he said triumphantly.
“ Yes.” She moved to the chair again, her face
growing harder through the necessity for re-
pression.
“What devil’s mood has taken vou to-night,
Sue?”
“I’m qualifying—for—Respectability.”
He looked askance at her. “ You intend to
marry, then ? ”
“Yes.”
“And who is the lucky man ?
“ Guess.”
“Ralston—Haines—no, damn it! You’d never
be satisfied with those boobies. Grainger—too
slow for you. Who is the man ? ”
“Yourself.”
“Me?” he laughed loudly. “Come here, my
girl. I knew you were only joking.”
“It is this —You will take me to town, and
there we shall be married quietly. You will be
mine then. Should you go back to her, the Law,
which makes you support her, will also , make you
support me—for it will punish you, rich as you
are. You cannot have both of us. Now I leave
you to choose.” She moved to the door.
“ It is not necessary.”
She shook her head sadly: “ Yes, Walter.
“Why ? ”
“ Choose —Me or Respectability.”
He sat awhile staring at the closed door.
* * * *
As she heard the clinking of the spurs die a/way
in the night she said to herself, bitterly: “Like
a man —Respectability ! ”

Somebody writes to the Sydney papers to
complain of the practice “of men of the local
volunteer regiments wearing their uniforms when
off duty.” But it’s safe to say that a prohibition
against wearing uniforms, except when on duty,
would drive 25 per cent, of the rank and file and 50
per cent, of the officers out of the volunteer ranks
altogether. Half of the officers of such corps as
the Saint Narcissus’ Rifles and the Aboi’iginal
Bayoneteers are officers simply for the pride and
joy of wearing gaudy uniforms to impress the girl
in the private bar and to cut a dash in the subur-
ban drawing-room. You may see officers (and
gentlemen) at the theatres in all the glory of silver
lace and colored cloth, not forgetting spurs ; and
a list of the occupations which these officers follow
would further betray the prevalence of the snob in
Australian military circles. The volunteer and
partially-paid officers are, as often as not, merely
fools with a desire to strut in fine feathers, or
undertakers, money-lenders, tailors, and so on,
who have a snobbish idea of becoming “ gentle-
men ” by means of a commission.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Success of Abundance in A.J.C. Derby brought
him into strong request for the coming V.R.O. blue
ribbon. Latest eliminations were Brakpan and Our State
(on the course), leaving seven starters, and at that the
field was well up to the average of recent years, and its
quality, on the whole, good. Abundance was bred by
Victorian Jas. Wilson, sen., near Queenscliff; by imported
Pilgrim’s Progress from Beanfeast, by Plebian from Fes-
tival, by Sterling. The time (2.45) was somewhat slow,
and would have been slower had the winner not forced
things over the last half of the trip. Trident’s Derby
finished 7sec. faster, but many others haven’t been as
speedy. Abundance had been trained at Randwick for
months past. Many “wouldn’t have him” because his
neck and shoulders didn’t fit in with their ideals, and
because jocks, who had ridden the nag, gave out that he
was clumsy. Abundance is engaged in the V.R.O. Derby,
the Caulfield Cup (7.9) and Melbourne Cup (7.5), wherein
he will, in view of his Derby success, be compelled to
hump lib. more.
• * • *
Only malcontent on course of 29 carded for
A.J.C. Epsom, Saturday, was Sir Leonard, who, if started,
would have carried a big weight of coin for the cutest of
punters. Money came so persistently for Cyanide that
she went out a hot first choice at 9 to 2 against—this
mainly on the strength of a gallop at Rosehill. when the
Metal mare beat by lsec. a great go credited to Oast Iron
on the same course. A bolt of 11 m. completely settled
Juindo’s prospects. The luck of the race was all with
Ibex and Air Motor, inasmuch as they got well away, and
stayed in front, until the straight saw Ibex do his stopping
act in most pronounced fashion and then retire. Here
Air Motor assumed the lead, and, opposite the Leger, was
lengths away from all others. A great roar went up as
Sequence was seen forging forward, wiping Air Motor
out amid intense excitement. Jocks and nags had every
ounce on it, and it looked as if Sequence’s mighty effort
would pull him through. Suddenly another yell proclaimed
the appearance of Cyanide going great guns, but the end
was all too near, and Air Motor flashed past the post a
neck to the good from Sequence, whose head was partly
m front of Cyanide’s. It was a magnificent effort on
Sequence’s part with the steadier of 10.3, and he had a
none too comfortable passage all the way. Cyanide, too,
was badly placed the whole journey, and only got through
when the race looked over. Heavily-backed Myall and
Bon Voyage were never sighted once during the running.
Only two Epsoms since The Novice’s in ’B9 have been
slower than the latest, the time for which (1.41) would
have, years back, been written marvellous. The record
(1.38 J) belongs to Sequence, and was made last year.
* * * «
Air Motor was bred by Mr. H. L. Mackellar at
Kirkham, and is by imported Ayr Laddie (by Ayrshire
from Catherine Douglas, by Sterling) from Windmill, who
was by Goldsbrough from Merry-Go-Round, by Hamlet
from Whirlpool (imp.) by Fitz Roland. Air Motor is in
Caulfield Cup with 6.13 and Melbourne Cup with 7.3,
exclusive of 141 b. penalty in former and 101 b. in latter.

the last mile 1.45£, aud last 6fur. 1.16.
* * ♦ *
JEtandwick has never seen a bigger crowd than
on Saturday last (Derby day), when quite 23,000 passed
the turnstiles and provided the fattest “ gate ” ever
known. Tram traffic is officially announced as the
heaviest recorded —approximately, 150 car-loads of people
were carried to the course.
* * * »
A.J.C, Metrop., run on Monday, #will always be
remembered for the very disappointing performances of
the two red-hot favorites, Patronage and Cyanide, who, for
some weeks past, have been showing excellent form, and
the fact that, for the first time since Yowi won 11 years
ago, the race went to a nag below the 7.7 mark. Winner,
Queen of Sheba, carried 6.7, and started fourth choice (12
to 1) in the betting. Patronage went out 7 to 4 and
Cyanide 3 to 1 against, while the best obtainable about
Felicitous, who got into the market with a late rush, was
7 to 1. The struggle was almost completely devoid of
incident. Caravel (principally) cut out the running to the
3fur. post, where Queen of Sheba headed him and in an
exciting finish beat The Persian by half-a-length with Air
Motor a similar distance off third. Patronage, buried
in the ruck until the last furlong, came with great
dash at the end and was just behind the placed
horses as they passed the judge. Air Motor, handicapped
with a 101 b. penalty through her success in the Epsom,
was always well up with the leader or second, thus
unarguably demonstrating that the winning of the mile on
Saturday was far from a fluke. Race was soundly run
throughout, and the time (2.36), though behind the
record, was distinctly good, particularly as track con-
ditions were against a speedy performance. Only twice
has the Metrop. ljm. journey been thrown behind faster
—by Survivor in ’97 and San Fran last year, the figures
in each instance being 2.35. The Persian showed great
grit and pace ; he was not visible till the run home, and
then finished game enough to get near pipping the money,
for Queen of Sheba tired very fast.
* ♦ *
Bay Eagle endorsed his Grand National form by
literally running away with the A.J.C. first Steeple, Mon-
day. Best backed opponents were Victorian rep. Drum-
mer, the Riverina lepper Railway, and Monarch, winner
of Hunt Club ’Chase at Rosehill, recently. Palings at
the foot of the hill were responsible for Masher and
Timmey each coming a cropper early. Monarch hit the
fence near the half-mile post and lost his rider, B. Walley,
who had an arm fractured. Railway and Bay Eagle raced
together till the palings ,at the bottom of the hill were
reached a second time ; there, Railway turned turtle,
followed by Muskeith, leaving Bay Eagle with a final lead
of, perhaps, 150yds from Sportsman. Drummer was a
long way off, third.
• » * *
Queen of Sheba, winner of rich Metrop., is a
light-fleshed five-year-old mare bred by late Mr. W. For-
rester at Warwick Farm (Sydney). She was raced un-
profitably by her breeder and another, and at the dis-
persal of Mr. Forrester’s stud passed into the hands of
present owner, W. R. Wotten, who got her for 55gs.
She comes from a good galloping family, being by
Niagara, from Happy Land, by Metal (imp.) from Pales-
tine, by Epigram from Matilda, by Yelverton from
Pauline, by The Swede from Camilla, by Capapie from
Violet, by Gratis from Camilla, by Bay Camerton from
Old Betty by Hector.
Said that Queen of Sheba’s owner, “Dick’
Wootton, who has had a long run of ill-luck, threw in for
a big win, and, besides the £2OOO prize, gathered some-
thing over £lOOO in bets and a like sum from the drawer
of the nag in “ Tatt.’s ” sweep. Queen of Sheba was well
backed for the lipsom, but could get no nearer than
seventh, though it is told now that she was badly inter-
fered with twice. Few spectators noticed the happenings,
and it was on the strength of this information that the
stable and its connections made such a substantial haul.

(Caulfield) and 6.12 (Melbourne) to hump.

* * * •
For fiendish brutality that “international mili-
tary ride ” from Brussels to Ostend, in which 13 out of
the 71 horses engaged died of exhaustion, takes a high
place. It wasn’t even useful in a military sense. Certainly
it proved that cavalry isn’t fit for service after doing 13
hours through heavy sand, but that much was known
already. Also it proved that 13 blackguards out of 71
would rather ride a dumb animal to death than drop out
of a senseless race, and probably a large proportion of the
other 58 blackguards were built on similar principles
but that much was also known already. A French black-
guard of the rank of lieutenant won. His horse was
amongst those that died. If there is any poetic justice in
the world he should be haunted by its ghost, and its
spectre should look over his shoulder and breathe horribly
into his absinthe all the rest of his days.
• * • *
Ahsan ul Hak is the latest importation of
Middlesex County (Eng.)—the county which gave a place
to ex-Australians Albert Trott and Roche. In English
cricket circles it doesn’t seem to be a question of where
a person comes from, or whether he is black, brown,
yellow, or the colors of a rainbow—the only qualification
needed is that he can play cricket.
* * * *
No matter how the concluding engagement of
the tour may go, Trumper has added to his laurels the
record of best average ever obtained by an Australian
batsman in England, even if he does not notch another
run. This, too, without the aid of a solitary “ not out.”
Victor also has the unique experience of nearly 50
journeys to the batting-crease with not one failure to
score registered against him.
* * * *
Cabled that, exclusive of himself, Lord Hawke
had eight players assured for the M.L. tour. Personnel
doesn’t, as regards strength, impress one much, though
it may be equal to any combination M.L. can get
together. Talked of match (which seems possible)
between N.S.W. and the Englishmen on their homeward
journey should result fairly easily against the latter.
Best bats of the team are Burnup and Warner, of whom
former was the seventh Englishman to get his thousand
this season—with an average of 32.54.. and both have
shaped successfully for the Gentlemen against the Players
on different occasions. Bar ex-Australian Albert Trott,
who it is said, will re-visit M.L. and probably play with
the Englishmen occasionally, only bowlers are Hargreave
and Wilson.
* * • •
L. O. S. Poidevin, erstwhile N.S.W, represen-
tative cricketer, now studying medicine in London, was
the first member of London County Club to score the
coveted 1000 runs ; and that club includes such per-
formers as Old Man Grace, Murdoch, and Braund
(occasionally).
• • * •
Players opposed to Australians at Harrowgate,
last week, were, on the whole, but a second-rate lot;
their best combination was engaged against the Gentle-
men, at Scarborough, and, as most close followers of
cricket anticipated, the home side went under badly— by
an innings and 47 runs. Vic. Trumper notched his tenth
century (127) of the tour, and now only wants three more
to be level with Fry’s much-trumpeted record of last
year, when fine weather prevailed. Conning over totals
made by Trumper in each of his half-score of hundreds
suggests the notion that, after getting the three figures,
he became careless or played out, for not once has he
topped his initial accumulation (128), though latest neared
it; particularly is this impressed by the ease with which
he adds to his lot till the hundred is passed.
• • * *
