* * * •
King’s School, Parramatta, again carried off
the N.S.W. Great Public School Rugby Premiership—
this time with an unbeaten record and the championship.
It played eight competition matches (and the only score
recorded against it was a penalty goal) amassing 163
points against opponents’ three. During the last two
years the King’s School line has only been crossed once
during competition matches, and only nine points have
been put up against it. It won the shield continuously
from 1890 to 1895; Newington College won in 1896;
Sydney Grammar School in 1897 to 1899; Newington
College in 1900; King’s School in 1901 and 1902—50 that
in 13 years the latter has won eight times (in 1897 and
IS9B it didn’t compete, owing to a dispute), and on six
occasions gained the title of champion—which is given to
the team which goes through the season without a defeat.
It is also the only college which has gained the title of
champion. The Schools’ Challenge Shield has been the
means of augmenting each year the prosperous University
Football Club, which has frequently gone so close to
annexing the senior grade—in fact, ex-King’s School boys
numbered nearly half the team which occupied the posi-
tion of runner-up during the past season.
• • • *
Richmond (Yic.), having won the Association
football premiership the other Saturday, proceeded next
day to cheerfully rub it into their chief opponents—
Ports. Both teams were level till the last match, when
Ports went down before Williamstown. Sunday after-
noon Richmond barrackers, in four drags and a dozen
waggonettes, with horses decked out in yellow-and-black
and players’ stockings on the horses’ legs, drove allround
the suburbs, winding up with a real joyful time in Port
Melbourne. Several willing goes resulted, and the cele-
bration threatened at one time to develop into a riot.
* * * *
Ingenuous note received by a prominent Vic.
footballer from the secretary of a school club :
“ Dear Sir, —You have been recommended to us as a
good firm referee, and so should be obliged if you could
assist us against team. They are rather a rough lot
of players, and we are comparatively small and light, so
watch their men and be firm and just, and we shall ever
be grateful.
P.S. Please kindly note that, if we win, drinks will be
free for the referee, and we hope you will not be nervous,
but firm and just.
* * * *
Stan. Wickham, captain of N.S.W. football
team now in Queensland, first appeared in representative
football in 1895 against Central Queensland ; and has since
played against Mongrelia (1896-1901) and M.L. (1897-
1901). lie is captain, also, of the Western Suburbs, which
recently won the Premiership ; and is admitted to be the
best three-quarter footballer now in the State.
Plugger Bilmartin writes, from Yankeedom,
that the ’Murkan saucer track “ sits up like the roof of a
house, and makes him feel very uncomfortable. Men
used to it can run rings round those who are not. It
gives you no time to think ; the minute you start think-
ing you stop and skiddle down to the pole and fall under.
There d be heaps of fun and money in building a saucer
track in Australia, and charging admission to see men
have their first ride on it.”
* * • *
M. Chappell, the winner of the Dunlop Road
Race from Warrnambool to Melbourne, is 25 years old
stands sft. sin., and weighs 9st. 101 b. He had no adven-
tures of any sort on the race ; the tucker supplied agreed
with him excellently, and he got home first by riding
himself to a thorough standstill. So complete was his
exhaustion that, on taking a hot bath just after the
finish, he fell asleep in the bath, and his mate had no
small trouble to awake him. A cold shower frightened
him up, however, and he soon got right again. This win
is Chappell s first noteworthy success on the bike
• * * *
Pankaroo ” : Melbourne motor-fiends turned
out m great force to view the finish of the Warrnambool-
Melb. road race. The inevitable dog was also on hand,
and brought a new 5-h.p. motor to an end. The motor-
man was whizzing along between the barriers, with a
smile on his face, when suddenly a dog worth about 6d.
rushed out and tackled the machine, and there was a
piteous hash of motor, dog, and driver. The dog had evi-
dently endeavored to seize the electric spark, but had by
mistake got the main cog.- The motor-car will never be
reasonably safe till some one invents a reliable dog-
remover to put in front of it and behind it.

* * » *
Floyd A. M'Farland, a Murkan cyclist of some
repute, who holds a number of championships for events
ranging from 1-mile to six-day contests, contemplates a
raid on this continent. He is 25 years of age, and the
tallest racing cyclist in the world, standing 6ft. 4in.
m his socks, and is known in the land of his
making—-the place where Nance O’Neil oomes from—
as the Elongated Californian.” He won his novice
race in 1895 ; and the same year he carried off
the amateur championship of the Pacific Coast, and since
then seems to have amused himself journeying from one
part of the Union to another to rake in dollars. It is
claimed for him that he is the greatest money-making
cyclist in the world, which is a good, large claim. This
country has an insatiable appetite for sports of any sort
who have hard cash to lose. This country’s love of
foreign capital, in fact, amounts to a disease.
* * * *
N.S. W. pigeon murdering supremacy was con-
tested, t other day, when 27 bird butchers, representa-
tives of Victoria, M.L. and N.S.W., made slaughter among
collection of weak fliers. Best performer of the day
although he didn’t win, was Victorian Daish, who grassed
a sequence of 16, all with the first barrel, and killed 20 of
the required 21. Goulburn (N.S.W.) man, H. Baxter, ap-
propriated the trophy and the honor (such as it is),
the only one to account for the full complement; it was
noticeable, too, that he got very few of the easiest things
Though there was a £5 sweepstake on the shoot Baxter

was entered upon. The alleged pigeon-shooting sport is

so he is no chicken at the game. J "

The Bulletin’s London correspondent: “ How
would the Austral-Angler like this applied to the Parra-
matta, or Yarra, or Brisbane river? It has just been
decided here that because Sir Roger anything owns the
land on both sides of the Thames for some miles, no one
hut he can fish the water from boat or otherwise. In fact
he owns the water-fishery because the water flows over
the soil that is his—so the ‘Mr. Justice’ decided. The
length in question was Maidenhead Bridge to Cookham
one of London’s few' real beauty spots.”

He recent American par crediting
J. E. Gorman, of ’Frisco, with a world’3 revolver-shooting
record because he scored 934 points out of a possible
1000, nothing is said about the essential-to-be-known'
points of target size and shooting distance. Also a record
to be worth anything, must be made ir. a public oompeti-
™on' J A L B l sley , th ? revolver “ bull ”is 2in. diameter at
20 and 30yd. and 4in. at 50yd. No rest is allowed * the
bore of the pistol must not be under -38, and the trigger-
pull not under 31b. With contests conducted on these
conditions all over the world the performances of the
shooters can be compared ; but in some American com-
petitions a much smaller bore and a “hair’’-pull are
allowed, thus conducing to more accurate shooting
owing to absence of recoil and easy trigger. A smaller
bore than - 38 is not desirable when the target is alive, as
it is generally close, and must, therefore, be put hors-de-
combat before it can retaliate. Colonel Finn (ofN.S.W.)
told me a friend of his plugged a number of men with his
Mauser pistol (about '23 bore) in Egypt when the enemy
was charging, and not one dropped. Revolvers for target-
shooting only are made with a 7-in. barrel, and of as
small a bore as "22. A very small charge of powder in
ratio to bullet-weight is also used ; but, though pheno-
menal accuracy may be thus attained, the target-revolver
will hardly “ stop ” a man on the rush.
* « *
A well-known Yic. athlete put medical Melb.
by the ears, the other day. He swallowed the contents
of an unlabelled black bottle to allay his colic, and then
jumped to the conclusion that the mixture was a deadly
poison. Telephones were set going, and doctor after
doctor was rung up. The occurrence of the medical con-
gress, however, led to none being in, until finally a stay-
at-home practitioner was fluked on who hustled along
and pumped the muscular patient out. Scarcely had he
finished when another medico came along, and during
the next hour all Collins-st. East dropped in with
stomach-pumps and antidotes as they went home from
the place of meeting. To put an effective climax on the
comedy, the recovered athlete produced the bottle which
had done the awful deed, and found that it contained a
harmless cough-mixture. He is now diplomatising with
about 14 prominent medicos who want their fees.

“T.D.”: As Sandow has been running his
physical culture colleges and his system for some years
now, isn’t it about time that he came along with a few
prize samples of his pupils ? The strong man states that
it only takes three years to attain to his own physical
perfection (proportionately, of course), and adds that he
was a bit of a weakling to begin with, and now lifts two
tons on occasion merely because he developed his muscles
scientifically for three years. Surely some promising
physiques must have been placed under Sandow’s care,
but one never hears of any Hercules but the boss
strong man himself as a product of the system. There is
surely some excuse for taking a large spoonful of salt
when he makes the announcement about his early un-
soundness.
• » * •
It is said that Prof. Miller, Melb.’s old-time
strong man, has a pupil somewhere out Williamstown
way who is willing to be pitted against Sandow.

Easy success of blocky Jack M ‘Go wan Over
comparatively lathy Rollo for Australian lightweight pug
supremacy, in Sydney, t’other night, brings to mind the
mild manner of his training as compared with the extent
to which others go, and suggests the idea that his excep-
tional staying powers are, in some degree, attributable to
this fact. M’Qowan’s daily work while getting ready
consists of a five or six-mile walk in the morning, and a
lively, willing “go” with his trainer, Joe Stokesbury,
and some sprinting over 50 or 70 yards, to sharpen the
wind up, in the afternoon. This much, and no more, does
the little Melbourne terror, who, besides other notable
things, not long ago fought off the tall, very clever
middle-weight black, Tom Mitchell, for 20 rounds.

“Upper-cut”: The death of Joe Goddard re-
calls my first sight of that cyclonic and courageous cham-
pion. It was at the old Crystal Palace, in Richmond
(Vic.), and Prof. King had just thrown out a challenge
to anybody of his weight, Herb. Goddard pre-
ferred. There rose above the far side of the
ropes a shining belltopper, and a gorgeous figure
entered the ring. It was tall and broad, and was attired
in a long coat, with deep astrachan round the collar and
cuffs ; that was Joe Goddard, the Barrier champion, then
just approaching the zenith of his fame. He did not
speak, but, reaching into his breast-pocket, brought forth
handfuls of notes there is no other way of describing
them—he simply shovelled his money out. That finished
Kibg. Herb. Goddard died of consumption seven or
eight years back, and Joe has now been shot by a drunken
German—or by a black policeman, accounts vary.

Black bruiser Barnes and Murrumbidgee rough
’un Lynch fought a fierce, forceful thing at Sydney Gaiety
A. 0. last week. From beginning to end they went at each
other like a railway collision. Lynch, the cleverer, dealt
out sfoush in loads, but the negro was grit every inch and
was satisfied if he got a punch home once in away. Both
were strong all the time. They scrambled and scuffled
allover the floor, and through the ropes at times, while
the crowd screeched delightedly. It ended in Barnes
being’ knocked limp while the twelfth round was going
°P- Ea °h presented a badly “ slugged-out ” appearance
at the end, the black being much the worse ; but Lynch
was in a sorry plight, too.

The “Kid” Parker-Williamsimposition atKal-
goorlie (W. A.), recently, about flattened out the already
tottering popularity of the bruising game in that
section, where, at one time, it paid well. Parker,
though a strong athlete ordinarily, never was a boxer,
and never could be. He is too muscle-bound, and can
only scramble and sprawl at best, but he didn’t even do
this with Williams who, fat as mud and ungainly to a
degree, labored round the ring like an old woman. A
punch on the arm or anywhere would see Parker go
down, and a lead would cause him to duck and grip the
other chap round the legs, and sometimes this would
trip Williams, and they’d sink to the-boards together like
a large shapeless mass of nothing in particular. It was
the sickest thing in fights ever the West suffered, which
is saying a deal. The farce terminated by Parker falling
to one knee in the third round without being hit, as he
often did before, and Williams, losing his head, struck
him while in that position—a distinct foul—but Parker’s
previous offences had shut off all sympathy with him, and
Williams got the verdict. Parker should stick to ball-
punching for Harry Rickards; he’s an artist at that—
nature never intended him for a fighter.
Ruined.
Business men speak of the fortunes of trade as
a sew-saw, one up and the other down. They
take their good and bad luck philosophically,
like the North American Indian, who believes
that what is to be will be. The utter fallacy of
this kind of reasoning may be seen in the manner
how the health steps in to decide whether a man
is to succeed in a commercial venture or not. Bad
health is often spelt in the word ruined. What
people require is a remedy that cures, after other
so-called remedies fail. The remedy that cured
these people will cure you. Truthful words are
like well aimed arrows; they are straight and
pointed, and fly true to the mark. There is no
fooling about the truth, it always strikes home,
and this is how the words written by Mrs. W.
Clarkson, Enmore, N.S.W., make such a deep
impression on everybody who reads them. She
writes :—“ Myself and family have been cured of
biliousness and liver ailments by using Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root pills. I can also state that they are
superior to all kinds of tonics for the hot and
muggy weather that we experience in New South
Wales.” Mrs. S. Aust, who resides at Hornsby-
street, Maldon, Vic., states: “1 havegreat pleasure
in testifying to the good results I have obtained
from the use of your Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills. For some time I have suffered with severe
pains in the head, also nervousness, and after
taking two bottles of these pills, I had great
relief, and I can conscientiously give them a good
word wherever I go.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills are a perfect blood purifier, and a positive
cure for biliousness, indigestion, constipation,
headaches, sallow complexion, liver and kidney
troubles, piles, pimples, boils and blotches, and
for female ailments. Sold by chemists and
storekeepers, Is ljd per bottle, or six bottles 6s 6d.
Sole proprietors, The W. H. Comstock Co.,
Limited (Australasian Depot), 58 Pitt-street’
Sydney. Packed in amber bottles and the full
name blown thereon.

Ex-constable Cornelius Crowe pegs away
persistently at the “ Maladministration and Cor-
rupt Practices in the Vic. Police,” but he seems
to get no nearer to the realisation of his dream—
a Parliamentary Board of Inquiry into the
character of the police department. He laid his
old, old charges before Chief-Sec. Murray the
other month, and Murray practically referred the
matter to the men who don’t want to be inquired
into, saying “ Is there any need for further inves-
tigation ? ” And the police officers furnished the
Chief-Sec. with the expected reply. Nevertheless
it seems not improbable that Crowe will force cer-
tain specific charges under the notice of the Vic.
Assembly and obtain the Board his soul longeth
for. Vic. has gone 20 years without an official ex-
posure of its police force scandals. The exposure
of 20 years ago was instructive, but unproductive.
One constable at that time was found guilty of
having “ put up a job,” and the late Francis
Longmore, who presided over the Inquiry, re-
marked that he “ ought not to be in the service
another day.” The Board advised his dismissal.
But he was not dismissed. On the contrary he
rose high in the force.

The Big Tank.
“ F.M.” speaks to the point
The Big Tank of Australia has a surface area of not less
than 200,000 square miles. There are 62,000 square miles
in N.S.W. In Queensland and South Australia the areas
not yet surveyed are certainly greater, but 200,000 square
miles are enough for a generation, and a century, and
the making of a nation. The 39,680,000 acres in N.S.W.
represent a tank of tolerable dimensions. Its depth is
uncertain, but as it consists of Alpine ranges crumbled
down in an old, old time, and deposited as sandstone
rock, it is not likely to be a scaly layer ; nothing akin to
the coal seams, products of vegetable growth. What
would the Hawkesbury or the Blue Mountain sandstone
average if crumbled down and rolled out ?
There is also a mouth to the tank in the outcropping
rocks through which the rain constantly percolates. In
N.S.W. it is 40,000 square miles in area, in the Continent
proportionately more. Through that mouth flows, in
N.S.W., fully 50 per cent, of an average rainfall of 26in.
Reckoning stock consumption, evaporation, and outflow
of the rivers, the N.S.W. meteorological authorities could
never account for anything like 50 per cent, of the rain-
fall. All that cannot be accounted for in other ways goes
into the Big Tank. Is it possible, by any means, to get
any idea of the various capacities and possibilities of this
tank? It is not scientific attention which is required.
Science has made her investigations, applied her tests,
written her Q.E.D. The Big Tank of N.S.W. is mapped
out as plainly as any lake on the Continent. It is there ;
it is full now ; it has its constant sources of supply. Its
resources are also available. They have been tapped
by a thousand odd bores in three States, and
they draw off now near 600,000,000ga1. a day—more
than 20 times the full water supply of Sydney or Mel-
bourne ; within one-seventh of the quantity Colonel Hope
estimated as requisite to keep the Murray going below
Albury. N.S.W. draws through bores, fairly dotted over
her share of the Tank, 120,000,000 gals. That is four
times as much as Sydney wants, and six times as much
as she gets. What proportion it represents of the quan-
tity available it is difficult to estimate. The figures are
bewildering as those of star distances. Even to reckon
them is like starting to bottle off Prospect. We can no
more conceive the figures than we could stack the bottles.
Yet the Big Tank exists just as certainly as does Pros-
pect, and its waters might be as beneficially utilised.
But a vote of £200,000 a year to be devoted to purposes
of water conservation will not begin that work, nor is
the “statesmanship,” which permitted such a paltry
matter as the Sydney supply to drift into its present con-
dition, likely to tackle it successfully. The Big Tank,
moreover, is uncommonly well roofed. Over some few
millions (of acres, in New South Wales the roof surface
is loosely-compacted, red, black, or grey soil. Not a rock
for a hundred miles. The same sort of country stretches
away north-west, south-west, right west. It includes, as
has been said, 62,000 square miles, generally good and
largely very rich country. Millions of acres of it are as
fat as Nile mud or the Hunter River flats. Such is the
country nature made, and such is the country on which
man has sat weeping and wailing about drought,
devastation, and pending damnation. Such is the
country that sent its unemployed to aid in the
grinding of civilisation off the face of the veldt, and
to help in the spending of £200,000,000 over that good
work. If only £20,000,000 were spent in working our
Big Tank, what might it not accomplish for Australia
and for Empire.
But Empire does not move magnificently in that sort
of way. Victor Hugo estimated that the world he knew
spent 300,000,000 francs a year in firing salutes. We
cannot justly say that the world, or any part of it, has
become much wiser since that time. If N.S.W. desires to
open out her own share of the Big Tank she must do it
by her own resources, handled with her own statesman-
ship. The problem to be undertaken is as plain as it is
vast. The area of the tank roof is known, and the
character of the soil in its various districts. The cost of
winning the water is ascertained. It is near enough:
£2OOO for 1,000,000ga1. a day. The average cost and yield
of the bores work out that way. How much has it cost
to provide 30,000,000ga1. a day for Sydney or Melbourne ?
Artesian boring will compare very favorably with surface
storage when that question is answered.
But really the cost is the least difficulty in the matter.
The 800 bores in N.S.W. and Queensland have cost a
million-and-a-half. A million-and-a-half for a summer
Murray, flowing as desired about the central wilderness.
And, in addition, the actual Murray runs—to waste
mostly—through millions of acres of fat country, era-
versed by railway lines, and within 200 miles of the ©out
Australian capital! Use will when the
artesian area of one State, or of the Commonwealth, 1.
considered and legislated for as a separate whole, ine
land is there, the water beneath, the sunshine, which
might be all-blessing, above, and the men who hoia
that land must wet it with the water or
make room for others. One of the greatest
perhaps the very greatest—of the elements of individual
well-doing and "national prosperity in Australia, lies in
the Big Tank, atop of which, for more than a hundred
years now, Australian men have been carping as helpless
as pelicans in the wilderness, like the Arabs who prowl
about the ruined aqueducts and jungle-grown irrigation
farms of old Assyrian kings. .
But the work can’t be done if it is to stop short when-
ever Coiien in London doesn’t see his way to supply
another big loan, to lag whenever Coiien of London is
reticent with the money, or to be hung up for a few years
every now and then till Cohen in London gets his second
wind, or to be dropped and the half-finished works left to
fall into decay when Coiien in London turns off the finan-
cial tap. Australia must do it out of its own revenues if
it is to be well done, or done at all.
“Veteran” : Even if S’Langdon Bony thon
had succeeded in extracting an explanation about
those 10 alleged drill-instructors sent from N.S.W.
to S. Australia, he wouldn’t be possessed of the
information that the so-called drill-instructor is a
mere sham, who has at most two hours’ drill-
instructing a week, all the rest of his time being
devoted to quill-driving for his amateur colonel,
and making out the reports and returns which
that exalted personage signs without in the least
understanding them. There is an old story about
an Australian cavalry regiment which was to have
its first lot of officers chosen by examination. The
drill-instructor went round to the candidates and
offered to sell to each all the answers for £lO.
Most of them paid up. The drill-sergeant, in
fact, is the real commander of an Australian regi*
ment, the colonel being merely an ornamental
figurehead who does the log-rolling and political
and social part of the business. The Drayton
Grange horror ought to open the eyes of the Aus-
tralian people to the sort of fraud that is being
put upon them by the “Barracks Push, ’’ which
practically furnished, in conjunction with its
social connections, the whole of the “colonels”
and “captains” on board. Not one of them,
according to the sworn evidence of themselves,
the surgeons, and the ship’s officers, made the
slightest attempt either to keep the lower decks
clear for ventilation, to make the men keep them-
selves and their quarters clean, to give the men
exercise, or provide room for the sick —all which
things lay within their duty. If the drill-ser-
geant was there he was quartered on one of the
lower decks, and knew better than to offend the
men who were only separated from him by a can-
vas partition, by teaching officers, who had neither
the will nor the nerve to protect him, what they
ought to do. Drill is a mechanical business, and
the man who teaches it is very useful in his way,
but when an emergency like that on the Drayton
Grange arises, brains are wanted, and the drill-
sergeant is not paid for brains. Drill and fighting
are not the whole of soldiering.
Perhaps the nearest approach to human happi-
ness is the soothing sensation which pervades a woman’s
breast when she feels that the triumph of her dressmaker
cannot fail to arouse the jealousy of her dearest friends.
And, perhaps, there ,is no earthly power that can create
this ecstacy so easily as the gentle embraces of - the P.l>.
corset. The lucky inventor of that beautifier was, as
everybody knows, made a Knight of the Legion of Honor,
by the King of the Belgians. And the genial Mr.
Borsdorff, of Melbourne and Sydney, has so advanced the
mission of the P.D. in this country, that the enduring
gratitude of every Australian heart is due to him.*

Bananaland—a country which pays a third
of its revenue, or \\ millions, in interest, which
raises half a million deficit on the top of this, and
only keeps itself afloat by spending tons of bor-
rowed money per annum—is just about to raise a
loan of a couple of millions in London. In
order to spur on the halting Philpite Cabinet
to further action, a Progress Association at N.
Bundaberg (a typical hamlet of the Kyabram
type) is vigorously stirring up the Queensland
Ministry to float a further loan of three millions,
for irrigation purposes. This modest demand
(£6 per head for all Queensland), if acceeded to,
would probably result in a crop of wholesale
repudiations of liability on the part of local irriga-
tion trusts ; also in a wild howl for the dismissal
of two-thirds of the public servants of the State,
by way of squaring things, and finally, in a fresh
clamor for a fresh loan of ten millions—by way of
setting the country up again.

The last will and execration of a bitterly
Vindictive agriculturist, formerly of Boggy
Creek (Vic.), was recently before the courts. The
old man, in his will, instructed that his daughter
Gladys was to have the farmhouse for her residence
all the days of her life, or until she married. His
son George was to hustle for himself, in his own
house, but was not to be denied casual accommo-
dation by Gladys if he required it. Son John was
apparently on the same basis as son George.
Apart from the right of domicile at the house,
these three were to share the property equally,
But on his son Francis Thomas the old man of
Boggy Creek laid his extra-special, boiling-hot
curse, and specified thg,t if he or his wife, son,
daughter, executor or assign received shelter or a
crust at the old farmhouse, the person giving the
said sustenance to Thomas should forfeit all
interest in the property. Later, the old man
took objection to son John also, and cast
him forth to be with Thomas in outer darkness,
though he wasn’t cast out so definitely as Thomas.
Then the ancient settler died in the odour of
animosity, and it was discovered that the farm
was only a village-settlement lease, which reverted
to the State at his death, the State paying for im-
provements. The Court decided that the money
thus received should be divided between George
and Gladys, but all the pleasant dreams of Thomas
coming back to the old home in rags, and having
the old family dog sooled on to him, fall through.
It must be a terrible thing to keep your gun for
years loaded to the muzzle with a big curse, and
then, when the psychological moment comes, to
find that it isn’t your gun after all, and that you
can’t fire it.

If You Please, Miss, Give Me Heaven.
“ Papa, I’m so sad and lonely,”
Sobbed a tearful little child ;
““Since dear mamma’s gone to Heaven,
Papa, darling, You’ve not smiled.
I will speak to her and tell her
That we want her to come home;
Just you listen, and I’ll call her
Through the telephone :
Chorus—
“lf you please, Miss, give me Heaven —for my Mamma’s
there;
You can find her with the angels on the golden stair.
She’ll be glad it’s me who’s speaking—call her, won’t you,
please,
For I want to surely tell her we’re so lonely here.”
When the girl received this message,
Coming o’er the telephone,
How her heart thrilled in that moment,
And the wires seemed to moan ;
“ I will answer, just to please her :
‘ Yes, dear heart, I’ll soon come home.’ ”
“ Kiss me, Mamma, kiss your darling,
Through the telephone.”
Chorus— “ If you please, Miss,” etc.

“ Nina Jeane ” has a theory and expresses
it very plainly:—
The recent upheaval in Sydney of the W.C.T.U. and
Consul Orlando Baker did not throw much light on the
lady beer-puller and other women-workers. The W.C.T.U.
and Orlando Baker declared that the barmaid’s occupa-
tion was conducive to the demoralisation of the women
engaged therein. In support of this sweeping statement
they produced no evidence, quoted no reliable statistics,
and asked that their unsupported statement be taken as
sufficient. To prove that the barmaid class contains a
larger proportion of crooked girls than any other occu-
pation was obviously impossible. Coghlan reckons im-
morality mostly by the illegitimate births, which is no guide
to the extent of the immorality of any particularclass. And
it is generally arather decent girl that brings her baby into
the world and struggles along to support it; the really
immoral women is too worldly-wise. Speaking from
personal knowledge the most dangerous class of feminine
work is that of the chambermaid. The lady who serves
out beer is usually in the company of more than one man,
and there’s safety in numbers. Also, the lady who ladles
out the fermented wet is further protected by a high,
wide counter which has a decidedly moral and restraining
influence. But the poor chambermaid is continually
being surprised in lonely bedrooms by wicked male
citizens who are apparently always on the alert for the
purpose. If the W.C.T.U. really wants to prevent a few
thousand brands being thrown to the burning annually
let it agitate for the abolition of the chambermaid from
the list of female duties, and it will have deprived
the male boarder of his happiest hunting-ground.
When I kept a boarding-house, and had a young and
pretty chambermaid, it was remarkable, whenever
bald gents, with bunches of muscle on their jaws, came
“ to look at the apartments ” (they never came to see about
board and lodging) how their wicked old faces lit-up
when they found a bedroom in some remote corner at the
top of the house. If it was also down a long side passage,
and lit with only a skylight, they would offer to pay five
shillings per week extra to make sure of it. It was the
sudden slump in the value of those remote bedrooms
when a gaunt, wizened female of 52 became chamber-
maid, vice Grace, resigned to take a trip to somewhere or
other, that revealed the dangers of the position. If Mr.
Orlando Baker would only take up bachelor quarters in a
top-side room for a fortnight he would probably be ready
to hold forth in every Seotchbyterian kirk on the urgent
necessity of legislation to provide that no chambermaid
shall be less than 72 years of age, and ugly to boot.

“ Hermes ” on some kinds of women :
Someone was recently bewailing, in a daily paper, the
“frightful epidemic” of judicial separation which is
sweeping over N.S.W., and at the end of his moan he
plaintively inquired could nothing be done to check it.
I know something that could be done. During the last
six months three newly-married couples have come to
live at our boarding-house, and of these the first two
brides now come down to breakfast with their hair in
curling-pins, and one already makes a practice of going
about all the morning with half-an-inch of underwear
showing between the bottom of her blouse and the top
of her skirt. The last comer does none of these things as
yet, and is still a dainty little woman with brown eyes
and hair, who wears soft little billows of chiffon about
her neck and bosom. But this is how she talks : “ I’ve
only been married six weeks, but I know all about men—
my word ! I find they have an insatiable habit of telling
their wives what a devil of a fellow I was ‘ before I met
you, my dear ! ’ And you can make up your mind that
he’ll be the same devil of a fellow again after the newness
has worn off—if you’ll let him. But I’ll take all sorts of
care Jack never gets the chanee. If any of these cats of
women you meet nowadays were to some chasing round
Jack, making eyes at him, I’d kill them stone dead—yes,
stone dead, if I swung for It.” Then she glares
round the table at the women and looks brightly
at the men, who admire the sparkle in her eyes,
while Jack matters something incoherently, and says
he’ll go outside and have a smoke, And I can see
that this sort of talk, which wo get at every meal in some
form or another, is getting on Jack's nerves, and there is
a gleam of triumph in the eyes of the golden--haired
girl who sits at the other end and glances at Jack through
the trends of the big palm in the middle o,f the table.
And when Jack has gone out to smoke, the produce-man,
who sits at the middle of the table, tells Mrs, Jack; how
mush he admires her spirit, poth these women will be
going- about with hair in pins and down-at-heel slippers
in some hoarding-house by-and-bye, and they won’t even
he string about respectably—the conventional kind of re*
epeetability. And when a woman has her clothes sticking
Cut between her blouse and her- skirt, and is “ living in
Sin w as well, it is almost as bad as if she wore a large
grease-mark Oft her-frock and lived with her own husband.
The Melb. penny R.C. organ, The
Tribune, publishes this under the heading
“Little Stories'for Little People ! ,T
The Governor, in a rage, paused to think what kind
of death he could inflict on the saint. Iron hooks, the
rack, and other such instruments of torture seemed far
too gentle in his cruel eyes. At last he said to the
executioners—
“ Take him, and let him see and desire death, without
being able to obtain it. Out off his limbs joint by joint,
and this so slowly that the wretch may know what it is to
abandon the gods of his ancestors for an unknown
deity.”
The executioners dragged Arcadius to the place where
many other victims had already suffered.
Here the martyr lifts up his eyes to heaven, and
implores strength from above ; then stretches out his
neck, expecting to have his head cut off; but the
executioner bade him hold out his hand, and, joint after
joint, chopped off his fingers, arms, and shoulders. Lay-
ing the saint afterwards on his back, he, in the same
barbarous manner, cut off his toes, feet, legs and thighs.
.... After so many martyrdoms, his body lay a mere
trunk, weltering in its own blood.
Ugh ! nice reading for little people 1
Broken Hill is a good enough place for
anybody but a mining boss or a publican to go
mad in ; and yet 17 in one week is a big record.
Seventeen people deemed to be insane were pre-
sented to the Bench during a recent week. Five
went down to the asylum ; 11 were discharged.
At whose initiative were all these arrests made ?
A little initiative of that sort throughout the bush
would lead to queer results. There are many
hundreds very properly deemed insane, and yet
perfectly safe. Drought probably drove a few of
them in, like the birds and the beasts, and
“somebody” hadn’t sense enough "to leave ’em
alone. The mischief, not very well understood
amongst responsible people, is, that once put
“inside,” such people often quickly become
dangerously mad, and remain horrors to them-
selves and their surroundings, and permanent
charges on the States.

The Bushman and the Crow.
‘‘l ain’t a harrl man, strike me blind !” the
Bushman said to me ;
“ I love the dumb beasts, every kind wot walks in
land and sea ;
It’s maybe—l’ve been thinking mate — because I
love them so
That in my heart I hold the hate o’ hell against
the crow.
“See here: when drought and dry wind bring
King Death upon the track,
He sneaks behind, on smooth black wing—wi’
heart three times as black ;
And little does the d- n wretch care which
beast or where it dies,
So long’s his hungry beak may tear at offal,
heart, and eyes !
“White-livered crawler at the best, he hops
among the dirt,
And keeps a careful distance, lest his d—— d black
hide get hurt;
He’ll dance around some poor bogged thing and
pick its glaring eyes,
And if it moves he’ll take to wing—afraid the
poor wretch rise !
“ He sits upon the ringbarked limb when you and
me goes by ;
Hark to that blarsted ‘Kawr!’ That’s him!
Daren’t look you in the eye !
You don’t know when your kids, Jim Brown,
might miss the school-house track—
My God ! — with him to mark them down ! —the
MURDERING (BLANK) IN BLACK ! !
“ I understan’ that droughts may come for reasons
various;
There’s whips o’ sinners humpin’ drum might
bring such things on us,
I kin conceive how floods might be sent westward
far and wide
To let us poor blamed rubbish see we’ve small
excuse for pride,—
“ But this black devil—let me curse ! the name’s
too mild for him
That tears the poor lambs, throbbing warm, before
their eyes grow dim !
Well, God had many things to make—and how
was He to know ?
But, Jim, He made His one mistake the time He
made the crow ! ”
JIM BROWN.


Colonel Stuart, who was Acting-Command-
ant of the S.A. forces for several years during the
absence of Brigadier-General Gordon, has not
been provided with a position by General Hutton,
and it is believed that an attempt will be made to
retrench him. He was once a coach-painter, but
he went to Germany at his own expense and
attended the manoeuvres there and is a thoroughly
qualified officer. The Hutton’s aristocratic staff,
however, probably thinks he has still the smell of
turpentine about him.
“ Veteran ”: General Hutton doesn’t seem
to know his own mind. Recently he removed
Colonel Templeton from the charge of the Vic-
torian rifle clubs, partly, it was said, by way of
retrenchment, and partly because Templeton was
over the regulation age-limit. But then the posi-
tion was given with a good fat salary (Templeton’s
post had been honorary) to a Major who had just
been retired from the N.S.W. staff because he also
was over age. There is a very peculiar odor about
the whole transaction.
About the fate of certain officers of the
Bush veldt Carbineers, an old Bulletin contribu-
tor, now representing an English paper at
Pretoria, writes:
Pretoria was the headquarters of the Bushveldt Carbi-
neers, who were mostly Australians. I have met
about a hundred of them, and with one accord they
denied the blood-guiltiness of Morant and Ilandcock.
They declared the stories, current and printed, con-
cerning those officers, either gross exaggerations
or cruel inventions. What had been done by
them was strictly in accordance with instructions, and
if they deserved death for their actions, at least a
thousand others should have been shot in gloomy Pretoria
gaol. The courts-martial extended over three weeks,
yet the published evidence occupies but a few columns.
I have seen the original evidence and compared it
with the scant extracts, and was amazed at the careful
emasculation of the full report. In most essential points
the facts were perverted, and everything which told in
favor of the men was carefully excluded. Morant, no doubt,
was maddened by the mutilation of his personal friend,
Captain Hunt, but even then he did not exceed orders. As
for Handcock—a simple-minded, uneducated fellow
—the evidence would acquit him in any civil
court in the world, and all the Bushveldt Carbi-
neers I interviewed are unanimous as to his absolute
innocence. The printed evidence regarding Handcock
is simply ridiculous and infamously misleading,
to his friends and to all Australians. I have made full
and careful inquiries, and these demand that something
should be done to remove the stigma cast upon'Austra-
lians. The true story will stagger Australia, and, as a
patriotic Australian, I hope it will come out.
N.Z. Loan and M.A. Co. Ltd. reports for week
ended Sept. 6th that wool continues firm and unchanged,
the demand being chiefly for good combing merinos. Short
and faulty wools hard to place. Some inquiry for cross-
breds. Labor troubles are interfering with the despatch
of clips to Sydney and the article is arriving in very small
quantities. Next sales on 17th and 18th September.
Sheepskin catalogues large, but almost wholly of defec-
tive station skins. Inquiry strong, though coarse cross-
breds are somewhat weaker. A slight improvement was
noted in marsupials, bears frequently bringing Jd. per
skin higher. The supply of ’possums is distinctly weak,
and all medium and good qualities show an improvement,
the only exception being that Queeensland sorts are all
rather neglected. Hides seemed to have been taken from
starved stock, and the few choice samples available were
snapped up at late rales. Tallow slipped down some
more, and there being no shipping inquiry, prime mixed
is worth only £2B 55., which is from ss. to 10s. lower than
last sale. Choice bones and glue-stock sold well, also
horns, but horse-hair slumped considerably. Breadstuffs
had a quiet week, though prices remained steady. The
late rains have brightened the outlook considerably, and
1902-3 crop promises to give a yield and quality equal to
any previous record. Quotations, 4s. lOd. to 4s. lid. per
bushel for prime milling samples.*

and certain Australian scapegoats. Read “ Bush-

Paringa, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), reconstructed
barely five months ago, likely to require a fresh corpse-
reviver soon. Out of £40,000 new funds raised, by the
first call of Is. a share only £6300 was left after liquidat-
ing old liabilities. Shaft now down trifle over 1000 ft.,
and is to go 300 ft. more before any driving is to be
done to prove—the utter worthlessness of the pro-
perty. This work is not expected to be complete till
February. Admitted now that lode so far discovered is
valueless— ergo, it is not the Brown Hill lode. Directors
are to adopt the Elmore oil treatment —when they find
some ore.
• * * *
Queensland Menzies (W.A.) : At 446 ft. level,
eastern reef averages 2ft. wide worth 30dwts., and wes t-
ern Bft. wide of high grade. Estimated ore reserves
above this level 12,000 tons. Present 10 head of light
stampers treat 350 tons monthly, and additional 5 heads
of 10001 b. stamps have been ordered, and should easily
double output. Adjoining Crusoe mill has been tempor-
arily hired so that, in future, monthly output will reach
500 tons. Latest result 435 tons for 9120 z. Better divs.
in sight.
* • * *
The Goodenough, Menzies (W.A.), now held
under option by Boulder No. 1 Co., has had a final clean-
up before being handed to the co. Result reads, 6500 z.
from 312 tons, making a total of 18470 z. from 679 tons
since April. If the co. can come within cooee of such
results working on a big scale, Boulder No. 1 shares
should be a tempting purchase.
*•• • *
The buyers of the Standard litter of wild cats in
W.A., the New Century Trust, have floated a new co. to
take over the felines. Co. has 500,000 shares of 10s. each,
with a 2s. 6d. assessment. Paddington Consols is to be
launched on its own. It is insanity or ignorance which
suggests that it can be made to pay on the proposed
capital. The other derelicts, none of which are gold-pro-
ducing, will be miserable weaklings for the New Standard
Go. to nurse. Ex-Gov. Jerry Smith is one of the nurses.
* * * *
Menzies, Alpha (W.A.), during year ended
April 30th, made an alleged profit of £799. This feat was
achieved by debiting £3526 spent on mine development
to capital account; otherwise by treating depletedstopes
and other holes in the ground as assets. This system of
finance is a perfect godsend to the State, as it enables the
Treasurer to scoop in profit tax on purely mythical pro-
fits. The Treasurer is now prosecuting several co.s who
have been sending him amended balance-sheets showing
losses instead of the faked ones issued to bamboozle
scrippers.
* * • •
Capt. Parkes has quitted the Oroya (W.A.), and
Hollow, of the Brown Hill, is to boss both properties.
Mine will probably be surrounded by barbed wire
entanglements like the Brown Hill, and a bull pup sooled
on to “speering” visitors. Parkes pulled co. through
many difficulties, and mined and treated 50,000 tons of
Bdwt. ore at a cost of 17s. a ton. Parkes retires with the
reputation of having ploughed a straight furrow.
• • * *
Yankee Loring, who recently took charge of
Sons of Gwalia (W.A.), has reduced hands from 700 to 400
without reducing output. Transformation has been
effected by increased use of machines and by firing out a
lot of useless surface hands and alleged departmental
bosses. Unfortunately mine is not looking too well and
developments are backward owing to blanks met with.
* m * *
Associated Northern (W.A.), has shipped 700
tons of ore to smelters. As some of this assays up t 0.12
and 13oz. to the ton, a big jump in the monthly returns
will eventuate when the yield is published. Co. still
working very rich ore, but exploration confined chiefly to
the rich chute, outside of which mine is practically barren.
Quite possible another div. may soon be declared.
* « * *
Great Fihgall Consols (W.A.) costs work out as
follows Mining and reduction, 255. 3d. ; development,
11s. ; capital account, 245. 4d. The last two items are
temporarily very high, owing to sinking new main shaft,
and erecting machinery. Eventually total costs should
not exceed 355., against ore worth £5 6s. per ton. On
7600 tons monthly this would leave a profit of £26,500, or
over 2s. a share. The winzes below No. 8 level are going
down in good ore.
* * * •
Frank Hartmann, late of Lake View Consols
(W.A.), is again in charge of Ivanhoe South Extended
and Southern Development Co. In former, main shaft is
to be carried down to 1500 ft. to cut Ivanhoe middle lode,
alleged to have been located by bore. As the Ivanhoe
lodes are practically vertical for the 800 ft. tested, and are
600 ft. east of the Extended’s boundary,J,he surmise looks
far-fetched. At past rate of progress the riddle will
remain unsolved for years, as there are still 300 ft. to sink.
* • » *
Mainly, ifc seems, on the recommendations
of the Sulman-Picard firm, the Elmore concentrating
process is being taken on by Westralian co’s. This
is the interesting oil process against which people
have been rather prejudiced by the claim that it can deal
with any ore—gold, copper, silver, or anything else. It
is simple enough. After the ore has been crushed fine
(30 or 40 mesh), the pulp, not too wet, is thoroughly
mixed with a thick, sticky mineral oil, the residue left in
the stills after the refining of paraffin. The oil lays hold
of metal particles, but passes all rock or earthy particles
hy on the other side. With its burden the oil presently
floats off, is caught, and in a centrifugal machine the two
are parted again. That is all. The claim is that oil
answers best where there is heavy float losses or where
the substances it is desired to separate are of almost the
Same specific gravity. It fails, however, to act on metallic
Oxides.
* * • *
Ivanhoe (W.A.) return for August: 10,767 tons,
12,1720 z. Total charges, including development, 41s. per
ton. Profit for month, about £20,000.
• * * •
In August 12 new cos., with a nominal capital
of £223,950, were registered at Charters Towers. Of this
capital, £71,750 is represented by paid-up shares, so
contrib.-holders will have the satisfaction of knowing
that, while they will have to bear all the expenses for
certain, they can only reap about two-thirds of the
problematical profits—and in some instances the profits
are very problematical indeed. During same period 33
mining leases, totalling 868 acres, were applied for ; so
doubtless September will see numerous “ cats ” foisted
on the market—that is, if the public is not already sated
with cats’-meat.
* * * *
The boom in Charters Towers stock is waning
sooner than expected, there having been an all-round
decline in values last week. The appreciation in the
value of shares compared with 12 months ago is fully
£1,500,000, and new flotations will probably represent
£500,000 more. As the Towers has to rely almost solely
on its own people for the maintenance of share-values, it
was quite unreasonable to suppose that high prices could
be long sustained. With so many mines in the prospect-
ing stage and so many new companies starting, it is neces-
sarily imperative that many holders of good stock must
realise, to get money with which to hold up the other
stocks. One thing is to be said in favor of the increase in
share values—in the majority of instances, mine pro-
spects have fully warranted it.
* * * *
Charters Towers divs. for August totalled
£26,716, and Bendigo had the fair record of £20,500.
Latter has made a good start for current month with
£10,574 for the first week.
» » « *
Bonnie Dundee (Charters Towers) shareholders
have had their patience sorely tried. The deep shaft was
to have bottomed some time ago. First delay caused by
rope breaking, the bucket falling on and killing a man.
At the inquiry it was stated that the engine was not
powerful enough to lift its burden steadily from the
bottom (2240 ft.) and the bucket had to be taken with a
jerk. Result, new rope and engine bought. New rope
condemned by mine inspectors. Then it was decided to
cross-cut for the Brilliant Reef. Latest development is
the striking of hot water in the face of the cross-cut. The
heat fnnst have been intense as the manager reports
that tha machines had to be left in the face.
The connection of the Bonnie Dundee deep shaft with the
Brilliant Extended (they bear the cost of sinking between
them) would mean an increase in the output of the latter
with the same amount of labor, as, owing to the heat, 10
minutes in and five out is recognised as a fair thing.
A shute of good stone has been struck in the Day Dawn
Consolidated (Charters Towers). Reported formation 6ft.
thick and going strong underfoot. If so, a record will
probably be put up in sinking: “ Plant’s ” Day Dawn deep
shaft.
* * * *
Negotiations in connection with the proposed
amalgamation of the Long Tunnel mines (Vic.) have been
cried off. The two co.’s practically got within easily
adjustable difference as to the proportion of shares to be
allotted to each in a new co., and then the question of
managerial control cropped up. One co. was unreason-
able enough to stipulate that its managers and directors
should have sole control of the amalgamated mines.
This was too selfish a suggestion for the other co. to
agree to, and now each will continue to run “ on its own,”
the result of which will be that tens of thousands of
pounds will be wantonly wasted. Long Tunnel 00. has
accepted a tender to sink the first 1000 ft. of the new
3000 ft. shaft. The letting of this contract displays lack
of up-to-date business methods in mines. Anywhere else
than in Victoria the 3000 ft. of sinking would have
been let in one contract, and the contractor would
have been bound under severe penalties to have the work
completed within record time. By dividing the work
into three contracts, as intended, the chances are that the
work will extend over twice the period that it should.
L.T. Extended last return was an excellent one—lBosoz.
from 889 tons, the stone being won from below 2000 ft.
• * * •
There has been a big slump in the value of
Hustlers (Bendigo) stocks, consequent on the falling off
in the return from No. 1 mine, the records for two weeks
being 194 tons for 6290 z. and 200 tons for 4460 z. The last
crushing was taken from all parts of the mine, but the
average of 2£oz. per ton, good as it is, is not considered
good enough to keep the stock up to 40s.
* * * •
The Cassilis Gold Co. (Vic.), the company with
the future, the company which is always bothered by bad
roads, troubled by feeble air-compressors, or dry dams,
or more machinery, or bank interest, or worried by
“ timber, fuel, tools, stores, and explosives,” and many
other of the ills that badly-managed mining companies
seem heir to, has produced a threnody in accountancy
quite beating a bike road-race in disaster. After spend-
ing £17,000 on machinery, £11,480 on the mine, and
£2578 on the Commercial Bank, the directors seem
“ getting no forrader.” The gold raised during the six
months ending 28th August was sold for r1C,616, and of
that £13,270 was spent on a list from “Office Expenses”
to “ Dapto Smelting Charges,” which are quite worthy of
a Walhalla mine and not nearly so easily justified. “It
was only out of consideration for the shareholders in
these bad times that your board decided to try and make
the mine find the money, and we have been so far
successful that we shall be able to accomplish our object.”
And no mention is made of the Commercial Bank and its
overdraft of £2578 17s.
* * * ->*
Lord Nelson (Vic.), has got over the £200,000
div. mark, and it is estimated that there is £lOO,OOO
actually in sight. Stock quoted at 605.-635., but these are
merely nominal figures. Shares are strongly held. Co.
would do well to acquire Nelson North leases, which it
now has a favorable opportunity to do on reasonable
terms.
• • • »
Jubilee, Scarsdale (Vic.), seems to be a mine of
solid merits. It has a shute of ore continuous for 800 ft.,
the lode averaging about 2ft. oin. Crushings since
inception of co. show lldwts. per ton. Prospectus
promise was B£dwts. Realisation in excess of antici-
pation is a refreshing novelty. There are two levels prac-
tically intact, and shaft is down ready for starting
another. Lode has shown improvement in value in each
successive sink. Shares at about 30s. look like a sound
investment—as sound a thing as one could expect in
mining, wherein there is no certainty as great as the un-
certainty.
* * * •
Bendigo has been famous as a goldfield for over
50 years, and still seems to be far from worked out. Its
mines have lately been returning more than half of the
total dividends paid by Victorian co.’s. The aggregate
average value of the ore from paying and non-paying
mines is about 325. per ton, and more than half of this
is returned as profit.
* • * •
London mining men are continually harping on
the alleged excessive rentals charged on mining leases in
Australia, but in their own country they submit to mon-
strous extortion and consider it moderation. The co.’s
working'gold-mines in Wales have to pay a royalty of 4
per cent, on the gross value of gold and silver recovered,
and this, we are told, is “ quite a nominal figure.” This is
irrespective of income-tax. The royalty is a “perk” of
the Prince of Wales. What a howl of indignation there
would be if the Australian legislatures insisted on having
a 2 per cent, royalty on the value oif metals and minerals ?
Ada Reeve had the splendid audacity to tell M.A.P.
she was the first girl to turn a catherine-wheel somer-
sault on the music-hall stage. Ada’s claim to celebrity on
that account called forth reminiscences of the time when
Fannie Leslie, who is old enough to be Ada’s mother,
used to go whirling round the stage on feet and fingers.
And wasn’t Lottie Moore an earlier cathetine-wheeliste
than Ada Reeve ?
The most charming spectacle at Sandow’s private
demonstration in Melb. was smooth Pastor Bevan
prodding the strong man gingerly in his anatomy to see
what it was like. Sandow, a bit of a humorist, imme-
diately singled out the cherubic one and appealed to him
to dig a little harder, as it didn’t hurt. Then he seized
the fat Bevan hand and rubbed it violently up and down
his three extraordinary abdominal muscles. Bevan must
have imagined he was having his knuckles peeled on a
scrubbing board. Sandow upsets many theories, principal
among which is the one that “ hardness” of muscle must
show itself even in repose. When Sandow stands at ease
he is as flabby as a sponge, and you can dig holes in him
with your fingers, but when he tautens up he is a
“ knubbly ” mass, like a heap of road metal for hardness.
He affirms that this is one of the conditions of a healthy
strength.
“ F.” : Miss Nance O’Neil, it is cabled, has had a “ fair
success” in London as Magoa—perhaps her best part,
with the exception of Hedda Gabler. The news con-
firms the critical opinion of the bountiful O’Neil formed
in Australia —a woman with great talent, perhaps with a
soupcon of genius, but “ run wild,” untrained, vociferous,
and with a tendency to throw a good part of a ton of
flesh about recklessly. If Nance O’Neil had been
caught young by a thoroughly skilful manager—one with
the head of Dot Boucicault and the body of Lawrence
Foley—who could, if necessity arose, drag her around the
stage by the hair and explain things at the same time,
she might have made a really great actress.
“ Thesp.” : Somebody wants to know where and when
Kate Bishop acted Juliet? She played the part to Frank
Oates’ Romeo in the old Opera House, Melb., over 15
years ago.
“ Jimmie P.” : Wallace King, the tuneful Tivoli tenor,
now in Maoriland, has been stricken with paralysis, and
lost both singing and speaking voice. Doctors say that
he will probably never sing again. P. R. Dix has opened
a subscription on behalf of the afflicted one.
“Haresfoot”: Richard Stewart, just deceased, was a
prime Sydney favorite 42 years ago. He and Ned Hol-
loway were the leaders in a famous theatrical strike.
This was partly the reason why Stewart turned his atten-
tion to Melbourne. There he first appeared under
Edwards and Faucett Rowe, at the Royal. They were
also the lessees of the Princess’s. Stewart was transferred
there, and appeared with Julia Mathews and Rowe in
“Aladdin,” and other successful burlesques. When
Wheatleigh brought over the “Colleen Bawn,” and
acted Danny Mann, Stewart took Myles na Coppaleen.
Jefferson was the next noteworthy star. He opened as
Rip Van Winkle, Stewart being Knickerbocker.
Next, Stewart supported Jefferson as Sir Lucius
O’Trigger in “The Rivals,” and Lord Dundreary
in “Our American Cousin.” Then the versatile
Richard changed to Uncle Pete, in “The Octoroon.”
Ooppin opened the Haymarket with “ Our American
Cousin,” Jefferson and Stewart appearing in it. Stewart
also did well as Fangarronades to Dillon’s Belphegor. He
brought forward Miss Theodosia Guerin, his step-
daughter, as Miss Docy Stewart, in Planche’s extrava-
ganza, “ The King of the Peacocks”; and also Miss
Maggie Guerin, known as Stewart. When Barry Sullivan
opened as Royal lessee, C. Young was low comedian
there. He refused to play Tony Lumpkin in “ She
Stoops to Conquer ” at brief notice, and left the theatre.
Stewart replaced him as Tony. Afterwards Young re-
turned, and both remained at the establishment. _ “ The
Hunchback ” was cast with remarkable strength, includ-
ing Sullivan, Heir, C. Vincent, Lambert, Young, Stewart,
Miss Cleveland, and Mrs. Heir. Stewart and Young
alternated Khorsanbad, in “ Lalla Rookh,” Christmas, 1863.
The Harlequinade was the best ever seen in Australia,
with the Edwins and the Leopolds. Stewart was at the
Royal under the management of Clarence Holt and Hos-
kins. He acted Gulliver, in the Christmas pantomime, in
1866. Next year came the management of Lambert, Vin-
cent, Harwood, Bellair, Stewart and Hennings, opening
with “ The Serf.” “ The Flying Scud ” was a great hit.
The management underwent various changes, Stewart
sticking in it until Coppin got the concern. After the
Are, and rebuilding, Coppin took in Hennings, Stewart,
and Greville as partners. Miss Nellie Stewart appeared
as a boy, in “ Saratoga,” and strengthened her position a
good deal as Orpheus in the burlesque of Pluto. The
whole Stewart family went on a tour of America, with
Garnet Walch’s “Rainbow Revels” and If. Thence-
forward there is not much to write about Dicks pro-
fessional career.
Tyrone Power, late of Australia, has been playing
Bassanio in Irving’s revivals of “ Merchant of Venice.

A prayer which, Dean Hole avers, a North
Country (Eng.) parson was in the habit of offering
up for Queen Adelaide
0 Lord, save thy servant, our Sovereign lady the
Queen ; grant that as she grows an old woman she may
become a new man ; strengthen her with Thy blessing
that she may live a pure virgin, bringing forth sons and
daughters to the glory of God ; and give her grace that
she may go forth before her people like a he-goat on the
mountains.
“ F.C." : I am sick of those letters in
the press from ex-cohentingenters declaiming
against the State Governments for their treatment
of the pseudo-patriots. As one who received a
chance to go (when acceptance meant popularity,
glory and a good billet) and refused because he
had adopted Australia as his country, and
recognised that his first duty was to her,
I would like to ask these mercenaries
what they expect. The best that can be
said of them is that they abandoned Australia —
their abandonment may not have meant much,
but that is a detail—for the sake of a 12 months’
billet at 355. a week and their tucker, and to help
England beat the Boers. Then why does their
wail pervade this country, like the rabbit and the
sparrow ? Let them look to England for their
reward. As for the Jingo promises of open
billets, State employment and so forth, does not a
nurse, to get the peace and quietness she wants,
promise a squalling child the moon ? And does
the infant get it ? Are these men babies, fools or
what? Let them take a lesson from those who
stopped at home and worked in and for Australia ;
and from those other workers in England who have
to foot the heavy bill, one item of which is for cohen-
tingenters who were paid double what they were
worth.

A FEW WORDS TO THE UNINITIATED.
PERSONAL AND PERTINENT.—Those who have Dot met Herr Rassmussen, and
who have not given his Alfaline Herbal Remedies a trial, may find it beneficial to become
acquainted with the good work which he has done in the interest of suffering humanity for
the last twenty years.
ALONG BACK IN THE BEGINNING OF THE EIGHTIES
Ilcrr Rassmussen introduced his now world-famed Alfaline Herbal Remedies to the Australian public at 547 George
Street, Sydney. Australia was then flooded with American importations and proprietary remedies, manufactured
by doubtful persons with assumed names and fictitious addresses, who took the people’s money without
responsibility, and very often without giving them fair value for same.
HERR RASSMUSSEN BROUGHT ABOUT A REVOLUTION
by straightforward and fair dealings with the public, offering to stake his reputation and future if his remedies
were not as efficacious as he claimed them to be, accepting all responsibility, and willing to abide by the people’s
verdict.
THE HAPPY RESULT IS KNOWN TO EVERYONE.
Over twenty years have passed away, hundreds of remedies have come and gone—many of them were forced on
the public in numerous designing ways—thousands of pounds spent on advertising same with the assistance of the

Supposing increments are stopped in
N.S.W. public service, where does the unfortu-
nate professional division come in ? The Public
Service Board managed, after deep thought, to
work out some kind of a regular scheme of incre-
ments for the lower divisions of the service, and,
in a precarious sort of way, they got their little
rise. But the professional division consisted of
men whose work required special knowledge, and it
took the Board so long to estimate the value of that
special knowledge that it never estimated it at all.
The professional division never heard anything
definite concerning its rise, because the Board was
still buried in thought about the matter and felt it
too important to be lightly dealt with. And mean-
while the salary of Smith, who, at the first grading
of the service, was put in the clerical division, has
overtaken and passed that of Jones, who was put
in the professional division at a higher wage
because of his extra ability, and the more respon-
sible character of his work. Now that all incre-
ments are to be stopped, and the salary of the
inferior Smith is to remain permanently ahead of,
or level with, that of the superior Jones, Jones
naturally calls the Public Service Board a wooden-
headed ass.

Business, Robbery, etc.
The Adelaide 'papers have, in the interests of
traders, re-started the practice of publishing the names
of persons brought up each week on Unsatisfied Judgment
Summonses. They fill several inches of space and are
monuments of harassed indigence. Strange to say it was
the magistrate who advised the resuscitation of the
practice, which had been discontinued for years.

“J.F.D.”: The’Murkan gentleman in charge
of the .canvassers for a local medical work, now in the
press, has written up a special pamphlet giving all the
necessary instructions how-to effect a sale—from the
moment you say “ Good morning ” to the affixing of the
subscriber’s signature in the order T book. He says that
lonjif experience has taught him when the psychological
moment arrives hy watching his man’s eye, when it is
only necessary to hand him the pen and the order-book.
Tried my hand, and thought I had struck it With a subur-
ban! resident, w,ho never spoke once while I dilated on the
surpassing merits of the work. At last I insinuatingly
handed him the pen, whereupon he coolly Wrote across
the order-book : “ I am deaf and dumb, but I suppose
you’re a book-canvasser, get to hell out of this ! ”

“Loco.” : Went into a tobacconist’s shop, in a
Vic. country town, t’other day. The shopkeeper asked
me iff I was a railway-man. (It is said that there is some-
thing peculiar about a railway-man, which enables anyone
to “ pick ” them as easily as townfolks “pick” a coun-
tryman.) “Yes,” I answered. “Then, of course, you
want a double in the two Cups ? ” He knew the railway-
man’s weakness. “ Ikey Mo” is not in favor of the
retrenchment of a class comprising some of his most con-
sistent customers.
* '• « *
“ Nymsie ” : Few of the thousands of Vic.
Friendly Societies’ members who trust themselves to the
tender mercies of the lodge doctor, and take £1 a week
when incapacitated by illness, know that Govt, actuary
Owen works while they are sleeping, on their behalf. At
the ' beginning L of'-the year he called for a tabulated list
of financial benefit members, in alphabetical order,
from each friendly and benefit society, and he is plod-
ding through these lists to look up discrepancies.
Saw a recent letter of his wherein he inquired if I was
not being overcharged in my contributions—a mistake
had been made in compiling the local lodge quarterly
returns from which above-mentioned official list was com-
piled, making me out five years younger than I am. And
another pointing out that a member who entered when
within a fortnight of being 17 years old should have been
charged as from last birthday—viz., 16 years of age—a
saving of 2d. a week. Little matters these, but of vital
import in the working of these benefit concerns. If there
was a constant State audit and. valuation of securities,
some wonderful muddles in book-keeping and trustee-
ship might be brought to light in the books of the various
brotherhoods.

In an appeal on an income-tax case in Vic.,
t’other day, it came out that a syndicate that put £36,000
into a Gippsland estate and cut it up into dairy-farms
netted a clear £28,300 on the deal in a little over two
months. Which is one reason why dairying doesn’t pay
quite so well as it did.

There was no mealy-mouthed hypocrisy about
an old sea captain who, in a recent Melb. salvage case
was asked, “Why were you feeling your way along the
coast so carefully ?” “ She was an uninsured vessel and I
was using extra care.” “Oh ! then you use more care
with an uninsured vessel than with an insured one ? ”
“Yes,”he frankly replied.

“ F.”: Was recently assured by a woman-
canvasser that she was making £5 Weekly with a “ riever-
known-to-fail ” preparation for ladies.
Related that the boss of a certain Melbourne
yard had the misfortune to lose his son, but was too ab-
sorbed in grief to think of closing the works on the day of
the funeral. Notwithstanding this the staff, mostly
boys, assembled respectfully and bareheaded at the gate
as the funeral passed. This display of sympathy evidently
touched the grief-stricken parent,' for he put his head out
of the mourning-coach window and roared : “ Now then,
wot yer loafin’ there for?”

A Dunedin (M.L.) pawnbroker exhibits, in
addition to the orthodox three-balls, a signboard
inscribed : ‘‘The Golden Fleece.”
• • • •
The Amalgamated Society of Engineers, which
has possibly the biggest influence of any trade-union in
the world, is being stirred by some of its members to an
interest in “ free patents.” The first proposal was that
the society should take up the approved patent ideas of
members, and, if necessary, apply them at the society’s
own works ; a later proposal is for free patents. The
argument is that many a valuable idea formed in the
workshop is simply allowed to slide by the helpless Work-
man who can’t afford to protect it, and that industrial
progress will be fastest (other things being equal) where
the inventor is dealt with the most liberally.
• • * •
* The contract-versus-day-labor controversy has
spread to Dunedin. The Council lately let a contract
for the building of a steam street-roller, and when the
machine name home it started to roll the muddiest street
in all that muddy city. It got bogged fast, and a fearful
battle raged in the Council as" to whether it was
cheaper to let a contract to recover the engine, or for the
Council to dig it out by day labor. A third party has
been formed to agitate that another roller be bought.
